Bouchard v. Insona


Civ. No. 44174


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


105 Cal. App. 3d 768; 164 Cal. Rptr. 505; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1824



May 15, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 379196, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the motion to vacate is reversed, and the case is remanded for hearing of the motion by another judge. 4



4 Counsel agree that appellant has reconveyed the real property to respondent and returned the furniture which was in her possession. Apparently, only the question of damages remains in issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contractor's wife sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which refused to vacate and set aside a default judgment entered against appellant and her husband.


OVERVIEW: Respondent property owner filed a complaint against a contractor alleging that he fraudulently induced respondent to convey title to her residential property to him. Default was entered against both contractor and appellant contractor's wife on October 8, 1977. Appellant and contractor requested that the default judgment be vacated on the grounds of mistake, inadvertence, or excusable neglect, claiming that they believed that they had additional time to answer. Appellant's counsel further filed a motion for peremptory disqualification of the judge under Cal. Code of Civ. Proc. §170.6. The motion was denied on the ground that it was untimely. The court found that the matter had been assigned to a department and not to a judge. The time for filing dictated by the statute referenced a particular judge. The court held that decisions involving advance assignments for trial did not apply if the assignment was merely to a department. The court held that since the motion was timely filed, the trial court was without jurisdiction to hear the motion, and the case was remanded for a hearing on the motion to vacate by another trial court.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order denying the motion to vacate the default judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for hearing of the motion by another judge because the peremptory disqualification of the trial court was timely, and therefore, the trial court was without jurisdiction to hear the motion.

State Bd. of Equalization v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 22182


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


105 Cal. App. 3d 813; 164 Cal. Rptr. 739; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1828



May 15, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 16, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 433845, Louis M. Welsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Board of Equalization challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) which denied its petition for writ of mandate and invalidated a rule which precluded reducing real property value under Proposition 13, Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 1 (amended 1974).


OVERVIEW: Appellant State Board of Equalization (board) adopted a rule providing that an amendment to Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 1 (amended 1974) (Proposition 8), which clarified an earlier amendment (Proposition 13) and permitted a decrease in actual fair market value in determining the value of real property for tax purposes, applied prospectively to the 1979-1980 taxable year. When respondent San Diego Board of Supervisors applied the "decline in value" standard to 1978-79 property assessments, appellant petitioned for writ of mandate. The trial court denied the petition and declared appellant's rule unconstitutional. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court had properly fulfilled its role in interpreting the administrative rule. It found that voter pamphlets, pertaining to Proposition 13, expressed no intention to relinquish the constitutional guaranty of taxation based on fair market value. Proposition 13 harmonized with Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 1 to maintain the constitutional fair market value limitation, which should be given effect. Proposition 8 required retroactive application as a clarification of Proposition 13.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying the writ of mandate and declaring invalid the rule of appellant State Board of Equalization which applied prospectively a constitutional amendment that allowed for a "decline in value" standard in determining the value of real property for tax purposes.

 

1638 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ramos


Crim. No. 3888


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


106 Cal. App. 3d 591; 165 Cal. Rptr. 179; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1902



May 14, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 16, 1980. Time for grt. or den. hg. extd. to August 12, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 18687, David L. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entered a conditional plea of guilty to the offense of robbery. As a condition of the plea agreement, the people agreed to dismiss the special allegation of use a deadly or dangerous weapon and agreed that sentencing would be at the trial court's discretion. Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) that sentenced defendant to the aggravated term of four years in state prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to robbery and was sentenced to the aggravated term of four years. The court rejected defendant's allegation that the trial court committed sentencing errors and affirmed. The court held that the trial court did not need to orally state the reasons for denying probation because it was implicitly explained by the choice of imprisonment. Although the trial court erred by failing to adequately state the reasons for imprisonment pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1170(c), the subsequent articulation of the aggravating factors cured any error. Despite defendant's allegations of error with respect to the imposition of the aggravated sentence, the court held that that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in imposing the aggravated term for robbery pursuant to Cal. R. Ct. 421(a). The court held that the trial court properly considered the threat of great bodily harm, defendant's use of a deadly weapon, the victim's particular vulnerability, and premeditation for purposes of aggravation. The court also held that, pursuant to Cal. R. Ct. 421(b)(2), the trial court could use defendant's prior adult convictions and juvenile adjudications for aggravation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in imposing an aggravated term for robbery. The court held that the trial court erred by failing to adequately state the reasons for imprisonment, but cured that error by its articulation of the aggravating factors. The court also held that the trial court properly considered the aggravating factors when it imposed sentence including the use a deadly weapon and defendant's prior convictions.

Deas v. Knapp


Civ. No. 43241


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


105 Cal. App. 3d 646; 164 Cal. Rptr. 641; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1814



May 13, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted June 12, 1980; (See 109 C.A.3d 171)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 415397, Lyle E. Cook, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, loan broker and California Real Estate Commissioner, challenged an adverse judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California). Appellant borrowers challenged the limited amount of that judgment in their action for usury and sought a larger award from appellant loan broker. Appellant commissioner contended that the superior court erred in awarding litigation costs to appellant borrowers in addition to their judgment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant borrowers applied for payment of the judgment obtained in their class action against appellant loan broker that alleged that he charged usurious amounts for financial services. The court affirmed the judgment subject to modification. The court held that appellant loan broker's challenge to the judgment's enforcement lacked merit because appellant borrowers had satisfied the requirements of the Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10471 to recover from the Real Estate Fund. The court determined that appellant loan broker brought an impermissible collateral attack on the judgment because he could not relitigate the issue of misrepresentation that occurred while he was licensed. The court held that appellant borrowers' challenge to the statutory limitation on their recovery lacked merit because Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10474(a), which required this cap on damages, applied based on their lawsuit's filing date. Plus, appellant borrowers could only recover for appellant loan broker's use of one license when only one license was required. The court ruled for appellant California Real Estate Commissioner by modifying the judgment to delete the award of costs to appellant borrowers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against appellant loan broker because his challenge to its enforcement lacked merit when appellant borrowers satisfied the legal requirements to recover from the Real Estate Fund. The court upheld the judgment's limited award to appellant borrowers in the usury action. The court modified the judgment for appellant California Real Estate Commissioner by deleting the award of litigation costs to appellant borrowers.

Myers v. Astoria Convalescent Hospital


Civ. Nos. 56699, 56866, 56937


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


105 Cal. App. 3d 682; 164 Cal. Rptr. 495; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1818



May 13, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 252271, C 246565, C 250837, Jess Whitehill, Temporary Judge, + Richard F. C. Hayden and Leon Savitch, Judges.


+ Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgments on the pleadings under appeal are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Director of the State Department of Health Services appealed the judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in favor of defendant hospitals, in the attorney general's action to enforce defendants' citations for violations of the Long-Term Care, Health, Safety, and Security Act of 1973 (Act), Health & Safety Code §1417 et seq., on the grounds that the Act's penalty setup was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The attorney general brought separate actions against defendant hospitals to enforce penalties for defendants' violations of statutory provisions regarding proper health care. The trial court found that Cal. Health & Safety Code §1428(b) deprived defendants of due process of law because it induced defendants and others not to contest the agency action in exchange for a minimum penalty. The court reversed the trial court judgments in favor of defendants. The court found no violation of constitutional due process in the penalty provisions of § 1428. The court held that § 1428 was a legislative offer to settle the citation at the earliest possible time in exchange for the least possible penalty. The court found § 1428 analogous to minimum fines imposed in traffic citations in return for the citee not contesting the citation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed judgments in favor of defendant hospitals in actions brought to enforce penalties for defendants' violation of statutes proscribing proper health care because the penalty provisions of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1428(b) did not deprive defendants of due process of law by inducing defendants not to contest the action in exchange for a minimum penalty.

People v. Superior Court of Santa Clara County

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution challenged a decision of respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which struck the special circumstances allegations from two informations which were to be used to subject the real party in interest, a criminal defendant, to the death penalty or life imprisonment without parole.

1642 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harris


Crim. No. 19363


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


105 Cal. App. 3d 204; 164 Cal. Rptr. 296; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1768



April 29, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 25, 1980. Bird, C. J., Mosk, J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 22089, Robert J. Cooney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to determine any additional sentencing credit to which appellant is entitled consistent with the views expressed in this opinion, to resentence him as necessary, and to modify the abstract of judgment accordingly. The court is further directed to transmit a certified copy of any modified abstract of judgment to the Department of Corrections and other appropriate authorities.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contested a decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which convicted him of second degree burglary and grand theft, with a finding of a prior felony conviction. Appellant claimed the court erred when it denied his motion to suppress, that evidence of prior arrest was improperly admitted, that a jury instruction was erroneous, and that he was entitled to sentencing credits under Cal. Penal Code §§ 2900.5, 4019.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his conviction for second degree burglary and grand theft, with a finding of a prior felony conviction. The court affirmed the conviction but remanded for a determination of the sentencing credit appellant was entitled to under Cal. Penal Code §§ 2900.5, 4019. The court held that the denial of appellant's motion to suppress was proper because there was probable cause to arrest another suspect, and the search of the suspect's purse incident to her arrest fell squarely within the search warrant exception for a search incident to arrest. Therefore the evidence was legally obtained and was admissible. Probable cause was based on the suspect's suspicious movements in the middle of the night, her active assistance in appellant's escape efforts, and her untruthful answers to the officer. Evidence of the prior convection was admissible because the Beagle factors were fully satisfied. Appellant's argument that Cal. Jury Instruction - Criminal No. 2.62 was unconstitutional failed because that jury instruction did not violate the Griffin rule or improperly single out appellant's testimony for special consideration by the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction but remanded for a determination of the sentencing credit. The motion to suppress was properly denied because there was probable cause for a suspect's arrest, and the warrantless search of her purse incident to her arrest was proper. Evidence of appellant's prior conviction was admissible, and the challenged jury instruction was proper.

Stackler v. Department of Motor Vehicles


Civ. No. 18306


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


105 Cal. App. 3d 240; 164 Cal. Rptr. 203; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1770



April 29, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 25, 1980. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 267602, William K. Morgan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensee sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that sustained the demurrer of respondent department of motor vehicles to appellant's complaint seeking to renew his driver's license without a full face photograph under Cal. Veh. Code § 12811(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant licensee brought an action to compel respondent department of motor vehicles to renew his driver's license without the requirement of a photograph, claiming respondent had to authority to demand the same on license renewals and that the requirement violated his right of privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1. The trial court sustained respondent's demurrer and dismissed the action and appellant sought review. The court affirmed, finding that Cal. Veh. Code § 12811(a) gave respondent the authority to require a photograph on a license renewal. The court determined that that the words "issue" and "issued" encompassed both original delivery of a license and any redelivery thereof by renewal. The court concluded that if the foregoing were not so, certain statutory provisions relating to renewal would have no meaning because they incorporated the provisions pertaining to original issuance of a license. The court found that the photograph requirement did not violate appellant's right of privacy because the only requirements for production of the license pertained to situations where he would be present and could be observed by the person requesting the license.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that sustained the demurrer of respondent department of motor vehicles to the complaint of appellant licensee that sought to compel respondent to renew his driver's license without requiring him to submit to a full face photograph, finding that respondent had statutory authority to require the photograph on a license renewal and that no invasion of appellant's privacy was caused thereby.

 

1644 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ballard


Crim. No. 9781


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


104 Cal. App. 3d 757; 164 Cal. Rptr. 81; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1723



April 16, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 18, 1980. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. CR-33773, Rex W. Cranmer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which found defendant guilty of two misdemeanor counts of commingling campaign contributions, under the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code § 81000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of two misdemeanor counts of commingling campaign contributions, in violation of the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code § 81000 et seq. Defendant contended that testimony by the deputy district attorney constituted prejudicial misconduct because he was the chief legal advisor to the grand jury. As the court found that the attorney was a witness and not the "advocate," no misconduct existed. Defendant argued that the attorney's testimony was a breach of Cal. Rules of Prof. Conduct Rule 2-111(A)(4) because a member of his "law firm," the district attorney's office, represented the state. The court affirmed, as defendant's failure to object to the deputy district attorney's testimony or to attempt to recuse the district attorney's office waived his contention of "misconduct" because it could have been "cured." The court concluded that Cal. Gov't Code § 91002's prohibition against being a candidate for any elective office for a period of four years following defendant's conviction date was not unconstitutional because it gave candidates fair notice of crimes with which they might be charged.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that found defendant guilty of two misdemeanor counts of commingling campaign contributions was affirmed, as the Political Reform Act of 1974, which prohibited defendant from running for elective office for four years after conviction, was constitutional, and any misconduct in deputy district attorney's testimony at trial was waived because defendant failed to object.

 

1645 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Demery


Crim. No. 34299


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


104 Cal. App. 3d 548; 163 Cal. Rptr. 814; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1704



April 14, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 11, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A320604, A328424, Warren D. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a doctor, sought review of his convictions, following a jury trial in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), on 35 counts of prescribing a controlled substance not in his regular practice, all in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11154. Defendant's motions to set aside the information under Cal. Penal Code § 995 and for a judgment of acquittal under Cal. Penal Code § 1118.1 were denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a doctor, argued that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11154 was too vague and indefinite to provide a standard by which a trier of fact could determine a defendant's guilt and, therefore, was unconstitutional as a denial of due process of law. Defendant also argued that the trial court erred in refusing to give an instruction offered by him which set forth in detail the factors that could be considered by the jury in determining whether violations of § 11154 had occurred. In addition, defendant argued that he was deprived of his constitutional right to the effective assistance of counsel at trial because of his counsel's failure to argue that his prosecution was barred pursuant to the doctrine of collateral estoppel. On appeal, the court found no merit in any of defendant's contentions. The court held that § 11154 was immune from constitutional attack. The court also held that defendant's requested instruction was not required, and that since it was clear that no potentially meritorious defense was withdrawn as the result of the claimed omission, defendant's contention of inadequate representation failed. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which found defendant, a doctor, guilty of 35 counts of prescribing a controlled substance not in his regular practice. The court held that the statute under which defendant was convicted was immune from constitutional attack, that defendant's requested instruction was not required, and that defendant's contention of inadequate representation failed.

 

1646 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dickinson


Crim. No. 10490


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


104 Cal. App. 3d 505; 163 Cal. Rptr. 575; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1699



April 11, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. VCR 517, Joseph A. Katz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), denying his suppression motion. Following the denial of the motion, defendant pleaded guilty to possession of marijuana for sale. The appeal challenged the stop and search of defendant's vehicle at a California plant quarantine inspection station.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of a decision by the trial court denying his motion to suppress evidence. Defendant challenged the stop and search of defendant's vehicle at a California plant quarantine inspection station. On appeal, the court rejected defendant's challenge. The court held that there was no U.S. Const. amend. IV violation under the facts of this case. The court held that quarantine officers could stop motorists at the inspection stations and request to look into the trunk of the vehicle. The court also held that if the motorist voluntarily opened the trunk of the vehicle, the quarantine officer could look therein and, as here, remove any plant materials in plain view for further inspection. The court also rejected defendant's suggestion that a sign should have been placed at inspection stations or that quarantine officers should have advised motorists that they may refuse to give their consent to any search. The court held that the lack of a warning sign or advice by the quarantine officer did not render the circumstances of the case violative of the Fourth Amendment or Cal. Const. art. I, § 13. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence. The court held that quarantine officers could stop motorists at the inspection stations and request to look into the trunk of the vehicle. The court also held that if the motorist voluntarily opened the trunk of the vehicle, the quarantine officer could look therein and remove any plant materials in plain view for further inspection.

 

1647 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Jennings v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 46634


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


104 Cal. App. 3d 50; 163 Cal. Rptr. 391; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1650



April 1, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 1, 1980. Scott, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. The petition of real party in interest for a rehearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 4, 1980. Clark, J., Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Having reached this conclusion, we must issue the peremptory writ. Let peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the San Francisco Superior Court to vacate its order denying petitioner's motion to suppress and to enter an order suppressing the evidence derived from the unlawful arrest.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for a writ of mandate seeking a reexamination of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied defendant's motion to suppress a bag containing heroin found under the back seat of a police car used to transport her to jail. Defendant contended that her arrest and seizure for violating San Francisco, Cal., Code § 20A (1977), an obstruction ordinance, was unlawful.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for obstructing a sidewalk in violation of San Francisco, Cal., Code § 20A (1977). The authority for adopting the ordinance came from Cal. Penal Code § 647c. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress a bag containing heroin found in the police car used to transport her to jail. On appeal, the court directed the trial court to vacate its order denying the motion, finding that the ordinance was critically different from Cal. Penal Code § 647c because it only required that the obstructing be done "willfully," rather than "willfully and maliciously," as provided in § 647c. The court noted that by requiring only willfulness, the ordinance swept in all variety of harmless activities. The court found that the ordinance was to be read as requiring maliciousness and when read that way, the ordinance did not justify defendant's arrest because there was no evidence to support a strong suspicion that she acted with malice in obstructing the sidewalk. Finally, the court found that the integrity of the judiciary precluded it from permitting introduction of evidence which, but for the existence of a defective ordinance could not have been properly seized.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ directing the trial court to vacate its order denying defendant's motion to suppress. The court found that the obstruction ordinance was to be read to require maliciousness and that when that requirement was added, defendant's arrest and seizure was unlawful because there was no evidence to support a strong suspicion that she acted with malice in obstructing the sidewalk.

 

1648 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Fenske v. Board of Administration


Civ. No. 4804


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


103 Cal. App. 3d 590; 163 Cal. Rptr. 182; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1605; 22 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P30,884



March 20, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Administration of the Public Employees' Retirement System challenged the judgment of the superior court (California) that issued a writ of mandate that commanded appellant to redetermine whether respondent female employee should be classified as a safety employee for purposes of calculating her pension, and that declared Cal. Gov't. Code § 20020 unconstitutional as it applied to respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent female employee applied for her pension and contended her job duties entitled her to benefits at the higher "safety" employee rate rather than the lower "general" employee rate. Appellant Board of Administration of the Public Employees' Retirement System denied respondent's request. Respondent filed a petition for writ of mandate in the trial court and the trial court ordered appellant to redetermine respondent's classification. The trial court declared that the statute appellant relied on, Cal. Gov't Code § 20020, unconstitutional as it discriminated against respondent solely because of her sex. The appellate court affirmed. The court ruled that respondent's claim was not time barred because the statute of limitations did not begin to run until appellant made its determination in the case. The court ruled that Cal. Const. art. III, § 3.5 limited appellant's authority to determine a statute's constitutionality, and rejected appellant's argument that it divested the trial court of such authority. Rather than invalidate the statute in its entirety the court construed it to apply also to females.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court. The court found that a recent change to the California Constitution did not divest the lower court of jurisdiction to review the issue of a statute's constitutionality. The court ruled that the statute at issue was unconstitutional insofar as it purported to apply only to males.

Martin v. Heady


Civ. No. 54147


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


103 Cal. App. 3d 580; 163 Cal. Rptr. 117; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1604



March 20, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 21, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 87579, Charles H. Older, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment under appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in favor of defendant lienors. Plaintiff had filed an action against defendants for conversion of a customized DC-3 airplane, defendants had sold the plane at a lien sale pursuant to the aircraft lien law, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1208.61 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff failed to pay defendant lienors' bill in connection with repairs to an airplane he owned. Defendant sent notice to plaintiff of defendants' intention to sell the plane at public auction. The plane was purchased by one of the defendants for the exact amount of the repair bill plus claimed storage charges. Plaintiff did not exercise his right of redemption. Plaintiff brought an action against defendants for conversion. He claimed that the lien sale was unlawful. The trial court's judgment was in favor of defendants. The appellate court reversed. The court ruled that the aircraft lien law, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1208.61 et seq., violated the procedural due process provision of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a) because the law permitted an involuntary sale and a transfer of property without affording the owner an opportunity for hearing.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the judgment for defendant lienors, who had sold plaintiff's airplane at a lien sale. The court ruled that the statute that provided the basis for the sale violated California's procedural due process guarantee because it permitted an involuntary sale and a transfer of property without affording the owner an opportunity for a hearing.

 

1650 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hill


Crim. No. 34488


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


103 Cal. App. 3d 525; 163 Cal. Rptr. 99; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1599



March 19, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 14, 1980. Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 077132, Charles A. Loring, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of pimping and pandering in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 266h and 266i, respectively. Defendant argued that the instructions given by the court were inadequate to give the jury an understanding of his theory of defense, and that the court had a duty, sua sponte, to give adequate instructions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of pimping and pandering in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 266h and 266i, respectively. On appeal, he argued that the trial court's instructions to the jury did not adequately advise the jury of the law and his theory of defense. The court found that defendant's theory of the case -- that the alleged prostitute was merely engaged in nude modeling -- required the trial court, sua sponte, to instruct the jury that nude modeling did not constitute an act of prostitution and that an act of procuring a person solely for the purpose of nude modeling did not violate either the pimping or pandering statute. In the absence of such instructions, the jury might well have considered defendant's evidence -- even though different from the prosecution's evidence -- as itself amounting to the commission of the crimes charged. Therefore, the court reversed the conviction because the trial court failed to give instructions necessary for the jury's understanding of the case.


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court had a duty, sua sponte, to give instructions necessary to the jury's understanding of the case and that it failed to do so. The court further held that it was reasonably probable that a result more favorable to defendant would have been reached had not such errors been made. Therefore, the judgment convicting the defendant of pimping and pandering was reversed.

People v. Barlow


Crim. No. 10568


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


103 Cal. App. 3d 351; 163 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1581



March 14, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. C36269, Richard J. Beacom, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal is dismissed because it is both untimely and not in compliance with Penal Code section 1237.5. What we have deemed a petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County, finding defendant had violated terms of probation, reinstating probation, and requiring defendant to serve an additional 60 days.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, without assistance of counsel, entered a plea of guilty to reckless driving with injury pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 23104, and following 30 days of incarceration defendant was placed on probation. Defendant subsequently breached the terms of his probation when he was arrested and charged with violation of Cal Veh. Code § 23102(a), misdemeanor drunk driving. Defendant was found to have violated the terms of probation and probation was reinstated with conditions. Defendant appealed and contended that the court erred in failing to adequately war defendant of the risk and pitfalls involved in self-representation. The appellate court found substantial evidence to support the fact that defendant's waiver of his right to counsel was made knowingly and intelligently. Defendant's appeal was dismissed because it was untimely and not in compliance with Cal. Penal Code § 1237.5. The court deemed defendant's action in regard to his plea as a petition for writ of habeas corpus. The petition was denied based upon the substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: Defendant's appeal was dismissed because it was both untimely and not in compliance with state penal code; what the court deemed a petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied, because the court found substantial evidence that defendant knowingly and intelligently entered a plea of guilty.

 

1652 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Alameda Tank Co. v. Starkist Foods, Inc.


Civ. No. 56018


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


103 Cal. App. 3d 428; 162 Cal. Rptr. 924; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1588



March 13, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 1, 1980, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 14, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. SO-C 40851, 400851, Carroll M. Dunnum and Robert A. Wenke, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of dismissal) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant equipment manufacturer sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that dismissed appellant's cross complaint against respondent employer after respondent sought indemnification for damages to an injured employee, on grounds that Cal. Labor Code § 3864 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Employee and his wife sued appellant equipment manufacturer alleging that while working for respondent employer, he was seriously injured by equipment manufactured by appellant for respondent. Appellant denied liability and filed a cross-complaint against respondent seeking indemnification. Respondent's demurrer was sustained and an order of dismissal followed. The arguments centered on the demurrer to the cause of action for implied indemnity. Respondent argued that the theory was barred by Cal. Lab. Code § 3864. Appellant contended that § 3864 was unconstitutional as abridging a substantive right, violating due process, equal protection, and the privilege and immunities clause of the state constitution. The court affirmed, finding that there was a public purpose sufficient to sustain § 3864, which allowed the legislature to create, enlarge, diminish, or abolish substantive rights. The court determined that the privileges and immunity clause was not applicable to corporations and held that the legislature could differentiate between various classes of citizens as long as the classification was founded on a reasonable basis.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent employer, finding that there was a public purpose served by the state statute, which exempted respondent from liability for indemnification to appellant equipment manufacturer. The court ruled that respondent had a statutory right to be free from liability beyond that imposed by worker compensation laws in a personal injury claim brought by an injured employee.

 

1653 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Haynes


Crim. No. 11108


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


104 Cal. App. 3d 118; 164 Cal. Rptr. 552; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1659



March 12, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 28, 1980, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 8, 1980. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should he granted. As Modified April 4, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 43120, Earl H. Maas, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed without prejudice to Haynes to petition for habeas corpus as discussed in our opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) for receiving stolen property, Cal. Penal Code § 496(1), contending that he was denied effective assistance of counsel and that the trial court erred by failing to instruct the jury to view defendant's admission with caution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of receiving stolen property under Cal. Penal Code § 496(1). Defendant admitted at trial that he bought the car from a black man, in contradiction to an officer's testimony that defendant told him that he bought the car from a Mexican. Defendant sought review, arguing that counsel was ineffective for failing to object to hearsay testimony and for failing to file a pretrial motion to suppress evidence, and that it was error for the trial court to fail to instruct the jury sua sponte that it should view his admission with caution. The court affirmed, holding that the record shed no light on counsel's reasons for the challenged conduct, and that the proper procedure for defendant was to file a petition for habeas corpus, which would allow an evidentiary hearing that would more fully explore the issues and allow counsel an opportunity to be heard. The court found that it was not probable that the trial would have had a different result if the trial court had sua sponte instructed the jury as to defendant's admission, because there remained ample evidence from which the jury could have inferred that defendant had knowingly received stolen property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant 's conviction for receiving stolen property. The court held that the trial court's failure to sua sponte instruct the jury to view defendant's admission with caution did not effect the outcome of the trial, and that an evidentiary hearing via a writ of habeas corpus was the proper procedure for defendant to bring his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.

Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three

DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is granted. CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney general sought review of the decision of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction to the law firm, the real party in interest, that restrained petitioner from conducting any search of the offices, files, and records of the law firm.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney general obtained a valid search warrant to search a law firm, the real party in interest. The law firm applied for and obtained from respondent superior court a temporary restraining order that restrained petitioner from executing the search warrant. A preliminary injunction was granted by respondent later and petitioner sought a peremptory writ of mandate. The court granted the peremptory writ of mandate and ordered respondent to set aside its order that enjoined respondent from executing the search warrant. The court held that search warrants directed against attorneys' files in their offices were not per se unreasonable as violative of U. S. Const. amend. IV. The court determined that § 1524 required a hearing, and therefore if petitioner still had an interest in pursuing the investigation he must comply with the statute.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner attorney general's application and issued a peremptory writ of mandate that commanded respondent superior court to set aside and vacate its order enjoining petitioner from executing a search warrant for the search of the files and records of the law firm, the real party in interest.

Graham v. Scissor-Tail, Inc.


Civ. Nos. 53891, 55436


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


103 Cal. App. 3d 115; 162 Cal. Rptr. 798; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1561



March 5, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 14, 1980 (See 28 C.3d 807)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 70116, Alfred L. Margolis and Vernon G. Foster, Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff promoter appealed from a judgment entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which affirmed an arbitration award in favor of defendants, musician and his wholly-owned corporation, following a contract dispute.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff promoter entered into a contract with defendants, musician and his wholly-owned corporation, by which plaintiff agreed to promote two concerts to be performed by defendant musician. The first concert lost money. The second turned a profit. The parties could not agree on who would bear the losses from the first concert. Plaintiff filed suit to settle the dispute. Defendants filed a cross-claim to compel arbitration, relying on a provision in the contract requiring arbitration before the union. The trial court ordered the parties to arbitrate the dispute. The arbitrator ruled in defendants' favor and ordered plaintiff to pay damages. The trial court affirmed the arbitration award. On appeal the court reversed, concluding that plaintiff was denied due process of the law in the arbitration proceeding and that the award was therefore unenforceable because the union was not a neutral arbitrator and the procedures were otherwise wholly in defendants' favor. The court further found that plaintiff did not waive his right to an impartial arbitrator by signing the contract because he was acting under economic duress when he signed it.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment affirming the arbitration award in favor of defendants, musician and his wholly-owned corporation, because plaintiff promoter was denied due process of the law in the arbitration proceeding and he did not waive his right to a neutral arbitrator. The court remanded the matter with directions for the trial court to appoint a neutral arbitrator.

 

1656 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hinojosa


Crim. No. 3565


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


103 Cal. App. 3d 57; 162 Cal. Rptr. 793; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1556



March 4, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 30, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 220057-4, Hollis G. Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the sentence imposed by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which sentenced defendant to state prison based on defendant's conviction for selling heroin.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by the trial court of selling heroin. Defense counsel urged the trial judge to impose a lower term sentence under a determinate sentencing law, but the trial court refused and instead denied probation and imposed an indeterminate sentence of state prison. Defendant appealed that ruling and claimed that the refusal to provide a determinate sentence was prejudicial as his sentence might have been reduced by one year and that the indeterminate sentence amounted to an ex post facto application of the law. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court sentence. The court held that the sentencing statute at issue, Cal. Penal Code § 1170, was assigned a specific enactment date by the legislature to avoid drastic consequences under the new law. Although defendant was not sentenced until after the effective date, his crime took place prior to the effective date and, therefore, the statute was properly not applied to defendant. This denied defendant no vested rights and was therefore not an ex post facto application.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court ruling, which sentenced defendant to state prison based on defendant's conviction for selling heroin. The court held that an amended sentencing statute was properly not applied where it became effective after defendant's crime occurred but before defendant was sentenced.

OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of involuntary manslaughter, adjudged a mentally disordered sex offender, and committed to a state institution. At the end of his commitment, the state petitioned pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6316.2 to extend the commitment because defendant remained mentally disordered and dangerous. At the institution, defendant was provided, and at times accepted, treatment for his mental disorder. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the petition, arguing that he was not amenable to treatment. Loza v. Panish


Civ. No. 56355


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


102 Cal. App. 3d 821; 162 Cal. Rptr. 596; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1532



February 27, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 26, 1980. Beach, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 10015 to compel respondent registrar to reject any candidate's statement submitted under Cal. Elec. Code § 10012 that contained any reference to anything other than the candidate's own education and qualifications.


OVERVIEW: Appellant petitioned for a writ of mandate pursuant to Cal Elec. Code § 10015 to compel respondent registrar to reject any candidate's statement, submitted under Cal. Elec. Code § 10012, containing any reference to anything other than the candidate's own education and qualifications. Appellant particularly opposed any candidate's statement containing any reference to any opposing candidate. The petition was denied and appellant sought review. Dinong v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 18732

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 24, 1980.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent superior court (California), ordered petitioner insureds to proceed to arbitration in their claim alleging careless treatment during petitioner wife's pregnancy, pursuant to an arbitration clause in their group health plan. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate ordering respondent to allow them to proceed to jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insureds' group health plan between their employer and the insurer provided for arbitration. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to allow them to proceed to trial. OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner insureds' request for writ of mandate to order respondent superior court to allow them to proceed to jury trial in their claim against the insurer of their group health plan. Libertarian Party of California v. March Fong Eu


Civ. No. 21101


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


102 Cal. App. 3d 446; 162 Cal. Rptr. 381; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1500



February 21, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 28, 1980 (See 28 C.3d 535)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 297384, Robert H. Green, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the California secretary of state and the registrars of voters in two counties, challenged the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which entered a judgment granting a peremptory writ of mandate commanding appellants to place on the ballot the names of respondent political candidates of a non-qualified party with their political party designation rather than "Independent" as required by Cal. Elec. Code § 10210.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, the California secretary of state and the registrars of voters in two counties, challenged the trial court's grant of a writ of mandate to respondent political candidates, which ordered appellants to place respondents' names on the ballot with the political party designation "Libertarian." Appellant secretary of state refused to certify respondents' designations on the ballot as "Libertarian," rather than "Independent," because respondents were candidates of a nonqualified political party. Cal. Gov't Code § 10210. Respondents argued that the section 10210 requirement that candidates who qualify by petition must be labeled "Independent" resulted in the denial of equal protection to candidates of nonqualified political parties. The court held that the provisions of section 10210 could be upheld only if appellant could demonstrate that the classifications drawn were necessary to achieve a compelling governmental interest, and this appellants could not do. The court held that by denying respondents the right to party designation enjoyed by candidates of the major political parties, the law impermissibly discouraged the growth of new political parties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because the election law provisions that authorized the ballot designation of a candidate's party affiliation with a qualified political party, and prohibited ballot designation of a candidate's party affiliation when the candidates qualified for the ballot by petition, were constitutionally impermissible as violative of the equal protection of the law.

 

1662 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Coldwell Banker & Co. v. Department of Insurance


Civ. No. 55067


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


102 Cal. App. 3d 381; 162 Cal. Rptr. 487; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1496



February 21, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 13, 1980, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 14, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 159518, Campbell M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), granting a writ of mandate in respondents' favor with respect to the issuance of an organizational permit and license to do business.


OVERVIEW: Respondents sought a writ of mandate and declaratory relief to compel petitioners to issue to respondent title company an organizational permit and a license to do business. The lower court granted the writ, and petitioners sought review of that decision. The court reversed. The appropriate standard of judicial review by the trial court was the substantial-evidence test and not the independent-judgment test. Because the trial court employed the wrong standard of judicial review, its findings of fact were irrelevant to a consideration of the issue of whether the administrative decision in question was supported by substantial evidence on the whole record before the administrative agency. The substantial-evidence standard required the trial court to look to the entire administrative record not only to determine whether the findings were supported by substantial evidence, but also to determine whether the agency committed any errors of law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The trial court erred when it applied the wrong standard of review; thus, its findings of fact were irrelevant to a consideration of whether the administrative decision was supported by substantial evidence.

 

1663 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sapper


Crim. No. 19438


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


102 Cal. App. 3d 301; 162 Cal. Rptr. 360; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1487



February 20, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 17, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-8288, Allan J. Bollhoffer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant drug dealer challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which convicted him of possession of marijuana for sale pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359 on the ground that the search of his package was a violation of Cal. Const. art. 1, § 3 and U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OVERVIEW: Appellant drug dealer handed a package to airlines baggage representative and stated that he wanted the package shipped out on the next flight to Philadelphia. The baggage representative thought the package looked suspicious, so he ran it through the x-ray machine. The x-ray confirmed that the package contained contraband. The baggage representative then opened the package and found marijuana inside. Two airport officers located appellant and arrested him. Appellant was later convicted of possession of marijuana for sale pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359. The trial court held that the search of appellant's package was a private search and not subject to the search and seizure clauses of Cal. Const., art. I § or U.S. Const. amend IV. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision, holding that the search was a private search and not subject to the state or federal search and seizure clauses. The court also held that the search of appellant's package did not violate his right to privacy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision to convict appellant drug dealer of felony possession of marijuana for sale, holding that the search of appellant's package did not fall within the state or federal search and seizure clauses, because it was a private search.

In re Rogers


Crim. No. 10365


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


102 Cal. App. 3d 61; 162 Cal. Rptr. 230; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1465



Feb. 11, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Feb. 21, 1980.
Hg. granted Apr. 10, 1980 (See 28 C.3d 429)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 54979, John J. Boskovich, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Community Release Board challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted petitioner's writ of habeas corpus and held that appellant improperly considered petitioner's two prior convictions during a serious offender hearing held pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1170.2(b).


OVERVIEW: In 1976, petitioner was sentenced to prison for three counts of kidnapping, one count of attempted rape, two counts of rape, and one count of oral copulation. The information also alleged that petitioner was convicted of two counts of rape in 1960 and two counts of lewd and lascivious conduct in 1971. In the 1976 proceeding, the trial court struck the two 1971 convictions as constitutionally invalid. Appellant Community Release Board held a serious offender hearing pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1170.2(b) and added three years to petitioner's term based on the 1971 convictions. Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus on grounds that the 1971 convictions were improperly considered. The trial court granted the writ. Appellant sought review. The court reversed the trial court because the striking of the 1971 convictions as constitutionally invalid was not the equivalent of a determination that petitioner did not suffer the convictions. Appellant was, therefore, permitted to enhance petitioner's term by use of the 1971 convictions. The prior convictions were relevant to a determination of the term necessary to protect the public from the repetition of violent crimes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court that granted petitioner's writ of habeas corpus and held that appellant Community Release Board improperly considered petitioner's two prior convictions during a serious offender hearing. The court held that the striking of petitioner's prior convictions as constitutionally invalid was not the equivalent of a determination that petitioner did not suffer the convictions.

Toso v. City of Santa Barbara


Civ. Nos. 49261, 49990


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


101 Cal. App. 3d 934; 162 Cal. Rptr. 210; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1451



February 6, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 26, 1980, and the petition of appellant Toso for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 14, 1980. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 106854, Robert G. Eckhoff, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed on the first two causes of action; it is reversed on the third, fourth and fifth causes of action. Neither party shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff zoning applicant and defendant city challenged orders by the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which denied plaintiff inverse condemnation damages and ordered defendant to rezone the property that plaintiff sought to develop to allow for construction of a resort hotel.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff zoning applicant appealed the trial court's denial of inverse condemnation damages in an action concerning a parcel of real property that plaintiff intended to develop as a resort hotel. Defendant city appealed the trial court's order instructing defendant to rezone the property to permit construction of the hotel. Because defendant denied plaintiff's original request to rezone the property, plaintiff had allowed his option to purchase the property to expire. The court stated that the trial court erroneously substituted its judgment for that of defendant by ordering the rezoning of the property. The court found that plaintiff did not have a cause of action for inverse condemnation because defendant had not made either a formal resolution of condemnation or taken any other official action towards acquisition of the property. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment denying plaintiff's request for inverse condemnation damages and reversed that portion of the judgment that required defendant to rezone the property to allow for construction of a hotel.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying plaintiff zoning applicant inverse condemnation damages was affirmed because defendant city had not taken any official steps towards purchasing the property that plaintiff sought to develop. The order requiring defendant to rezone the property to allow for construction of a resort hotel was reversed because the trial court had impermissibly substituted its judgment for that of defendant.

 

1666 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


HOFFMANN-LaROCHE, INC. v. FRANCHISE TAX BD.


Civ. No. 53838


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


101 Cal. App. 3d 691; 161 Cal. Rptr. 838; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1432



January 31, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 10, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 170998, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted judgment on the pleadings to defendant, California Franchise Tax Board, and held that the franchise tax computation found in Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 23135 was constitutionally valid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer filed a complaint against defendant, California Franchise Tax Board, for recovery of franchise taxes paid. The trial court granted judgment on the pleadings in favor of defendant, and plaintiff appealed. The court affirmed the judgment, holding that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 25135(b)(2) was not unconstitutional but called for a permissible allocation of plaintiff's net income that had resulted from interstate commerce. The court held that the net income of plaintiff was not "generated" at the place where merchandise was delivered but that the sales of merchandise that originated in California reflected business activity in California. The court found that the classification made in 15 U.S.C.S. § 381 was reasonable for determining whether sales activity should have been attributed to the receiving state or the state of origin. The court found that plaintiff had not cited any case that held unconstitutional the Uniform Division of Income for Tax Purposes Act, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 25120 et seq. (1966), formula of allocating interstate sales. Nor was there any showing that such formula created a "palpably disproportionate result."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendant, California Franchise Tax Board, because it found that sales of merchandise originating in California and shipped to other states reflected business activity in California and were subject to California's franchise taxes. The court held that the method of determining such franchise taxes was not shown to be unconstitutional nor palpably disproportionate.

 

1667 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williams


Crim. No. 3578


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


101 Cal. App. 3d 711; 161 Cal. Rptr. 830; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1434



January 31, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Modified of Opinion February 28, 1980. On February 29, 1980, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 10, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 18894, Gerald K. Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which convicted him of conspiring to commit first degree murder, under Cal. Penal Code §§ 182, 187, 189.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of conspiring to commit first degree murder. On appeal, the court affirmed appellant's conviction, holding that the corroborating evidence to the accomplice testimony was superabundant and included appellant's own testimony that he wanted to kill the victim upon learning that the victim had molested appellant's daughter. The court held that the trial court was not required to instruct the jury in the precise language requested by appellant, especially because appellant did not maintain the that the jury instruction was in error, only that his instruction was better. The court held that life imprisonment was not cruel and unusual punishment for the crime of conspiring to commit first degree murder. The court found that group criminal conduct called for enhanced punishment, and that the only offense more serious was first degree murder. The court held that given the nature of the offense and the offender, the imposition of life imprisonment was justified. The court found that the disparity in penalty for this crime between this and other states was not so shocking as to require the court to strike down the legislature's determination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of conspiring to commit first degree murder because the evidence corroborating the accomplice testimony was more than sufficient. The court affirmed the sentence of life imprisonment, finding that it was not cruel and unusual punishment given the nature of the offense and the offender.

 

1668 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Landes Constr. Co. v. Superior Court of San Luis


Civ. No. 5080


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


101 Cal. App. 3d 648; 161 Cal. Rptr. 691; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1428



Jan. 30, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Apr. 30, 1980 (L.A. 31253)
Proceeding dism. as moot May 14, 1980


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, first buyer, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent trial court to reverse its order, which granted a motion by respondent second buyers, to expunge a notice of lis pendens. Petitioner filed the notice pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. § 409.1 in its suit for specific performance of an agreement in which respondent sellers agreed to sell real property to petitioner.


OVERVIEW: Respondent sellers agreed to sell real property to petitioner first buyer. Respondent sellers then recorded a deed in favor of respondent second buyers. Petitioner filed for specific performance and a notice of lis pendens pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 409.1. Respondent second buyers filed a motion to expunge the notice, which respondent trial court granted. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate. The court issued the writ, finding that respondent trial court applied the wrong standard in ordering the expungement. The court held that under § 409.1, a party did not commence an action for a proper purpose and in good faith when: he did not believe the claim to be valid; he filed primarily because of hostility or ill will; he filed solely to deprive the opposing party of the beneficial use of his property; or he filed to force a settlement that had no relation to the merits of the action. The court held that even though a meritless underlying lawsuit was strong circumstantial evidence, respondent trial court could not expunge the notice based solely on the merits of the underlying action.


OUTCOME: The court issued the writ of mandate in favor of petitioner first buyer directing respondent trial court to set aside its order expunging the lis pendens and to hold a new hearing to redetermine the expungement in accordance with its opinion.

 

1669 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re James A.


Civ. Nos. 44725, 44727


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


101 Cal. App. 3d 332; 161 Cal. Rptr. 588; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1400



January 24, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 17, 1980. Newman, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 94214, 96907, Frances W. Mayer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As modified, the order in each case is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juveniles appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which sentenced them to confinement. Defendants argued that the court was not required to sentence them to the maximum available term and that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code. § 704 regarding the maximum term was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juveniles were sentenced to confinement at juvenile facilities for a term not to exceed the maximum possible, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 704. Defendants appealed their sentences, and argued that the trial court should have had the ability to sentence them to less than the maximum term, and that § 704 was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the sentences as modified to the correct maximum term, and held that the trial court was required to commit juveniles for the maximum possible sentence, and it was up to the California Youth Authority to determine the appropriate length of commitment. The court held that the purpose of a juvenile commitment was for rehabilitation, and once rehabilitation was achieved, parole would occur.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant juveniles' sentences with modification to the correct maximum term. The court held that the trial court had no discretion to sentence a juvenile to less than the maximum term, and that it was the role of the youth authority to determine the appropriate length of commitment.

 

1670 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Burres


Crim. No. 18597


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


101 Cal. App. 3d 341; 161 Cal. Rptr. 593; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1401



January 24, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 20, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 12652, Victor M. Castagnetto, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which convicted appellant for assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury upon the person of a peace officer in violation of Cal. Pen. Code §245(b), alleging the trial court erred in giving a jury charge on a presumption of intent without a qualification about the effect of the presumption.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested on two separate occasions by the same officer for traffic offenses. A pursuit ensued when appellant attempted to flee from the officer whom he allegedly feared from the first encounter. During the pursuit, appellant swerved into the officer's lane forcing the officer to the edge of the pavement to avoid a collision. A second officer was engaged and shots were fired at appellant. Appellant was later convicted for assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury upon the person of a peace officer under Cal. Pen. Code §245(b). The trial court gave a jury charge on the presumption of intent without explaining the effect thereof. On appeal, the court reversed the conviction and held the jury charge on the presumption of intent without qualifying the effect of the presumption was erroneous because it invaded the presumption of innocence and diluted the state's burden of proof; the effect of the presumption was to require appellant to produce only some evidence to show a lack of the requisite intent; and a conviction violated appellant's due process rights under the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and constituted reversible error per se.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction for assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury upon the person of a peace officer and held the trial court erred in giving the jury charge on the presumption of intent without explaining the effect of the presumption because it impermissibly shifted the burden of proof from the state to appellant; and the error violated the Fourteenth Amendment and constituted reversible error per se.

 

1671 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Roy E. Hanson, Jr. Mfg. Co. v. Los Angeles


Civ. No. 56036


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


101 Cal. App. 3d 381; 161 Cal. Rptr. 610; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1404



January 24, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. Feb. 21, 1980; Hg. granted Apr. 10, 1980 (See 27 C.3d 870)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 260985, Robert I. Weil, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants tax entities challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted plaintiff taxpayer's summary judgment motion and held the one-percent limitation on real property taxes set forth in Cal. Const. art. XIIIA (1978) applied to both secured and unsecured rolls, in an action under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 5140 challenging denial of plaintiff's refund brought under Cal. Rev. & Tax Code §§ 5096- 5097.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer's property was placed on the unsecured roll, but his tax rate was based upon the rate for the secured roll of the preceding year under the old statute, Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 12, which was almost three times the tax rate of those on the secured roll and did not reflect the one-percent limitation imposed by the new statute, Cal. Const. art. XIIIA (1978). Plaintiff paid the tax, sought a refund under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 5096- 5097, and filed a suit against defendants taxing entities under Cal. Tex. & Rev. Code § 5140 challenging the denial of its refund claim. The trial court granted plaintiff's and denied defendants' motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment and held the tax on plaintiff's property, on the unsecured roll, at a rate three times the one-percent rate of those on the secured roll was improper because such taxation was precluded under the new statute, which imposed a one-percent limitation and made no distinction between secured and unsecured rolls; and the new statute did not repeal by implication the old statute, because harmonization showed both statutes required equal treatment of secured and unsecured rolls.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of plaintiff taxpayer's summary judgment motion and held the taxing of unsecured rolls at a rate three times the one-percent rate of the secured rolls was precluded under the applicable new statute, which made no distinction between secured and unsecured rolls for assessment purposes and did not repeal by implication the old statute because both required equal treatment of secured and unsecured rolls.

Alfred v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 55707


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


101 Cal. App. 3d 260; 161 Cal. Rptr. 574; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1393



January 22, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 8, 1980, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 20, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 231196, Robert Fainer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff civil service employee appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed his complaint after the demurrer filed by defendant County of Los Angeles was sustained without leave to amend, in plaintiff's suit alleging failure to properly pay for the duties he had performed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff civil service employee appealed the trial court's dismissal of his complaint against defendant County of Los Angeles, arguing that his performance of duties outside his official title and job classification entitled him to compensation, and the denial of that compensation constituted a taking of property in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 19. The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint, holding that plaintiff was entitled only to the compensation expressly provided by statute or ordinance regardless of the extent of services actually rendered. The court commented that plaintiff had the opportunity to request an appointment to the classification of job in which he was actually working, but he did not do so. The court held that plaintiff's argument begged the question, because by the terms of his employment as statutorily defined, he was fully compensated for his services, so there was no "taking."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff civil service employee's action against defendant County of Los Angeles, because plaintiff had received all the compensation he could receive lawfully under the civil service provisions of the county charter.
