1674 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


ITT World Communications, Inc. v. County of Santa Clara


Civ. Nos. 46836, 46837, 46838, 46839, 46840, 46841, 46842, 46843


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


101 Cal. App. 3d 246; 162 Cal. Rptr. 186; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1392



January 22, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 723305, 710678, 723306, 710680, 710677, 723308, 723307, 710682, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant utility sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgments in favor of respondents, local governments and tax board, in appellants actions for a refund of property taxes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant utility filed actions against respondents, local government and board, after respondent board assessed appellant's property at values in excess of reproduction cost new less depreciation (RCNLD). The court affirmed the judgments of the trial court and found that the actions of respondent board were not arbitrary, in excess of discretion, or in violation of standards prescribed by law because in a regulated market like appellants where property received appraisal as a whole without reference to the value of its component parts, use of RCNLD as an absolute ceiling on the valuation of property was not necessarily appropriate. Furthermore, the court found that appellant did not present sufficient evidence that respondent board failed to deduct income that arose from enterprise activity when it used capitalization of earnings to make the property assessment. The court also found that respondent board was not bound by its prior policy of using RCNLD as a limit because the policy was never formally made into a regulation, therefore there was no need to formally repeal it, nor was the policy so well established that legislation was required to change it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of respondents, local governments and board, in appellant utility's actions for the recovery of taxes because respondent board did not act arbitrarily, in excess of discretion, or in violation of standards prescribed by law when it assessed appellant's property at values in excess of reproduction cost new less depreciation.

De Ronde v. Regents of University of Cal.


Civ. No. 16732


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


102 Cal. App. 3d 221; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1481



Jan. 21, 1980

* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant challenged the judgment in favor of co-appellant university by the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), which denied his motion to amend his complaint to include damages, and co-appellant challenged that portion of the judgment which found its admission procedures unconstitutional and enjoined the use of ethnic minority status as an admissions factor.


OVERVIEW: Appellant applicant was a white male that was denied admission to cross-appellant university's law school. Appellant sued cross-appellant claiming that its admission policy gave "minority" applicants admission preference. The trial court found that cross-appellant had violated the equal protection and due process clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the equal protection and privileges and immunities clauses of Cal. Const. art. 1, § 7, and enjoined co-appellant from using in the admissions process any criteria involving race, color, ethnic origin, or ethnic minority status to grant preference to applicants. The trial court denied appellant's motion for leave to amend his complaint to include damages because it was unnecessarily delayed and the evidence showed that he did not otherwise qualify for admission. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and found that under the doctrine of independent state grounds cross-appellant violated the provisions of Cal. Const. art. 1, § 7 and that appellant could not show any injury that arose out of the constitutional violations and he was not entitled to relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against appellant applicant I holding that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's late motion for leave to amend to plead damages, and affirmed the injunction against cross-appellant university prohibiting it from using any preferential criteria involving race, color, ethnic origin or ethnic minority in its admission process as it was unconstitutional to do so.

 

1676 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Walnut Properties, Inc. v. City Council


Civ. No. 54469


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


100 Cal. App. 3d 1018; 161 Cal. Rptr. 411; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1369



January 14, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 13, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C244644, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant movie theater challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's application for writ of mandamus to compel respondent city to set aside its revocation of appellant's business license. Appellant contended that the zoning ordinance under which the license was revoked was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant movie theater was purchased by new owners, who applied for and were granted a business license by respondent city. Before the theater was re-opened, a zoning ordinance was passed by respondent prohibiting business which provided adult entertainment from the area in which the theater was located. When respondent became aware that appellant was showing adult movies, the business license was revoked. Appellant filed an application for writ of mandamus with the trial court, and the trial court denied the application. Appellant challenged the trial court's decision, and argued that the zoning ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to appellant. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. The court held that zoning ordinances which limit the locations of adult entertainment are a valid exercise of police power and they did not restrict or limit access to adult entertainment in the city as a whole. The court held that respondent had not retroactively applied the ordinance to appellant because appellant could not be "grandfathered" under the prior business license because the prior owner showed family oriented, not adult movies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, and held that the zoning ordinance was a valid exercise of police power by respondent city and did not restrict or limit access to adult entertainment in the city as a whole. The court held that appellant movie theater could not argue that they were "grandfathered" by the prior license because the prior owner did not show adult movies.

 

1677 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Stanson v. San Diego Coast Regional Com.


Civ. No. 18554


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


101 Cal. App. 3d 38; 161 Cal. Rptr. 392; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1374



January 14, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 13, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 420768, Paul Eugene Overton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandamus. Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5. He sought to compel respondent, San Diego Coast Regional Commission, to issue a coastal development permit to approve of the remodeling of his commercial building to include a second-floor restaurant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant businessman owned a commercial building located within the coastal zone protected by respondent, San Diego Coast Regional Commission, by virtue of California's Coastal Act of 1976, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 3000 et seq. He sought to add a restaurant to the second floor of the building and was told that he would not need a permit from respondent to do so. When construction was almost complete, he was informed that he would need a permit, which was denied. He appealed to the California Coastal Commission, which affirmed the respondent's decision. The trial court held that he was not entitled to a writ of mandamus. The court reversed the trial court's decision and remanded for a new hearing. It held that the trial court erred in applying the substantial evidence test in reviewing the decision of respondent. It reasoned where appellant's property right, a constitutionally rooted right , was an issue before respondent, the independent judgment review standard was mandated in the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which denied appellant businessman's petition for writ of mandamus to compel respondent, San Diego Coast Regional Commission, to issue a coastal permit to approve the remodeling of his commercial building, and remanded the case. It did so because the trial court erred in applying the substantial evidence standard of review to respondent's decision.

 

1678 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Haskins v. San Diego County Dept. of Public Welfare


Civ. No. 18683


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


100 Cal. App. 3d 961; 161 Cal. Rptr. 385; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1365



January 11, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. 417321, 18683, Ross G. Tharp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant welfare recipient and husband challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which found no mandatory duty on the part of respondent County Department of Public Welfare to insure confidentiality of appellant's welfare records and no factual allegation of an unconstitutional policy and sustained respondent's demurrer and dismissed appellants' complaint.


OVERVIEW: Appellant welfare recipient and husband brought an action against respondent County Department of Public Welfare after appellant husband's then estranged wife used her position as an employee of respondent to initiate a welfare fraud investigation against appellant welfare recipient for living with a then unattached male. The investigation did not lead to any formal charges, but appellants claimed respondent violated its mandatory duty to protect the confidentiality of her welfare records. The trial court found respondent had no mandatory duty. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing appellant's causes of action, but found that respondent did have a duty to protect the confidentiality of appellant's welfare records. However, the court found that the duty was to provide safeguards to restrict disclosure to the functions necessary to further respondent's duties and only prohibited disclosure of welfare records to the public. Here, respondent employee used the information to initiate an investigation within respondent department and did not disclose the information to the public, therefore respondent did not breach its mandatory duty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant welfare recipient and husband's complaints on appeal because, while the court disagreed with the trial court and found that respondent County Department of Public Welfare did have a duty to prevent the disclosure of appellant's information, the duty only went so far as to prevent disclosure to the public and not disclosure directly connected with the administration of respondent's obligations.

 

1679 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Board of Supervisors v. Lonergan


Civ. No. 18580


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


100 Cal. App. 3d 841; 161 Cal. Rptr. 254; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1354



January 8, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified Jan. 30, 1980
Hg. granted Apr. 10, 1980 (See 27 C.3d 855)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 421865, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, county controller and tax collector, challenged an injunction issued by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which enjoined them from computing, billing, or collecting taxes on the unsecured tax rolls at a rate in excess of that authorized in Proposition 13, Cal. Const. art. XIII A, §§ 1, 2 (). The injunction was issued in a declaratory judgment action brought by plaintiff Board of Supervisors of San Diego County.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, county controller and tax collector, announced that San Diego county would tax property on the unsecured tax rolls, including possessory interests or improvements on real property owned by another, in the manner followed in previous years, under Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 12. Plaintiff Board of Supervisors of San Diego County sought an injunction and declaratory relief, under newly enacted Proposition 13, Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, which set a maximum ad valorem tax rate on real property. The trial court granted plaintiff's request and enjoined defendants from computing, billing, or collecting taxes on the unsecured tax rolls at a rate or an assessed value in excess of that authorized in Proposition 13. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that Proposition 13 applied to property on the unsecured tax rolls. The court noted that Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 1, which was not repealed by Proposition 13, provided that all property was taxable and should be assessed at the same percentage of fair market value. Thus, defendants' use of a higher tax rate only for property on the unsecured rolls was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the injunction granted against defendants, county controller and tax collector, because the use of the previous year's tax rate by defendants would impose an impermissible burden on property subject to the unsecured roll which was discriminatory and beyond the limits allowed by Proposition 13 and the California Constitution.

 

1680 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Covino


Crim. No. 19210


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


100 Cal. App. 3d 660; 161 Cal. Rptr. 155; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1343



January 4, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 69936, Joseph P. Kelly, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The convictions are affirmed. The judgment is remanded to the superior court for a new hearing on the question of aggravation or mitigation of the terms imposed for counts II and III.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which was rendered on a jury verdict finding appellant guilty of assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily harm under Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), and assault with intent to commit a felony under Cal. Penal Code § 221.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was apprehended by police while he was in his car trying to choke his victim. Appellant was convicted of assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury under Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), and assault with intent to commit a felony under Cal. Penal Code § 221, the felony being forcible oral copulation under Cal. Penal Code § 288a(c). On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and held that evidence regarding his victim's character was irrelevant to prove that appellant demanded she orally copulate him and assaulted her when she refused and was, thus, properly excluded. The court found that general intent, and not specific intent, was all that was required to be found by the jury in any assault case and that the evidence was sufficient to support the verdict because the actual injury to appellant's victim was not an element of the crime. The court remanded on the issue of aggravation of appellant's sentence because he did not actually have a prior conviction. The court also remanded the case on the issue of mitigation, because the trial court apparently refused to consider letters from friends and employers written on appellant's behalf.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for assault because the evidence was sufficient to support the jury finding and the jury was properly instructed that assault was a general intent crime. The court remanded to the trial court on the issues of mitigation and aggravation because it ignored letters in support of appellant, and appellant had no previous conviction.

 

1681 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harvey


Crim. No. 9889.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


114 Cal. App. 3d 252; 170 Cal. Rptr. 577; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2634



Dec. 31, 1980, As modified Jan. 5, 1981


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Apr. 9, 1981, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 52677, William H. Lally, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that convicted him for robbery and kidnapping and refused to limit the total of the subordinate sentence terms in accordance with Cal. Penal Code § 1170.1.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery and kidnapping. Essential evidence for defendant's robbery conviction was seized in a vehicle search of defendant's stolen automobile after an accident following a police chase. The appellate court found that police had probable cause to search the vehicle for weapons due to their observations and information received that the car had been involved in a shooting. In addition, due to defendant's abandonment of the vehicle, the search infringed no constitutional rights. The court examined Cal. Penal Code § 1170 .1 and Cal. Penal Code § 667.5 and concluded that defendant was not entitled to the benefit of the five-year limitation in § 1170.1(a) on the total of subordinate terms because his consecutive terms were for the violent felonies of robbery with a firearm within the definition of § 667.5. However, the court did err in imposing an additional one-year term for defendant's prior convictions of robbery and kidnapping which were served as part of a continuous term of imprisonment. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and modified his sentence by striking one year from the enhancement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision not to limit the total of defendant's subordinate terms as the statute did not require limitation, but the court did modify defendant's sentence due to an error in adding an additional one-year term based on defendant's prior convictions served continuously.

 

1682 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court of Santa Cruz County


Civ. No. 47966


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


102 Cal. App. 3d 505; 162 Cal. Rptr. 418; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1505



Feb. 25, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. Mar. 20, 1980; Hg. granted May 15, 1980 (See 115 C.A.3d 867)
Dec. 10, 1980, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 1, with directions to refileits opn. with appropriate reference to In re Jeanice D. (1980) 28 Cal3d 210.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned for a writ of mandate to challenge a decision of respondent, the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), that committed real party in interest to a youth authority after he was convicted of first degree murder. The state claimed that a newly enacted statute could not be applied retroactively.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest, a minor, committed murder in the perpetration of attempted robbery. At the time, Cal. Penal Code § 190 fixed the penalty for first degree murder at confinement for life and did not allow a person under age 21 to be committed to the youth authority. Before trial, § 190 was amended to increase the penalty for first degree murder, but to allow commitment to the youth authority. The superior court found real party in interest guilty, and in sentencing determined that old § 190 applied because the harsher, newly enacted statute would operate ex post facto. The superior court, however, concluded that real party in interest was qualified for commitment to the youth authority by reason of the newly enacted § 190. The state petitioned for a writ of mandate to determine the validity of the commitment to the youth authority, arguing that that statute could not be applied retroactively. The appellate court issued the writ and held that the superior court erred in committing the real party in interest to the youth authority because the new § 190 increased the penalty for first degree murder and would operate post facto to real party in interest.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the peremptory writ of mandate should issue because the trial court erred in committing criminal real party in interest to a youth authority after he was convicted of murder. The court ruled that a newly enacted statute, which increased the penalty for first degree murder but allowed commitment to the youth authority, could not be applied because application was a violation of the prohibition against ex post fact laws.

Deas v. Knapp


Civ. No. 43241.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


109 Cal. App. 3d 171; 167 Cal. Rptr. 146; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2151



Aug. 13, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 15, 1980 (S.F. 24224)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 415397, Lyle E. Cook, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant real estate commissioner challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Alameda County (California), which awarded plaintiff claimants costs in addition to $ 20,000 in their action to satisfy an underlying judgment they obtained in a class action against defendant real estate broker for usury. Plaintiff's challenged the amount of the award. Defendant real estate broker challenged the underlying judgment.


OVERVIEW: In the underlying class action, plaintiff claimants successfully sued defendant real estate broker for charging them illegal expenses, costs, and commissions. No appeal was taken from that judgment. Unable to satisfy that judgment, plaintiffs applied for payment from defendant Real Estate Education, Research, and Recovery Fund (fund) pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10471. The trial court awarded plaintiffs $ 20,000 plus costs. Defendant fund appealed the award of costs. Plaintiffs challenged the amount of the award. Defendant broker challenged the validity of the underlying judgment. On appeal, the court modified the judgment and affirmed. Defendant broker could not retry issues already determined against him by the underlying judgment. Plaintiffs' recovery properly was limited to $ 20,000. Although defendant broker had three licenses, the underlying judgment was based on acts for which only one license was required, and plaintiffs' claim, filed in 1971, did not qualify for the increased limitation in Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10474(b). The award of costs, however, was deleted. Under § 10474, the amount awarded could not exceed the statutory limitation of $ 20,000.


OUTCOME: The court deleted the award of costs to plaintiff claimants and affirmed the judgment as modified. In the suit to satisfy their underlying judgment against defendant real estate broker by seeking payment from defendant Real Estate Education, Research and Recovery Fund, plaintiff's recovery was limited to $ 20,000. Defendant real estate broker could not collaterally attack the underlying action.

 

1684 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Robinson v. Superior Court of Sacramento County


Civ. No. 18906


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


105 Cal. App. 3d 249; 164 Cal. Rptr. 389; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1771



Apr. 29, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. May 29, 1980; Hg. granted June 25, 1980 (S.F. 24185)
Jan. 5, 1984, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 3rd Dist., with directions to refile its opn. filed Apr. 29, 1980; and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish that opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Rules of Ct., rule 976(c)(2)).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) to grant petitioner's motion to suppress a tape recording of a conversation between petitioner and his wife.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner and his wife were arrested and placed into an interview room at the police department, where their conversation was secretly tape recorded. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent superior court to grant his motion for suppression of the tape recording. The court noted that, although prisoners give up some degree of privacy and rights consistent with their custodial status, prisoners do not give up all rights to privacy. In particular, the court noted that marital communications are presumed to have been made in confidence. The court found that the sole purpose of the eavesdropping was to obtain incriminating evidence, and there was no suggestion that a penological purpose was being served or that the security of a custodial institution was in any way involved. Accordingly, petitioner had a reasonable expectation of privacy in his marital communication, which was violated by the secret recording. A writ of mandate was issued and respondent was ordered to suppress the tape recording.


OUTCOME: A writ of mandate was issued and respondent superior court was ordered to suppress the tape recording of a conversation between petitioner and his wife. Petitioner was constitutionally entitled to privacy in his marital communication, the recording was done only to obtain incriminating evidence, and there was no suggestion that the security of a custodial institution was involved in any way.

People v. Henderson


Crim. No. 9939


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


102 Cal. App. 3d 854; 162 Cal. Rptr. 886; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 1536



Feb. 27, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Aug. 28, 1980


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 47822, William H. Lally, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which committed defendant as a Mentally Disordered Sex Offender (MDSO) pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6316.2, in defendant's hearing and examination to determine if he was an MDSO.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with forcing a minor to copulate him orally, willfully causing a child to suffer unjustifiable physical pain, and assault with force likely to produce great bodily injury. Defendant pled guilty and the lower court adjourned criminal proceedings and certified defendant for hearing and examination to determine if he was a Mentally Disordered Sex Offender (MDSO) pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6316.2. Defendant was found to be an MDSO and committed to mental health confinement. People moved for extension of the commitment, and the lower court appointed psychiatrists to examine defendant. Defendant was recommitted for an extended term of one year. On appeal, defendant argued that § 6316.2 was unconstitutional. The court affirmed and held that it had long been recognized that a psychiatric expert's appraisal of a defendant's dangerousness was directly related to the expert's evaluation of that defendant's existing mental state. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutional because it was vague and held that juries or judges would have to be convinced on case by case basis that a person poses a danger to others.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment of a one-year extension of defendant's Mentally Disordered Sex Offenders (MDSO) commitment because, under MDSO proceedings, the lower court was required to identify MDSOs with the assistance of psychiatrists. The court held that the MDSO statute was not an ex post facto law because, where legislature specified an operative date, the effective date was purely technical.

 

1688 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Kimbler


Civ. No. 56911


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


100 Cal. App. 3d 453; 161 Cal. Rptr. 53; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2458



December 26, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 20, 1980. Mosk, J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, adult bookstore clerk, sought a writ of habeas corpus claiming Cal. Penal Code § 311.2(c), (d), violated his rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV; Cal. Const. art. I, § 7; art. IV, § 16(a). Defendant's conviction for selling an obscene film under § 311.2 was affirmed and after denial of his habeas petition, the California Supreme Court issued an order to the court to show cause why the writ should not be granted.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, adult bookstore clerk, sold an obscene film to an undercover officer and was convicted of the misdemeanor offense of selling an obscene film in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 311.2(d). His conviction was affirmed. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus challenging the constitutionality of the provisions of § 311.2 exempting nonmanagerial employees without financial interest in their place of employment from criminal liability for exhibition of obscene matter. The court denied the writ. The supreme court issued an order to the court to show cause why the writ should not be granted. The court held the obvious purpose of § 311.2 was to deter the commercial dissemination of obscene matter. In light of this legitimate purpose, the court held it could not say that a legislative classification distinguishing between the exhibition and distribution of such obscene matter by nonmanagerial employees was palpably arbitrary and without any rational basis. The court held Cal. Penal Code § 311.2(c), (d), did not violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV; Cal. Const. art. I, § 7; art. IV, § 16(a), and petitioner's writ of habeas corpus was denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's, adult bookstore clerk, writ of habeas corpus because the court found the statutory provisions exempting nonmanagerial employees who exhibit obscene material did not unfairly discriminate against employees who distribute obscene material and were not unconstitutional. The court held petitioner failed to show the classification was palpably arbitrary.

W. E. J. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 56347


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


100 Cal. App. 3d 303; 160 Cal. Rptr. 862; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2425



December 21, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 27, 1980. Mosk, J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue requiring the respondent court to vacate its order of May 10, 1979, and conduct a new hearing. The stay order heretofore issued by this court shall remain in effect until respondent complies with this judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, adopting parents, appealed an order of the superior court (California) which granted custody of baby boy to real party in interest, biological father. At the same time, real party filed an original proceeding for a hearing in the Supreme Court, to determine whether or not the biological father of a nonmarital child, who was not a "presumed father" as defined in Cal. Civ. Code § 7004, had power to veto a proposed adoption.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, adopting parents, filed a petition to adopt a baby boy who had been released to them at birth by the mother and had been with them ever since. Real party, biological father, and petitioners appeared in the adoption proceeding, and sought custody of the child. The trial court entered an order finding that real party was entitled to the custody of minor and directed petitioners to surrender custody. Petitioners filed their petition seeking a prerogative writ, and a stay was entered. Real party filed an original proceeding for writ in the court. The court found that real party did not meet the statutory definition of "presumed father," because he and the mother had never attempted to marry, and the child had never been in his home. The court also found that real party's due process right was not violated because he had notice and an opportunity to be heard, and that his equal protection right was not violated because the gender-based classification statute was based on an actual difference in situation. A writ issued directing respondent court to vacate its custody order and conduct a new hearing, but the adoption could be ordered without real party's consent.


OUTCOME: A writ of mandate issued requiring respondent superior court to vacate its order granting custody to real party in interest biological father and conduct a new hearing because the biological father was entitled to be heard in opposition to the adoption proceedings, and to present his own qualification for custody, but the adoption could be ordered without his consent as provided by statute.

Goodhue v. Tureaud

Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


100 Cal. App. 3d 205; 160 Cal. Rptr. 677; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2416



December 20, 1979

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC 47774, Laurence J. Rittenband, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his motion to set aside respondent crime victim's default judgment in respondent's assault and battery action.


OVERVIEW: In respondent crime victim's assault and battery action against appellant inmate, the trial court denied appellant's motion to set aside the default judgment granted against him. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that despite the fact that appellant was incarcerated, he was not denied access to the courts. The court held that by failing to respond to respondent's suit, or to take steps to respond, appellant waived his right to access to the court. The court held that appellant had ample opportunity to respond, but chose not to.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant inmate's motion to set aside a default judgment obtained by respondent crime victim in her assault and battery suit. Appellant had ample opportunity to respond to the suit but failed to do so, and thus, appellant waived his right to access to the court.

San Diego Nursery Co. v. Agricultural Labor Relations Bd.


Civ. No. 18563


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


100 Cal. App. 3d 128; 160 Cal. Rptr. 822; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2409; 90 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,284



December 19, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 409508, Alfred Lord, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Agricultural Labor Relations Board sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction to respondent agricultural company prohibiting the agents of appellant from conducting worker education of agricultural workers on the premises of respondent.


OVERVIEW: Labor union attempted to enter the premises of respondent agriculture business for the purpose of engaging an organizational drive that could potentially culminate in an election petition. Under regulations promulgated by appellant Agricultural Labor Relations Board, respondent was required to furnish the names and addresses of all employees. Respondent refused, and agents of appellant entered respondent's place of business to obtain this information. Appellant later attempted to gain access to the premises, which was prevented by respondent. Respondent moved for a preliminary injunction, arguing that appellant did not have any power to enter private property to conduct worker education. The trial court granted the motion, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed, holding that under Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8, §§ 20900- 20901, appellant's right to access was limited in time, purpose, and place, and therefore was constitutional. However, even though the court determined that appellant's ad hoc procedure for conducting worker education was constitutional, the court stated that a clear rule was required in situations where unconsented access was desired.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of a preliminary injunction to respondent agriculture company prohibiting the agents of appellant agricultural labor relations board from conducting worker education of agricultural workers on the premises of respondent. The court held that the procedure of conducting worker education was constitutional, but that appellant's ad hoc procedure was insufficient to further the purposes of worker education.

 

1692 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lawson


Crim. No. 35951


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


100 Cal. App. 3d 60; 161 Cal. Rptr. 7; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2403



December 18, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Downey Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M 33126, F. Lawrence Plotkin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from their conviction by the Municipal Court for the Downey Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) for falsely reporting a crime to a law enforcement officer, Cal. Penal Code § 148.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were union members picketing in front of a plant and the incident giving rise to the charges of falsely reporting a crime to a law enforcement officer, under Cal. Penal Code § 148.5, grew out of a labor relations dispute between the striking workers and those still working. Defendants contended that a non-striking employee tried to run over one of the defendants as the employee was leaving the plant. There was contradictory testimony from the employee and a security guard that defendant actually stepped in front of the path of the truck and struck the hood with his hands. Other testimony supported the contention that defendants were harassing employees as they were leaving. The court affirmed defendants' conviction and held that even if the employee failed to yield to a pedestrian walking on the sidewalk in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 21952, the reporting of such violation did not insulate defendants from liability since the other reported violation of assault did not occur. The court concluded that § 148.5 did not infringe upon defendants' privilege against self-incrimination, did not punish honest mistakes and was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for falsely reporting a crime to a law enforcement officer. The court concluded that the evidence was sufficient to support the convictions and the contention that defendants were trying to implicate nonstriking plant employees in a criminal act by reporting that these employees had tried to run defendants over with a vehicle as defendants were picketing outside the plant.

Baker v. Martin


Civ. No. 18229


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


99 Cal. App. 3d 881; 160 Cal. Rptr. 619; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2383



Dec. 17, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. Jan. 16, 1980; Hg. granted Feb. 14, 1980 (S.F. 24118)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, Nos. 2119, 2339, 2415, 2355, 2337, Bill L. Dozier, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant district attorney sought review of an order by the Superior Court, San Joaquin County (California), which ruled that the institutionalization process of respondent mentally retarded persons violated the Due Process Clauses of the California and United States Constitutions and denied the petition for institutionalization.


OVERVIEW: Appellant district attorney filed petitions seeking the institutionalization of respondent mentally retarded persons. The petitions alleged that each was developmentally disabled and could not provide for their basic needs. The petitions further provided that respondents were not willing to accept care and treatment on a voluntary basis. The institutionalization procedure, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. §§ 4803 and 4825 provided for institutionalization of mentally retarded persons without a judicial hearing. The trial court ruled that this procedure was unconstitutional because it violated due process, and appellant sought review, arguing that respondents were accorded due process because they were referred by a regional mental health facility. The court affirmed, holding that the procedure for institutionalization outlined in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 48- 3 and 4825 violated the due process rights of respondents. The court came to the conclusion because it found that personal liberty was a fundamental interest, and therefore a judicial hearing was required because the state sought to deprive respondents of their liberty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling of the trial court that the procedure with which respondent mentally retarded persons would be institutionalized violated the due process clauses of the federal and state constitutions. The court held that personal liberty was a fundamental interest, and therefore due process required a judicial hearing whenever the state sought to deprive an adult of his liberty by committing him to a mental hospital.

Warden v. Kahn


Civ. No. 43440


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


99 Cal. App. 3d 805; 160 Cal. Rptr. 471; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2375



December 14, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 3, 1980. Elkington, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 7, 1980. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 484107-0, William J. Hayes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment below insofar as it dismissed the second amended complaint is affirmed as to all defendants, and insofar as it dismissed the first amended complaint is affirmed as to all defendants other than Melvin Kahn. (See fn. 8.) The judgment dismissing the first amended complaint as to Melvin Kahn is reversed 8 and remanded for further proceedings not inconsistent with the views expressed herein. Appellant is to bear costs of appeal.



8 The Kahns argue that it appears from the record that the statute of limitations had expired on any alleged Penal Code section 632 violation. This argument is based upon the allegations of the first amended complaint, filed in November 1976, to the effect that the alleged recordings occurred between 1964 and July 19, 1974. While the first amended complaint alleges also that plaintiff did not discover the recordation until April 30, 1976, the Kahns argue that allegation is belied by a letter dated June 27, 1974, from plaintiff to Melvin Kahn, which was submitted with Melvin Kahn's declaration in support of a motion for summary judgment, and which makes reference to "numerous recordings . . . surreptitiously made." Thus, the Kahns argue that plaintiff had knowledge of the recordings at least by June 27, 1974, and that the action was therefore barred by the one-year period of limitations prescribed both for actions upon a statute for penalty or forfeiture (Code Civ. Proc., § 340, subd. 1) and for general tort actions (Code Civ. Proc., § 340, subd. 3).

Since this argument was not raised in the trial court, and involves a potential factual issue, its consideration on appeal would be inappropriate. We note for the guidance of the trial court on remand, however, that respondents are correct in contending that the action is one to recover a "statutory penalty or forfeiture" within the meaning of Code of Civil Procedure section 340, subdivision 1. (Cf. Holland v. Nelson (1970) 5 Cal.App.3d 308, 312 [85 Cal.Rptr. 117].)

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorney challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which dismissed his first amended complaint and struck his second amended complaint against defendants, client and others, in a personal injury action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff attorney filed a complaint against defendants, client and others, alleging defendant client recorded telephone conversations between himself and plaintiff that were confidential and without his knowledge. The trial court sustained a demurrer filed by defendants and allowed plaintiff time to amend. Plaintiff then filed a second amended complaint. The trial court granted defendant's motion to strike plaintiff's second amended complaint and dismissed plaintiff's first amended complaint. Upon review, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held plaintiff's second amended complaint was improper because it stated entirely new and different causes of action. The court held plaintiff's first complaint sufficiently stated a cause against defendant client under Cal. Penal Code § 632 where he alleged defendant recorded confidential phone calls without the consent of plaintiff. The court found the test for determining whether the conversations were confidential under Cal. Penal Code § 632, was whether based on the circumstances either party desired the conversations to be confined and whether plaintiff reasonably expected the communications would be recorded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment striking plaintiff attorney's second amended complaint and reversed that part of the judgment that dismissed plaintiff's first amended complaint against defendant client. The court held that plaintiff sufficiently stated a cause of action for unauthorized recording of confidential conversations where plaintiff alleged defendant recorded phone conversations between himself and defendant.

 

1695 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Citizens against Rent Control/Coalition for Fair Housing v. Berkeley


Civ. No. 43451


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


99 Cal. App. 3d 736; 160 Cal. Rptr. 448; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2368



Dec. 13, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Feb. 27, 1980 (S.F. 24124)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 493837-0, John P. Sparrow, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city and others, challenged a order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of plaintiff political committee declaring that § 602 of the Berkeley, Cal., Election Reform Act of 1974 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff political committee filed suit against defendants, city and others, that sought injunctive relief and a judicial declaration that § 602 of the Berkeley Election Reform Act, which limited campaign contributions, was unconstitutional. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiff. Upon review, the court affirmed. The court held that § 602 was unconstitutional where the government did not have a compelling interest because the interest behind the act was to prevent potential for corruption implicit in contributions, and there was no such interest with regard to issue-oriented committees. The court reasoned there was a tighter nexus between a contributor and a one issue-organization, and that there was a greater likelihood the contribution would not be used as the contributor wishes than with the attenuated relationship between a contributor and a political candidate who was obliged to take a position on more than one issue. The court found that § 602 was a direct and substantial restriction on plaintiffs' rights of free speech and association because it employed means that were unnecessarily broad.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment that was entered against defendants, city and others. The court held a city campaign act that restricted the amount of contributions was unconstitutional where the government did not have a compelling interest behind limiting contributions to plaintiff political committee because there was no potential for corruption implicit in the contribution.

 

1696 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Western Elec. Co. v. Workers' Compensation Appeals Bd. (Smith)


Civ. No. 50276


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


99 Cal. App. 3d 629; 160 Cal. Rptr. 436; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2361; 44 Cal. Comp. Cases 1145



December 12, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The WCAB's decision is annulled and the matter is remanded to the WCAB for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion herein. 7



7 We note that Western failed to file any report from Dr. Shadid at the time of the appeals board litigation over the issues of injury and permanent disability. (See WCAB Rules of Practice and Procedure (Cal. Admin. Code, tit. 8, ch. 4.5, subch. 2) §§ 10608, 10615, 10622.)

We further note that at the time of the proceedings below the WCAB was under the impression that the mandated liberal construction of the workers' compensation laws in favor of the injured worker pursuant to section 3202 did not apply to section 132a. Judson Steel holds section 3202 does apply to section 132a. ( Judson Steel, supra, 22 Cal.3d at p. 668.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer sought review of a decision from respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California), which following reconsideration annulled the decision and order of the workers' compensation judge. The workers' compensation judge had denied respondent applicant's claim seeking benefits under Cal. Lab. Code § 132a (2a).


OVERVIEW: Respondent applicant sought an increase in workers' compensation benefits under Cal. Lab Code § 132, on the basis that appellant employer refused to allow her to return to work because she had filed a workers' compensation claim. The workers' compensation judge (WCJ), denied plaintiff's petition finding that there was no improper conduct by appellant. Respondent workers' compensation appeals board (board) reversed and awarded a 50 percent increase in respondent applicant's award. Upon appeal, the court annulled respondent board's decision and remanded. The court found that the finding of a misdemeanor violation of Cal. Lab. Code § 132a was not a condition precedent to assertion of the civil remedy of § 132a. The court held that respondent board improperly reversed the WCJ where it failed to review the entire record and where there was no evidence of "considerable substantiality" supporting its reversal. The court reasoned that respondent board merely isolated certain parts of the record that supported its decision and improperly ignored that part of the record that explained testimony, and also failed to give the WCJ the "great weight" to which they were entitled.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the decision of respondent workers' compensation appeals board (board) that reversed a judgment by a workers' compensation judge (WCJ) and awarded respondent applicant an increase in benefits. The court held that respondent board improperly reversed the WCJ where there was no evidence of "considerable substantiality" supporting reversal and where respondent board failed to review the entire record.

 

1697 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Universal City Studios, Inc. v. Workers' Compensation Appeals Bd. (Lewis)


Civ. No. 55017


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


99 Cal. App. 3d 647; 160 Cal. Rptr. 597; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2362; 44 Cal. Comp. Cases 1133



December 12, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Repondents' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied February 15, 1980. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The award is annulled; the order of the Board is reversed and the matter remanded to the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board with directions to grant reconsideration.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer sought review of the determination of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (Board) which awarded respondent employee compensation for her claimed disability, resulting from a sprained ankle. Petitioner claimed that the Board failed to consider all of the relevant evidence, and that evidence did not support the award of permanent disability based on a semi-sedentary work restriction.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board awarded respondent employee compensation for her claimed disability, resulting from a sprained ankle. Employee was injured when she jumped over a puddle in employer's parking lot and received a sprained ankle. The worker's compensation judge found the injury resulted in permanent disability of 61 percent based on a semi-sedentary work restriction. The court found that there was no objective evidence from which the doctors concluded that employee was permanently restricted because of the injury to semi-sedentary work. The court ruled that the percentage of disability determined by use of the rating schedule was only prima facie evidence of the percentage of permanent disability to be attributed to each injury and not absolute, binding and final. The court annulled the award, reversed the order, and remanded because the employee had not returned to work by choice, nor by medical restriction or prohibition. The court held that employee was entitled to some fixed and definite sum for actual permanent disability.


OUTCOME: The court determined that there was not sufficient evidence to support respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board's (Board) award to respondent employee. The court annulled the award, reversed the order of the Board, and remanded the matter to the Board with directions to grant petitioner employer's application for reconsideration.

Perlman v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 54725


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


99 Cal. App. 3d 568; 160 Cal. Rptr. 567; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2356



December 11, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of plaintiffs and respondents for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 7, 1980. Newman, J., of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 228706, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order (judgment) is reversed and the writ is remanded to the superior court with leave to defendants to amend to cure the defect in the verification of their petition. The superior court is instructed to thereafter enter judgment in accordance with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a peremptory writ of prohibition as to a proceeding in municipal court charging respondent religious group members with a violation of Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 44.12 by soliciting contributions without an information card.


OVERVIEW: Criminal complaints charged respondent religious group members with violating an ordinance requiring an information card be obtained prior to soliciting charitable contributions. Respondents' demurrer was overruled. Respondents filed a petition for writ of prohibition, verified by their attorney's signature, which was granted, and a writ issued. Appellant state's demurrer was overruled. The lower court ruled that the ordinance was unconstitutional on its face. The state appealed. The court found that the defective petition for writ was curable by amendment. The court reviewed the substantive issues, reversed the trial court, and held that the ordinance's disclosure requirements were reasonably related to the purpose of the regulation, that the value to the public in insuring that charitable donations were distributed as indicated on the information cards outweighed the burden of disclosure required of those who intend to solicit, and that the minimal requirement of providing information for public disclosure prior to solicitation was not burdensome when the public agency had no discretion to invoke prior restraint. The court held that the ordinance was constitutional on its face.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed and the writ was remanded to the superior court with leave to respondent religious group members to amend to cure the defect in the verification of their petition, and with instructions to enter judgment in favor of appellant state because the solicitation ordinance was constitutional on its face, and the requirement of obtaining an information card was a reasonable exercise of the police power.

Witt v. Union Oil Co.


Civ. No. 55617


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


99 Cal. App. 3d 435; 160 Cal. Rptr. 285; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2443



December 6, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 220439, Jess Whitehill, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment dismissing plaintiff's complaint is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff franchisee challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed a case against defendant oil company.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff franchisee appealed the dismissal of his action after defendant oil company's general demurrer was sustained and the case was dismissed. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that there could have been no wrongful refusal to renew a franchise unless the franchisee had some right of renewal. The court held that even if plaintiff had some right of renewal, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 20999.1 could not be applied retroactively. There was nothing in the lease requiring defendant to renew or any provisions that would justify plaintiff in believing that the lease would be renewed and there was no form of retaliatory action on the part of defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff franchisee's suit against defendant oil company because plaintiff had no right to renewal and there was no retaliatory action on the part of defendant.

Kinney v. Vaccari

OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded judgment against defendants, cabin owner and associated individuals, in a case brought by plaintiff tenants because penalties were constitutionally excessive. The court also found that it was proper for the court to exclude introduction of bank statements into evidence.

 

1701 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Garvey


Crim. No. 18872


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


99 Cal. App. 3d 320; 160 Cal. Rptr. 73; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2506



December 3, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 30, 1980. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Humboldt County, No. 8473, Thomas M. Montgomery, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Humboldt County (California), which sentenced him to imprisonment after a jury found him guilty of assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245(a). He contended that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence in the form of a letter written to a friend while he was detained in jail awaiting trial.


OVERVIEW: While in jail awaiting trial for attacking a fellow patron in a bar, appellant wrote a letter to a friend describing the events. His motion to suppress the letter was denied, as were his evidentiary objections to admission of the letter. The court affirmed the judgment, rejecting appellant's argument that the letter should have been suppressed. The court held that the jailer's actions did not violate appellant's First Amendment rights because the letter was copied under a county program to monitor all incoming and outgoing prisoner mail, consistent with U.S. Supreme Court precedent. The letter was copied for a proper purpose because it offered to provide a county jail inmate with a pistol. Appellant's U.S. Const. amend. IV claim lacked merit, and the court held that a prisoner generally had no expectation of privacy as to letters posted by him. The copy of the letter was properly admitted under an exception to the best evidence rule because the original was shown to be unavailable. The admission of part of the letter's contents after the defense rested was within the trial court's discretion in reopening the prosecution's case in chief pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1094.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the interception of the letter by the jailer under a county program for monitoring all inmate mail did not violate appellant's First Amendment rights or his Fourth Amendment rights, because a prisoner generally had no reasonable expectation of privacy as to letters mailed in prison. Appellant's evidentiary arguments as to the letter were also held by the court to be without merit.

 

1702 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


National Organization for Reform of Marijunan Laws v. Gain


Civ. No. 45596


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


100 Cal. App. 3d 586; 161 Cal. Rptr. 181; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2472



November 30, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 20, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 697445, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant marijuana users sought review of an order by the Superior Court of San Francisco County (California), which granted respondents, chief of police and attorney general, summary judgment on appellant's action for declaratory and injunctive relief. The action sought a ruling that Health and Safety Code sections that prohibited the private possession and use of marijuana were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant marijuana users filed suit for declaratory injunctive relief against respondent police chief. The action sought a declaration that California Health and Safety Code sections that prohibited the private possession and use of marijuana were unconstitutional. Respondent attorney general intervened and filed a motion for summary judgment, which was granted by the trial court. Appellants sought review, arguing that the Health and Safety Code sections violated appellants' constitutional rights of privacy, equal protection, due process, pursuit of happiness, and protection against cruel and unusual punishment. The court affirmed, holding that the right of privacy as set forth in Cal. Const. art. I, § 1 did not guarantee the privilege of smoking marijuana, even in the privacy of the home. The court further stated that the equal protection argument did not have merit because the legislature had a rational basis for determining that marihuana was harmful. The court also stated that it could not rule on whether statutes that set forth penalties for marijuana use constituted cruel and unusual punishment because such a determination was only used when punishment was imposed.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court granting summary judgment to respondents, police chief and attorney general, and denying declaratory and injunctive relief to appellant marijuana users was affirmed. The court held that the right to privacy did not guarantee citizens the privilege of smoking marijuana, even in the privacy of the home. The court also held that statutory penalties for marijuana use did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.

 

1703 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Sorenson v. Randolph


Civ. No. 45103


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


99 Cal. App. 3d 183; 160 Cal. Rptr. 69; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2496



November 30, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 24, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 196146, W. Howard Hartley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nightclub operators took issue with the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which ordered appellants' nightclub closed pursuant to the Red Light Abatement Act, Cal. Penal Code §§ 11225- 35. Appellants made several contentions of reversible error on appeal, including that the abatement order amounted to a prior restraint on their First Amendment right to free expression.


OVERVIEW: Appellant nightclub operators appealed from an order closing their nightclub, which featured pornographic films, nude dancing, hired female companionship, and public masturbation, as a public nuisance under the Red Light Abatement Act, Cal. Penal Code §§ 11225- 35. On appeal, the court affirmed, rejecting each of appellants' arguments. The trial court's failure to make the essential finding that the acts on the premises were lewd was not reversible, because had the court made such a formal finding it would have been adverse to appellants based on the evidence. No finding of specific intent to perform a sexual act was required in an equitable public nuisance proceeding. Nor were appellants entitled to a jury trial in a proceeding to abate a public nuisance. The order of abatement did not infringe on appellants' U.S. Const. amend. I rights as a prior restraint, because such interference was a reasonable exercise of the state's police power pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 11230. Finally, the court found unpersuasive appellants' contention that the abatement order was overbroad, holding that closure for one year was neither inherently unreasonable nor an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding that the abatement order was a reasonable exercise of the state's police power and did not improperly infringe upon appellant nightclub operators' First Amendment rights. The court held that the trial court had broad discretion to fashion appropriate remedies to abate public nuisances, and given the abundant evidence in the record in the case, the trial court did not abuse that discretion.

 

1704 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Carson Redevelopment Agency v. Wolf


Civ. No. 55451


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


99 Cal. App. 3d 239; 160 Cal. Rptr. 213; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2500



November 30, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 28, 1979, and the petition of plaintiff and appellant for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 24, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C146792, Herbert M. Klein, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order from which plaintiff has appealed is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants agency and corporation both challenge a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant corporation's claim for compensation for loss of business good will and awarded attorney fees to respondent land owner in appellant agency's eminent domain proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Appellant agency commenced an eminent domain proceeding on respondent landowner's property upon which appellant corporation was a lessee. Appellant corporation appealed the proceeding to request compensation for loss of business good will. Appellant agency also appealed when attorney fees were awarded to respondent when appellant agency sought a court order to inspect the property. On the joinder of appeals, the court held that appellant corporation was not entitled to compensation for loss of business good will because the legislation that provided for this type of compensation precluded proceedings commenced prior to 1976 and appellant corporation's proceeding began in 1975. The court found the legislation was constitutionally justified as an economic regulation and in compliance with the rational relationship test. The court also held that appellant agency had substantially interfered with respondent's business when it conducted an unannounced inspection of the property that the subsequent court order was protective in nature to prevent further interference and as such, respondent was entitled to attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The order denying appellant corporation compensation for loss of business good will in an eminent domain proceeding was affirmed because new legislation precluded the compensation and was constitutionally justified, and the order awarding respondent attorney fees was affirmed because the court order sought by appellant agency was protective in nature due to appellant agency's past interference with respondent business.

People v. Compelleebee


Crim. No. 4291


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


99 Cal. App. 3d 296; 160 Cal. Rptr. 233; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2504



November 30, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 26, 1980, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 14, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 16727, Joseph F. Noriega, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which extended defendant's term as a mentally disordered sex offender, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6316.2, and denied amenability to treatment as a prerequisite to the extension.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted and committed to a mental health facility as a mentally disordered sex offender. Before the expiration of defendant's initial term, proceedings were instituted to extend defendant's commitment period and the lower court recommitted defendant for an additional year. Defendant appealed, claiming that amenability to treatment was a prerequisite to his term extension and that the evidence was insufficient to show that defendant could benefit from further treatment. The court held that a proper interpretation of the extension statute in conjunction with the original commitment statute led to the constitutionally sound result that a prerequisite existed to determine whether defendant would benefit from treatment before extending his commitment term. The court also found that evidence indicated defendant would not benefit from further treatment as facility psychiatrists reported that defendant would continue to be a danger to society and his minimal improvements in the past year were due to the institutional environment of the facility rather than the facility treatments.


OUTCOME: The order extending defendant's term commitment as a mentally disordered sex offender was reversed on the grounds that proper statutory interpretation called for the prerequisite finding that defendant would benefit from further treatment prior to extending defendant's commitment term in a mental health facility and the evidence did not show that defendant would so benefit.

Ostrager v. State Board of Control


Civ. No. 45277


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


99 Cal. App. 3d 1; 160 Cal. Rptr. 317; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2479



November 29, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 27, 1980. Manuel, J., did not participate therein. Newman, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 734773, Byron Arnold, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant non-resident crime victim challenged an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which sustained respondent State Board of Control's demurrer to appellant's petition for mandate, declaratory and other relief that the residency requirement under California Victims of Crime Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 13959-13969.1, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In appellant non-resident crime victim's petition for writ of mandate, declaratory and other relief, seeking a determination that the residency limitation in the California Victims of Crime Act (Act), Cal. Gov't Code §§ 13959-13969.1, was unconstitutional, the trial court sustained respondent State Board of Control's demurrer. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the Act did not violate the Privileges and Immunities clause or the Equal Protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court held that recovering compensation for losses caused by crime was not a basic right, and therefore, treating residents and non-residents differently was not unconstitutional. The court held that treating non-residents and residents differently under the Act was rationally related to the state's legitimate objective of cost savings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sustaining of respondent State Board of Control's demurrer to appellant non-resident crime victim's petition for mandate, declaratory and other relief. The California Victims of Crime Act (Act) was not unconstitutional as recovering for losses caused by crime was not a basic right, and treating non-residents and residents differently under the Act was rationally related to the state's legitimate objective of cost savings.

Binet-Montessori, Inc. v. San Francisco Unified School Dist.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner private school sought a writ of mandamus seeking to compel respondent public school district to permit it to compete equally for leases for vacant school district property under Cal. Educ. Code § 39371.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner private school challenged as unconstitutional a resolution adopted by respondent public school district which restrained respondent from leasing vacant district property to private schools. Petitioner sought a writ of mandamus compelling respondent to allow it to compete equally for said leases. The court issued the original writ of mandate, holding that respondent's restriction was unconstitutional. The court held that there was a constitutional right to conduct a private school.
