1708 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Coate


Civ. No. 45767


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


98 Cal. App. 3d 982; 159 Cal. Rptr. 794; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2346



November 23, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 721311, Robert D. Carter, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment below is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff creditor appealed the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment to defendant insurer in plaintiff's suit to recover as beneficiary of debtor's insurance.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sued defendant to recover as beneficiary of deceased debtor's life insurance. After the trial court granted summary judgment to defendant, plaintiff appealed. On appeal, the court noted that the trial court did not ascribe any reasons for its ruling. The court reversed because defendant had the duty to notify an assignee of a prospective lapse and right to reinstatement of the policy. A similar duty existed on the part of the insurer to notify the assignee that the assigned policy had already lapsed. The court concluded that the trial court abused its discretion in granting the summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment to defendant.

 

1709 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Graham v. Superior Court


Civ. Nos. 47698, 47723


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


98 Cal. App. 3d 880; 160 Cal. Rptr. 10; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2336



November 20, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied December 19, 1979, and the application of petitioner Graham and the petition of real party in interest in No. 47698 for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied January 17, 1980.



DISPOSITION: A writ will issue commanding respondent court to proceed on the basis that no death penalty can be imposed for the offenses petitioners are charged with. The alternative writ in 1/Civil 47723 is discharged and the petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmates sought a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) to vacate its order finding the mandatory death penalty provision of Cal. Penal Code § 4500 to be constitutional.


OVERVIEW: When petitioner inmates killed a state correctional officer while they were incarcerated, they were charged under former Cal. Penal Code § 4500. After an initial mistrial and a subsequent reversal on unrelated grounds, petitioners filed a motion for an order determining that § 4500 was unconstitutional. When respondent superior court denied their motion, petitioners sought a writ of mandate. On review, the court observed that former § 4500 had since been amended to provide for an alternative punishment of life without parole. The court held that because the real party in interest, the state, had failed to establish that only death was an adequate penalty of malicious assault on a noninmate by a life prisoner and former § 4500 failed to provide for the consideration of mitigating factors, the statute as it existed at the time of petitioners' crime was unconstitutional. The court further held that the punishment provisions of the amended version of § 4500 could not be applied to petitioners retroactively. Thus, the court issued a writ ordering respondent court to proceed on the basis that the death penalty could not be imposed for the offenses with which petitioners were charged.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ ordering respondent superior court to proceed on the basis that petitioner inmates could not be sentenced to death for their crimes under a mandatory death penalty statute that had been amended since petitioners' crimes, because the statute was unconstitutional in its failure to consider mitigating circumstances and real party in interest, the state, did not show that death was the only adequate remedy.

 

1710 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Magalnick v. Magalnick


Civ. No. 56362


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


98 Cal. App. 3d 753; 159 Cal. Rptr. 889; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2319



November 14, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 8, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C238337, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband disputed the denial by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), of his motion to vacate judgment entered by the court clerk under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1710.15 and 1710.25. He argued that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1710.10- 1710.65 unconstitutionally denied him due process of law, as did respondent wife's failure to give notice under Cal. Civ. Proc. § 1710.30, and the judgment and a sister state's judgment were void.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband disputed the denial of his motion to vacate a judgment entered by the clerk under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1710.15 and 1710.25. On appeal, the court held that there was no valid constitutional objection to the procedure to enter a judgment based on that of a sister state because, before the judgment was effective through execution to divest appellant of any assets, he had the right to nullify the effect of the sister state's judgment in this state in the same fashion by which he could file an action in this state upon the sister state judgment to establish it. Respondent wife's failure to give notice did not deny appellant due process, because his time to move to vacate the judgment ran from the date of service of the notice. Under Cal. Evid. Code §§ 459 and 455, the court took judicial notice of the proceedings in the sister state and held that appellant had voluntarily appeared and participated in those proceedings. Therefore, the trial court's finding that the sister state judgment was not void was correct. Appellant was not prejudiced by the trial court's erroneous failure to make findings of fact and conclusions of law. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the entry of a sister state's judgment, where the statutory scheme did not unconstitutionally deny appellant husband due process because, before the judgment was effective, appellant had the right to attempt to nullify its effect. The failure of respondent wife to give notice did not deny appellant due process, because his time to move to vacate the judgment ran from the date of service of the notice.

 

1711 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Miller v. R. K. A. Management Corp.


Civ. No. 18404


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


99 Cal. App. 3d 460; 160 Cal. Rptr. 164; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2446



November 5, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 411702, Gilbert Harelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff debtor corporation's officers challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which sustained defendant asset purchaser's demurrer, dismissed plaintiffs' case based on joinder and res judicata issues, and expunged their notice of lis pendens. Plaintiffs sought to have invalidated the bankruptcy court's ordered sale of plaintiffs' real property, free and clear of any liens, to defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff debtor corporation's officers had transferred 226 lots of a piece of property, owned by the corporation, by security agreement to one of its officers. Plaintiffs' corporation declared bankruptcy. The bankruptcy trustee sold all of the corporation's real property assets to defendant asset purchaser, including the lots owned by one plaintiff. Plaintiffs filed an action to have the sale set aside, to quiet title, and to recover damages, and filed a notice of lis pendens. Defendant demurred. The court affirmed the superior court's order that sustained defendant's demurrer. The court concluded that jurisdiction of the matter remained in the federal court, not the state court, under 11 U.S.C.S. § 511. The court found that plaintiffs had failed to join the bankruptcy trustee, who was an indispensable party, and that the complaint was barred by res judicata because the federal court had already dismissed an identical action. The grant of the motion to expunge the notice of lis pendens was not appealable under Cal. Civ. Proc. § 409.4. The court assessed sanctions on plaintiffs for prosecuting a frivolous appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's order that sustained defendant asset purchaser's demurrer in plaintiff debtor corporation's officers' action to have a sale set aside, to quiet title, and to recover damages. The court concluded that jurisdiction remained in the federal court under the bankruptcy code and ordered that the dismissal of debtor corporation's complaint in state court, based on joinder and res judicata issues, was proper.

 

1712 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Western & Southern Life Ins. Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 55251


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


99 Cal. App. 3d 410; 159 Cal. Rptr. 539; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2439



November 2, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 28, 1979, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 27, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-3189, Alexander R. Early III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant requested review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which ruled in favor of respondent insurance company, finding that a provision of California's tax law, as it applied to respondent, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: At issue were two provisions of California law, which imposed taxes on foreign insurance companies. The trial court ruled in favor of respondent insurance company, finding that one of the provisions of this alleged retaliatory tax was unconstitutional. Respondent was an Ohio corporation doing business in the state of California, with its principle office in Ohio. The taxes imposed by appellant, the taxing board for the state, were claimed to be excessive by respondent. The amount of taxes imposed by Ohio on a California corporation doing business there, was more than the amount of taxes imposed by California on a foreign corporation. Respondent was then charged a retaliatory tax equal to the difference between the two. The trial court ruled that this retaliatory tax was unconstitutional. The appellate court held that the retaliatory tax was not unconstitutional and reversed the holding of the trial court.


OUTCOME: Appellant's request for relief was granted and the appellate court held that the contested provision of California's tax law, as it applied to respondent, was not unconstitutional, therefore the court reversed the lower court's decision.

 

1713 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Briggs v. State of California ex rel. Dept. Parks & Recreation


Civ. No. 44189


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


98 Cal. App. 3d 190; 159 Cal. Rptr. 390; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2264



October 30, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 29, 1979, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petition of the plaintiff and appellant for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 24, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 71974, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Each party to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant park department appealed the judgment entered in the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) in favor of plaintiff landowner in her action for inverse condemnation. Plaintiff counter-appealed on the issue of postjudgment interest and litigation expenses.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landowner filed an action against defendant park department for inverse condemnation when it began action to purchase her ocean front property but did not directly condemn it and refused her applications for permits to develop it. When plaintiff was awarded substantial damages at trial, defendant appealed, contending her cause of action for inverse condemnation was improper and the damage award was excessive. Plaintiff counter-appealed on the issue of postjudgment interest and litigation expenses. The court agreed with defendant and reversed the judgment, holding that defendant had the right to use temporary regulatory measures to preserve plaintiff's coastal land from development pending the adoption of a comprehensive land use plan, such as the park envisioned by defendant. The court found that defendant's actions were consistent with Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 27001, 27302 to preserve the scenic resources of the coastal area and supported by the condemnation procedures set forth in Cal. Gov't Code §§ 15850, 15853, 15860. Since plaintiff had no claim as a matter of law for inverse condemnation, the court did not consider the other issues on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, holding that plaintiff's inverse condemnation action was predicated on the erroneous legal theory that she had a cause of action against defendant park department. Consequently, the court did not address plaintiff's counter-appeal.

 

1714 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Madden


Crim. No. 3372


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


98 Cal. App. 3d 249; 159 Cal. Rptr. 381; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2269



October 30, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 211771-1, Simon Marootian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed as to appellant Levi Williams. As to appellant Bernice Madden, the conviction is affirmed but the case is remanded for reconsideration of whether appellant should be committed to CRC under the provisions of Welfare and Institutions Code section 3051.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions entered by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California) for sale of heroin, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352(a), and the true finding of an allegation under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.07(a)(2) of one-half ounce or more. One of the defendants was also convicted of being a felon in possession of a pistol, Cal. Penal Code § 12021.


OVERVIEW: In appealing their convictions for sale of heroin, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352(a), and the true finding of an allegation under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.07(a)(2) of one-half ounce or more, defendants argued that the mandatory prohibition of probation required by § 1203.07(a)(2) constituted cruel and unusual punishment in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Cal. Const. art. I, § 17. The court found that the ban on probation was not so disproportionate as to be unconstitutional where the offense was a serious one, California law had not singled out heroin sellers for disproportionate punishment where probation was also denied by law to a broad range of offenders, and 12 other states denied probation under similar circumstances. Thus, the court affirmed the convictions, but found that the trial court abused its discretion in finding that one of the defendants was not fit for commitment to the California Rehabilitation Center (CRC) under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051 because it applied factors other than the defendant's excessive criminality. The court remanded for the trial court to reconsider the CRC issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of conviction because it found that statutorily mandated prohibition of probation did not constitute cruel or unusual punishment in violation of the United States or California Constitutions. It remanded the case for the trial court to reconsider the question of whether one of the defendants was a fit subject for commitment to the California Rehabilitation Center.

 

1715 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Lafayette v. American Television & Communication Corp.


Civ. No. 42245


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


98 Cal. App. 3d 27; 159 Cal. Rptr. 271; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2251



October 24, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, Nos. 163105, 164575, Thomas F. McBride, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the action is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent herewith. Upon issuance of the remittitur herein the amounts heretofore collected and deposited or escrowed by respondent under the terms of this court's orders dated October 12, 1977, and December 6, 1977, and of the trial court's order dated October 28, 1977, shall become subject to disposition upon order of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipalities sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which denied appellants injunctive and declaratory relief against respondent cable television company and thus refused to enforce appellants' asserted rights to regulate the rates charged by respondent for cable service within appellants' municipal limits.


OVERVIEW: The lower court denied the request for injunctive and declaratory relief filed by appellant municipalities against respondent cable television company in which appellants sought to enforce their rights to regulate the rates charged by respondent within appellants' municipal limits. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the lower court incorrectly construed a contract between respondent's predecessor in interest and the county. The court held that, based on the express terms of the contract, the parties' conduct, and extrinsic evidence, the county's rights under the contract included the right to regulate rates and was not limited to an entitlement to license fees. The court noted that this right passed to appellants when they were later incorporated within the county's geographic area. The court held that the agreement was binding on respondent, and thus appellants' attempts to regulate respondent's rates neither unconstitutionally impaired respondent's contract nor offended public policy. The court remanded for the lower court to determine whether appellants had in fact assumed, retained, and effectively exercised the right to regulate rates.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's denial of injunctive and declaratory relief requested by appellant municipalities against respondent cable television company. The court held that respondent's predecessor in interest and the county had expressly contracted to give the county the right to regulate rates. The court held that these rights passed to appellants upon incorporation and that the agreement was binding on respondent.

 

1716 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Molar v. Gates


Civ. No. 20517


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


98 Cal. App. 3d 1; 159 Cal. Rptr. 239; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2250; 12 A.L.R.4th 1197



October 23, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 279851, Everett W. Dickey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the trial court was correct in holding that plaintiff's right to equal protection was violated under the provisions of both the California and United States Constitutions. The judgment below is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, sheriff, board of county supervisors and others, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which held that defendants' policies that provided minimum security jail facilities to male prisoners, but denied such facilities to female prisoners, violated the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions, and which directed defendant sheriff to end the discriminatory treatment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff female inmate filed petition for a writ of mandate against defendants, sheriff and county board of supervisors, which challenged defendants' practices that provided minimum security jail facilities for male prisoners, but not for female prisoners. Plaintiff contended that defendants only maintained a maximum security jail facility for female prisoners, and maintained both minimum and maximum security facilities, with outside job assignment and other privileges, for male prisoners. Defendants contended that Cal Penal Code §§ 4002, 4021 and 4029 mandated complete separation of men and women in county jails, and that equal treatment required deferral until receipt of funds was provided by the Legislature. The trial court held that defendants had denied female inmates as a class the equal protection of the law as mandated by Cal. Const. art. I. § 7 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The trial court held that defendants' reasons met neither the "compelling state interest" test required by the state constitution, nor the "important governmental interest" test required by the federal constitution. The court affirmed and held that defendants violated plaintiff's equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that plaintiff female inmate's right to equal protection was violated under the state and federal Constitutions. The court held that the trial court's remedy was not void for vagueness, and that defendants sheriff and county board of supervisors were only required to offer the same jail facilities or privileges, and were not required to provide any particular facilities.

 

1717 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Wayne J.


Crim. No. 34440


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


97 Cal. App. 3d 776; 159 Cal. Rptr. 106; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2225



October 18, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. J 544784, William L. Ritzi, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile appealed adjudication and disposition for possession of less than one ounce of marijuana of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), alleging that he was placed on probation in his home for a longer period than an adult would have been for the same offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was charged with possession of less than one ounce of marijuana. At trial, defendant was adjudicated a ward of the court pursuant to the Juvenile Court Law and at disposition placed on probation in his home for a period longer than an adult would have been placed on probation for the same offense. Defendant contended that disposition of placing him on probation in his own home for a period longer than an adult could have been placed on probation for the same offense was unlawful. Affirming the judgment of the lower court, the court held that specific findings on adjudication did not have to be made and placement of a minor on probation in his home involved little more loss of liberty than the duties of a minor living in his home, and such probation could have been for a longer time than an adult could have been sentenced for the same offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court because the Juvenile Court Law did not require specific findings on adjudication and a juvenile could have been disposed of by being placed on probation in his home for a period longer than an adult could have been placed on probation for the same offense.

 

1718 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Atkins v. Kessler


Civ. No. 53937


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


97 Cal. App. 3d 784; 159 Cal. Rptr. 231; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2226



October 18, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 13, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C. 146197, Joseph A. Wapner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant purchaser sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which quieted title to real property in favor of respondent owners, in respondents' action to set aside a treasurer's deed executed in favor of appellant.


OVERVIEW: Respondent owners filed an action to set aside a treasurer's deed executed in favor of appellant purchaser following foreclosure of a street improvement assessment lien on respondents' property pursuant to the provisions of the Improvement Act of 1911, Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 5000 et seq. Respondents did not have actual or constructive notice of the proceedings leading to issuance of the treasurer's deed. The trial court quieted title in favor of respondents. On appeal, the court concluded that although respondents' claim was not filed within the six month period of limitation under Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 6571, the action was not time-barred because respondents did not have notice of the proceedings leading to the application for a treasurer's deed's or appellant's application for a deed. The court found that the notice provisions of § 6571 were inadequate on their face and were not reasonably calculated to clearly and fairly apprise the homeowner of the pending sale proceedings or of the right to redemption. Because the notice was insufficient to meet procedural due process requirements, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court respondent owner's claim was not time-barred under the applicable statute. The court held that the statute was constitutionally defective because it did not provide respondents with sufficient notice of the sale proceedings and their rights to redemption.

 

1719 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Ruiz-Cano v. County of San Diego


Civ. No. 18368


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


98 Cal. App. 3d 803; 159 Cal. Rptr. 602; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2324



October 16, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No 417116, Paul Eugene Overton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a peremptory writ of mandate of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which ordered appellant to reinstate respondent employee to a previously held employment position with respondent, together with associated benefits, and awarded respondent back pay with interest.


OVERVIEW: Respondent employee was hired by appellant county as a mental health assistant. Pursuant to provisions of appellant's charter, respondent was informed that she would have to file proof of United States citizenship or of having filed a petition for naturalization in order to continue to receive compensation. Upon her failure to do so, she was given notice of termination. The trial court granted respondent a peremptory writ of mandate ordering appellant to reinstate respondent to her previously held employment position, together with associated benefits, and awarded her back pay with interest. On appeal, the court affirmed the writ. The court held, contrary to appellant's argument, that respondent's position did not meet either the special public interest or governmental function exceptions to the general rule that U.S. Const. amend. XIV prohibits employment discrimination against aliens. The exceptions apply only if the requirement of citizenship bears a rational relationship to a legitimate state interest. Because of its affirmance of the writ, the court did not address respondent's argument regarding federal preemption in the field of alien admission.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the writ of mandate in favor of respondent employee, holding that appellant county's ban on alien employment as contained in its charter provisions was unconstitutionally discriminatory and could not be upheld under the special public interest or governmental function exceptions.

 

1720 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mullaney v. Woods


Civ. No. 16926


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


97 Cal. App. 3d 710; 158 Cal. Rptr. 902; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2218



October 16, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 24, 1979. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 27, 1979. Mosk, J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 266561, Joseph G. Babich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order denying petition for writ of mandamus) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, mother and her children, appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandamus to compel defendant Director of the California Department of Benefit Payments to set aside his decision finding them ineligible for aid to families with dependent children pursuant to 45 C.F.R. § 232.10.


OVERVIEW: Defendant Director of the California Department of Benefit Payments denied plaintiffs, mother and her children, aid to families with dependent children (AFDC) because plaintiff mother failed to provide the social security numbers (SSNs) for plaintiff children as required by 45 C.F.R. § 232.10. Plaintiff mother cited religious reasons for her refusal to obtain the SSNs. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment denying plaintiffs' petition for writ of mandamus. The interpretation of the phrase "applicant for or recipient of aid" in 42 U.S.C.S. § 602(a)(25) to include the AFDC child was not erroneous. The court held that § 232.10 did not violate the Privacy Act of 1974, 5 U.S.C.S. § 552a, because the act did not apply to disclosure of SSNs required by statute. Equal protection was not implicated because plaintiff children, whose eligibility for AFDC was foreclosed by their failure to furnish SSNs, were not within the class of persons for whom protective payments were authorized. The state's interest in maintaining the fiscal integrity of the AFDC system was sufficiently compelling to counterbalance the infringement of plaintiff mother's free exercise of religion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court denying the petition for writ of mandamus of plaintiffs, mother and her children, because defendant director's denial of aid to families with dependent children benefits was proper under the statute and did not violate the Privacy Act of 1974 or plaintiffs' constitutional rights to equal protection and freedom of religion.

 

1721 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Chula Vista v. Pagard


Civ. No. 18268.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


97 Cal. App. 3d 627; 159 Cal. Rptr. 29; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2208



October 11, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Jan. 17, 1980 (L.A. 31212)
June 25, 1980, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 4th Dist., Div. 1, for reconsideration in light of City of Santa Barbara v. Adamson, 27 Cal.3d 123.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 376624, George A. Lazar, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a pastor and members of his congregation, appealed from a summary judgment in favor of plaintiff city entered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which found that defendants' households constituted a public nuisance and ordered them to come into full compliance with plaintiff's ordinances governing R-1 zones within 60 days from the date of the judgment.


OVERVIEW: As a matter of their religious belief, defendants, a pastor and members of his congregation, lived in 12 communal arrangements in plaintiff city. These religious households violated plaintiff's zoning ordinance because they consisted of more than 3 and up to 20 unrelated people living together. On appeal, the court found that the zoning ordinance did not violate defendants' freedom of religion under U.S. Const. amends. I, XIV or Cal. Const. art. I, § 4. The court reasoned that the ordinance did not encourage or discourage a religious belief or opinion and did not impinge upon a fundamental right. The court found that the zoning ordinance was at most an incidental restraint on a practice of a secular hue that had to be weighed against the interest of plaintiff in protecting the safety and quality of life in its community. Although the court found no constitutional violation, it did conclude that a factual issue existed as to defendants' claim of discretionary, arbitrary treatment of their applications for conditional use permits, and therefore summary judgment on that issue was improper. Therefore, the court reversed and remanded the judgment on that ground only.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of plaintiff city to the extent that it granted summary judgment on the issue of whether plaintiff acted improperly in denying conditional use permits but otherwise affirmed the judgment because it found that the zoning ordinance did not unconstitutionally impinge on the religious freedom of defendants, a pastor and members of his congregation.

 

1722 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan Water District v. Dorff


Civ. No. 56735


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


98 Cal. App. 3d 109; 159 Cal. Rptr. 211; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2259



October 9, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 5, 1979, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 6, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. SP-49129, Steven J. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant executive secretary of member public agency appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) for plaintiff public agency seeking to tax defendant's agency for water use on annexed land. Defendant refused to comply on the ground that Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 5, prohibited ad valorem taxes in excess of one percent on property not subject to such taxation prior to July 1, 1978.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff public agency, which was engaged in procuring water for its member public agencies, filed suit for ad valorem taxes on land annexed against defendant executive secretary of a member agency to certify the resolution to annex the land and submit the certified copy thereof to the governing body of the member agency in order that the annexation be completed. The lower court held that the tax for water usage was constitutional and the territory annexed to a municipal corporation or district was liable to pay its proportionate share of the existing indebtedness of the corporation or district to which it was annexed. Defendant contended on appeal that the tax was unconstitutional, pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 5, because an indebtedness on outstanding bonds existed before the property was annexed. Affirming the judgment of the lower court, the court held that the tax was constitutional and defendant was liable for certifying the resolution and submitting a copy thereof with the ad valorem tax proceeds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court because the ad valorem tax was constitutional and a district was liable to pay its proportionate share of the existing indebtedness of the corporation or district to which it was annexed. Defendant executive secretary was required to make and submit the resolution of the tax on annexed property.

 

1723 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Cooperrider v. Civil Service Com.


Civ. No. 42191


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


97 Cal. App. 3d 495; 158 Cal. Rptr. 801; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2192; 21 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P30,507



October 5, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of defendant and appellant for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 6, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 713672, Byron Arnold, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Cooperrider to recover her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant civil service commission challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted respondent applicant's petition for writ of mandate directing appellant to accept respondent's application for employment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant civil service commission refused to accept respondent applicant's application for employment solely on the basis that respondent did not satisfy the city's one-year residential requirement pursuant to San Francisco Administrative Code § 16.98(a). The trial court held the ordinance unconstitutional in violation of the equal protection clauses of the federal and state constitution. The court affirmed and held that the term "employees" as used by Cal. Const. art. XI, § 10(b), which prohibited chartered cities from requiring that employees be residents of the cities in which they worked, included applicants based on the historical context of the amendment. Thus, the court noted that the ordinance was contravened by Cal. Const. art. XI, § 10(b) and disposed of the appeal. Because the ordinance impinged on two fundamental rights, the right to intrastate travel and the right to apply for public service positions without invidious discrimination, the court further held it unconstitutional as appellant failed to demonstrate a compelling state interest in the classification it made, and was unable to prove that no less intrusive means would have achieved the same result.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision that held a residency ordinance unconstitutional because the city's one-year durational residency requirement violated the equal protection provision of the California Constitution.

 

1724 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Bray


Crim. No. 10522


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


97 Cal. App. 3d 506; 158 Cal. Rptr. 745; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2196



October 3, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition for writ of habeas corpus is granted. For the reasons heretofore stated, petitioner is entitled to be discharged from custody.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parolee sought a writ of habeas corpus for review of the decision of the Department of Corrections (California), which refused to honor his previously-set release date, and extended his parole supervision pursuant to provisions of the amended determinate sentencing law, which set the maximum statutory period of parole for confinement pursuant to parole violation from eighteen months to three years.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner parolee was convicted and sentenced to six months to fourteen years pursuant to the indeterminate sentencing law (ISL). He was paroled in 1976, violated parole and was re-incarcerated in January 1978, pursuant to the newly-operative determinate sentencing law (DSL), which allowed a maximum confinement of six months for parole violations. After the second parole, he violated parole, and again was returned to prison in April 1978. He was paroled a third time in September 1978 with a scheduled parole discharge date in February 1979, since the DSL mandated that he could only be retained under supervision for a maximum of 18 months. However, the parole provisions were amended by 1978 Cal. Stat. ch. 582, p. 2002, which increased the maximum statutory parole violation period from six to twelve months. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus on the grounds of the ex post facto application of the amended DSL. The court granted the application for habeas corpus, and held that the DSL was ex post facto as applied because petitioner had a vested right to be discharged from parole supervision after the 18 month period applicable to ISL offenders paroled under the DSL.


OUTCOME: Petitioner parolee's request for a writ of habeas corpus was granted, and he was held entitled to discharge, because the court ruled that as applied to his situation, the amended parole provisions of the determinate sentencing law had a constitutionally-impermissible ex post facto effect.

 

1725 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Couch


Crim. No. 34126


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


97 Cal. App. 3d 377; 158 Cal. Rptr. 647; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2180



September 28, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-193502, Thomas W. Fredricks, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his motion to quash the search warrant and suppress the evidence and convicted him of possession of marijuana for the purpose of sale.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession of marijuana for the purpose of sale and moved to quash the search warrant and to suppress the evidence contending that untimely return of the warrant invalidated the warrant. The trial court denied defendant's motion, and defendant entered a plea of nolo contendere to the crime charged. On appeal, defendant again challenged the validity of the untimely warrant. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that the return of the warrant one day beyond the maximum set forth in Cal. Penal Code § 1534, though a defect, did not prejudice defendant and did not require suppression of any evidence. The court found that the procedural requirements of Cal. Penal Code §§ 1534, 1537 were essentially ministerial in nature. Therefore, the court concluded that an untimely return of an otherwise validly executed search warrant should result in the suppression of evidence only if the accused demonstrates he was prejudiced by the delay in returning the warrant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and conviction of the defendant because an untimely return of an otherwise validly executed search warrant should result in the suppression of evidence only if the defendant demonstrated he was prejudiced by the delay in returning the warrant, and defendant failed to make any such demonstration.

 

1726 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Pennisi v. Department of Fish & Game


Civ. No. 45646


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


97 Cal. App. 3d 268; 158 Cal. Rptr. 683; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2170



September 26, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. M8596, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commercial fisherman challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which rendered judgment in favor of respondent agency in an action for declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Appellant commercial fisherman faced misdemeanor charges and a civil forfeiture action for having undersized net meshes in violation of Cal. Fish & Game § 8602. Appellant initiated a declaratory relief action seeking judicial interpretation of § 8602 and his rights thereunder. The trial court decided the issues of interpretation and constitutionality against appellant. The court affirmed, holding that the language of § 8602 clearly and unambiguously provided that the length of any net meshes was to be measured by taking four meshes while they were simultaneously drawn together. The court noted that the statute's interpretation was supported by its legislative history as well as contemporaneous and practical construction of the statute. The court ruled that such extrinsic evidence was highly relevant to show the legislative intent underlying the statute and therefore admissible. The court further held that the statute's ambiguity, if any, was dispelled by respondent's contemporaneous construction of § 8062 and, thus, the statute was not found to be unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgement below, holding that the trial court's interpretation of the statute was consistent with the legislative intent and contemporaneous construction and that the statute was not unconstitutional since the language of the statute was clear and unambiguous.

EWAP, Inc. v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 55837


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


97 Cal. App. 3d 179; 158 Cal. Rptr. 579; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2164



September 25, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 17, 1979, and respondents' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied November 21, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 214785, Charles H. Phillips, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the City of Los Angeles, board of police commissioners, police chief, and city attorney, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction to plaintiffs, nine arcade proprietors, restraining the enforcement of Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 103.101(c)(4) and (i) (as amended by Ordinance No. 150,184, approved Oct. 5, 1977).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, nine arcade proprietors, were granted a preliminary injunction restraining the enforcement of Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 103.101(c)(4) and (i) (as amended by Ordinance No. 150,184, approved Oct. 5, 1977). Defendants, the City of Los Angeles, board of police commissioners, police chief, and city attorney appealed. The ordinance required a permit for the operation of a picture arcade, and stated that a permit would be denied if, within the past two years, the applicant, his employee, agent, partner, director, officer, stockholder, or manager had knowingly allowed any act of sexual intercourse, sodomy, oral copulation, or masturbation to be committed in any arcade. The court reversed, and held that the ordinance was an unconstitutional prior restraint in violation of the First Amendment. However, injunctive relief was improper because plaintiffs did not show that they would be irreparably injured by enforcement of the ordinance pending trial. The court held that § 103.101(i), which prohibited closed or concealed booths, was a valid, reasonable regulation of the manner of arcade operation and was not preempted by state law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of a preliminary injunction restraining enforcement of the city ordinance because the denial of a permit to an applicant who had within the past two years knowingly allowed the commission of a sexual act at a picture arcade was an unconstitutional prior restraint in violation of the First Amendment, but the prohibition on closed booths was a valid exercise of the city's police power.

In re White


Crim. No. 4360


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


97 Cal. App. 3d 141; 158 Cal. Rptr. 562; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2159



September 24, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The writ is granted and the Municipal Court of the Fresno Judicial District is directed to either eliminate the fourth condition of probation or modify that condition in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, convicted prostitute, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Fresno County (California) that denied her petition for habeas corpus, seeking freedom from certain conditions of her probation imposed by the municipal court pursuant to her conviction for soliciting an act of prostitution in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(b).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, convicted prostitute, pled guilty to soliciting an act of prostitution in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(b), and the municipal court granted her probation with certain conditions. As a condition of her probation, petitioner was restricted from entering certain "map areas." Petitioner sought to be free from the imposed conditions, asserting that her rights to free speech, association, and right to travel were violated. The superior court denied her petition for habeas corpus, and petitioner challenged the order. The court granted petitioner's writ of habeas corpus because the blanket condition against entering designated areas appeared to be unduly harsh and oppressive and, therefore, unreasonable. The court held that the condition was too broad and unrelated to rehabilitation. The court also held that the condition did not pass constitutional muster because it limited petitioner's freedom to travel. The court directed the municipal court to eliminate the condition or to modify it in accordance with the opinion.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner, convicted prostitute's, writ of habeas corpus that sought freedom from a condition of her probation for her conviction for soliciting an act of prostitution because the condition that restricted petitioner's travel from entering certain "map areas" was unreasonable and unconstitutional. The court directed the municipal court to eliminate the condition or modify in accordance with the opinion.

Green v. Community Redevelopment Agency


Civ. No. 55082


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


96 Cal. App. 3d 491; 158 Cal. Rptr. 126; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2085



August 29, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 18, 1979, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied October 25, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 220116, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant real estate owner challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's motion to intervene in an action against respondent city redevelopment agency that was filed by respondent individuals, which challenged a redevelopment project that affected appellant's property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, the owner of real property within an area targeted for redevelopment by respondent city redevelopment agency, sought to intervene in an action to challenge the redevelopment project, brought pursuant to the Redevelopment Statute, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 860- 870. The action was commenced by publication of the special summons required by the Redevelopment Statute, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 861.1, 863, which set a deadline for the appearance of "any" interested person to appear in the lawsuit. Appellant sought to intervene, pursuant to the general intervention statute, Cal. Civ. Code § 387, after expiration of this deadline. The issue was whether the Redevelopment Statute provided the exclusive method for challenging the validity of redevelopment projects by interested parties, and thus trumped the intervention procedures of Cal. Civ. Code § 387. The court held that the Redevelopment Statute did provide the exclusive procedure to challenge a redevelopment project or plan, rendering Cal. Civ. Proc. § 387 both superfluous and inconsistent with its purposes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying appellant real estate owner's motion to intervene in an action against respondent city redevelopment agency because appellant sought to intervene after expiration of a deadline for appearing in the action set by the statute providing the exclusive method for challenging redevelopment projects.

 

1730 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Montano


Crim. No. 34430


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


96 Cal. App. 3d 221; 158 Cal. Rptr. 47; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2059



August 23, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 340967, Julius A. Leetham, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of conviction of attempted murder of both defendants are affirmed. The case is remanded, however, for resentencing in accordance with the views above expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that convicted and imposed sentences for attempted murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 664 and Cal. Penal Code § 187.


OVERVIEW: Defendants took the victim, and while one remained in his car, the other defendant, together with another accused took the victim down a hill to a vacant lot where they shot him and left him for dead. Defendants were charged with attempted murder, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 664 and Cal. Penal Code § 187. The trial court convicted defendants and sentenced them to various prison terms. Defendant who participated in shooting the victim contended that his crime, if any, was assault with intent to commit murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 217 and that he should be sentenced pursuant to that provision. Defendant who remained in his car contended that the evidence was insufficient to support his conviction, and that his conviction should have been for assault with intent to commit murder. The court affirmed defendants' convictions, but remanded the cases for resentencing. The court held that if defendants were guilty of any attempted murder, they were guilty only of assault with intent to commit murder, which was a form of attempted murder. Thus, the court ruled that punishment should have been imposed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 217.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendants of attempted murder. The court, however, remanded the case to the trial court for resentencing under the provision of the California Penal Code that dealt with assault with intent to commit murder. The court held that if defendants were guilty of any attempted murder, they were guilty of assault with intent to commit murder, which was a form of attempted murder.

People v. Superior Court (Olson)


Civ. No. 21098


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


96 Cal. App. 3d 181; 157 Cal. Rptr. 628; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2055



August 21, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 5, 1979, and the Petition of Real Parties in Interest for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied November 15, 1979.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue commanding the trial court to vacate its "Order granting defendants' motion for summary judgment and in the alternative determining issues without controversy" and to take further proceedings in the case consistent with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed A petition for a peremptory writ of mandate to set aside an order from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents and held that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 17500 et seq, 17200 et seq. violated the First Amendment protection afforded to commercial speech.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner brought charges against respondents for false and misleading advertising under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17500 et seq. and for unfair competition under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17200 et seq. The court held that respondents' free speech rights were not violated and the state could insure that the stream of commercial information flow cleanly as well as freely. Further, the court held that the statutory violations were to be determined by the number of people to whom the misrepresentations were made. A single publication constituted a minimum of one violation with as many additional violations as there were persons who read the advertisement or who responded to the advertisement by purchasing the advertised product or service or by making inquiries concerning the product or service.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner's request for a peremptory writ of mandate and commanded the trial court to vacate the order that granted respondents' motion for summary judgment because respondents' free speech rights were not abridged and because the trial court erred in calculating the number of violations.

San Diego White Truck Co. v. Swift


Civ. No. 18510


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


96 Cal. App. 3d 88; 157 Cal. Rptr. 745; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2044



August 17, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 370177, Ross G. Tharp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant truck company challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied its application for issuance of a writ of execution on property owned by respondent homeowner.


OVERVIEW: Appellant truck company challenged an order denying its application for issuance of a writ of execution on a dwelling house owned by respondent homeowner. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the legislature intended Cal. Civ. Proc. § 690.31 to operate retroactively to include obligations incurred before the effective date of the legislation, and that such application did not unconstitutionally abridge the rights of unsecured creditors. The retroactive application of the dwelling house exemption had a minimal effect, for the rights of creditors in California had always been subject to the homestead. Any reliance by a creditor on the debtor's failure to seek the benefits of that statute was questionable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant truck company's application for writ of execution in case against respondent homeowner because legislation applied retroactively and protected respondent.

 

1733 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Committee against Unfair Interest Limitations v. State


Civ. No. 54679.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


95 Cal. App. 3d 801; 157 Cal. Rptr. 543; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2011



August 2, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. den. Jan. 8, 1980


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 158433, Lester E. Olson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nonexempt lenders, defendant state, defendant attorney general, and intervenor exempt lenders all appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which determined that California's Usury Law, Cal. Const. art. XV, was unconstitutional as applied to "nonconsumer loans" and constitutional in all other respects, and held that the judgment had prospective effect only.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court held that California's Usury Law, Cal. Const. art. XV, was constitutional as applied to all loans. Accordingly, the court reversed the judgment below insofar as it held that California's Usury Law was unconstitutional as to "nonconsumer loans" and affirmed the judgment insofar as it held the law constitutional in all other respects. The court determined that plaintiff nonexempt lenders' equal protection challenge to the subject law was without merit because the validity of Cal. Const. art. XV had been upheld against such an attack by the state supreme court, which was a persuasive and definitive holding that was binding on the instant court. The court then rejected plaintiffs' assertion that the California Usury Law was unconstitutional under the commerce clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, because the usury laws created an unusual burden on and discrimination against interstate commerce, and there were reasonable alternatives available that defendant state had to utilize. The court concluded that there was no violation of the commerce clause, noting that Congress had made it clear that it recognized the validity of state usury laws.


OUTCOME: The court partially reversed the judgment below in order to overturn the holding that California's Usury Law was unconstitutional as to "nonconsumer loans" and affirmed the remainder of the judgment, which had upheld the validity of the law in all other respects. The state usury law, which was recognized by Congress, was not subject to a valid attack under either the equal protection or commerce clauses of the federal Constitution.

People v. McKinney


Crim. No. 30954


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


95 Cal. App. 3d 712; 157 Cal. Rptr. 414; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2005



August 1, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 30, 1979, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 26, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 327714, Laurence J. Rittenband, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment upon counts XVI and XVII is reversed and those counts are ordered dismissed. With respect to count II, we modify the judgment by striking the sentence imposed upon the armed allegation and ordering that McKinney's sentence upon that count run concurrently with his federal sentences. As so modified, the judgment upon count II is affirmed. With respect to counts III, V and VI, we modify the judgment by striking the sentences imposed upon the armed allegations and affirm the judgment upon those counts as so modified. The judgment upon counts I, IV and X is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of the first-degree murder (counts I and IV); kidnapping to commit robbery, with bodily harm (counts II and V); first-degree robbery, with great bodily injury (count III); first-degree burglary of a residence (count VI); conspiring to rob (count X); kidnapping to commit robbery (count XVI); and first-degree robbery (count XVII).


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of an order convicting him of the first-degree murder (count I); kidnapping to commit robbery, with bodily harm (count II); first-degree robbery, with great bodily injury (count III); first-degree murder(count IV); first-degree robbery, with great bodily injury (count V); first-degree burglary of a residence (count VI); conspiring to rob (count X); kidnapping to commit robbery (count XVI); and first-degree robbery (count XVII). Appellant argued that the trial court made several errors in evidentiary rulings and was biased. The court disagreed and upheld the convictions against various challenges to evidentiary rulings. The court reversed appellant's conviction on two counts that were not included in amended indictment. And, the court found error, albeit harmless, where the trial court threatened to fire a defense investigator if he were put on the stand to contradict testimony. Although the investigator was ultimately allowed to testify, the court said that such an order, had it been enforced, was tantamount to ordering that the defense could not put on its defense. The court also found that the punishment of life imprison was properly assessed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment upon counts XVI and XVII because they were not included in the amended indictment. The court affirmed the conviction on counts III, V, and VI. The court modified the sentences by striking the armed allegation and ordering that appellant' sentences to run concurrently with federal sentences. The court also modified judgment on counts III, V, and VI by striking the sentences imposed upon the armed allegations.

Minnick v. Dep't of Corr.


Civ. No. 42545


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


95 Cal. App. 3d 506; 157 Cal. Rptr. 260; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1983; 24 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1809; 20 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P30,233



July 27, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 23, 1979, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied November 8, 1979. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 700048, Byron Arnold, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The appeal purportedly taken on March 4, 1977, is dismissed. The "Judgment And Declaratory Judgment" and the "Permanent Injunction" entered on October 12, 1977, are reversed. The stay order made by this court on November 4, 1977, is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged Superior Court of City and County of San Francisco's (California) judgment that held appellants' affirmative action plan violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7 and 8, and Title VII of Civil Rights Act of 1964, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e et seq., and issued injunction.


OVERVIEW: Appellants adopted an affirmative action plan that intended to provide equal employment opportunities for all persons based on merit and intended to increase the number of female and minority employees. Respondents alleged the plan was unconstitutional. The trial court entered a declaratory judgment in favor of respondents and issued a permanent injunction against appellants. The appellate court held that the plan did not violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV because the preferences were necessary to promote the compelling governmental interest of the proper management of the correctional system. The evidence did not support a conclusion that the plan violated Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7 and 8. The plan did not violate Title VII of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e et seq. because the statute did not prohibit appellants from adopting a race-conscious plan. The permanent injunction was vacated because the plan was constitutional.


OUTCOME: Judgment that appellants' affirmative action plan was unconstitutional and awarding a permanent injunction reversed because the plan was necessary to promote a compelling governmental interest, the state constitution was not violated, and Title VII of the Civil Rights Act of 1964 permitted a race-conscious plan.

People v. Superior Court (John D.)


Civ. No. 46113


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


95 Cal. App. 3d 380; 157 Cal. Rptr. 157; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1970



July 26, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 22, 1979, and the Petition of Real Party in Interest for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 26, 1979.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue compelling the juvenile court to dismiss the section 300, subdivision (c), petition and modifying its order of January 18, 1979, to show that the factual basis of the section 602 petition was found to be true on August 31, 1978, and that the minor was found to be insane at the time of the offense, and presently insane at the hearing of December 12, 1978, and that upon such findings the order of commitment made pursuant to section 702.3 was proper. The order of dismissal made January 17, 1979, is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state requested a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Humboldt County (California) to set aside its order which dismissed a petition to have minor, a real party in interest, declared a ward of the juvenile court under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 and which sustained a petition to declare minor a dependent child of the court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 300(c).


OVERVIEW: Minor, a real party in interest, was convicted of second degree murder, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, and then found legally insane, both at the time of offense and at the time of the hearing. Respondent lower court granted a petition filed by minor's parents under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 300(c) to have minor declared a dependent child of the court, ordered minor placed in a hospital, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 702.3, and dismissed a petition under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 to declare minor a ward of the court. Upon review, the court vacated respondent's order dismissing the § 602 petition and directed respondent to issue a peremptory writ of mandate compelling the juvenile court to dismiss the petition under § 300(c). The court found that the finding of not guilty by reason of insanity on a § 602 petition did not deprive the juvenile court of the power to make a valid commitment order under § 702.3. Further, under § 702.3, minor could be committed until he was sane, because minor was convicted and then found not guilty by reason of insanity. Minor's commitment was not intended to punish minor but to protect society.


OUTCOME: The court vacated respondent lower court's order dismissing the Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 602 petition and granted the state's request for a writ of mandate to compel the juvenile court to dismiss the Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 300(c) petition. The court also found that the finding of not guilty by reason of insanity on a § 602 petition did not deprive the juvenile court of the power to make a valid commitment order under Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 702.3.

Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three

July 24, 1979

DISPOSITION: The judgment (the order sustaining the demurrer without leave to amend) is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, individuals and association, sought review of an order entered in the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which dismissed without leave to amend appellants' complaint against respondent attorney general, which alleged that the process of public employers reviewing criminal offender record information which included entries of arrest that did not result in conviction violated state and federal statutes and Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, individuals and association, filed a complaint against respondent attorney general, which alleged that if a public employer, authorized by law to receive criminal offender record information, but prohibited by law from considering a record of an arrest which did not result in a conviction, is sent a record containing any entries of arrest which did not result in convictions, this procedure violated state and federal statutes and Constitutions.
