In re Carson


Crim. No. 19424


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


95 Cal. App. 3d 123; 157 Cal. Rptr. 19; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1915



July 23, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Applications for a Hearing by the Supreme Court were Denied September 20, 1979.



DISPOSITION: The writ of habeas corpus is denied, and the order to show cause is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus which complained that the Community Release Board (California), had fixed his sentence contrary to the state's new determinate sentence law under Cal. Penal Code § 1170.2(a). Petitioner contended that the board erred in its enhancement of petitioner's sentence for first degree robbery by one year for being armed with a deadly weapon. Petitioner claims error because only an accomplice was armed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted under the indeterminate sentence law, for a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211(a), first degree robbery. The Community Release Board enhanced petitioner's sentence by one year under the state's new determinate sentence law, Cal. Penal Code § 1170.2(a), for being armed with a deadly or dangerous weapon. The record showed that only an accomplice of petitioner's was armed. Petitioner brought a writ of habeas corpus and complained that the board had fixed his sentence contrary to statute. Petitioner contended that under the statute, the "being armed" enhancement provision only applied if petitioner himself was personally armed. The court denied the writ of habeas corpus and held that § 1170.2(a), authorized such enhancement if petitioner was armed as specified in Cal. Penal Code § 211(a), prior to July 1, 1977. Prior to that date § 211(a), provided that all robbery perpetrated by a person being armed with a deadly weapon was robbery in the first degree. Judicial precedent also provided that if any one of a group of perpetrators was armed, all were deemed to have been armed and were guilty of first degree robbery. Therefore the enhancement was proper.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's writ of habeas corpus and found that the Community Release Board properly enhanced petitioner's sentence for first degree robbery by one year for being armed with a deadly weapon because statute provided that all robbery perpetrated by being armed with a deadly weapon was in the first degree, and if any of a group of perpetrators was armed, all were deemed armed and guilty of first degree robbery.

 

1739 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Reeves v. City of Burbank


Civ. No. 55138


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


94 Cal. App. 3d 770; 156 Cal. Rptr. 667; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1936



July 3, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C113111, Bruce Geernaert, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant municipality appealed a decision from the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) in favor of plaintiff property owner in an action concerning the constitutionality of a municipal ordinance. The court declared that the ordinance was arbitrary, capricious, and unconstitutional. The court also awarded plaintiff costs and attorney fees pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 800. Defendant appealed the award of attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Defendant municipality appealed a decision in favor of plaintiff property owner in an action concerning the constitutionality of a municipal ordinance. The trial court held that the ordinance was arbitrary, capricious, and unconstitutional, and awarded plaintiff costs and attorney fees available under Cal. Gov't. Code § 800. Defendant appealed from only that portion of the judgment awarding plaintiff attorney fees. On appeal, the court held that Burbank, Cal., Municipal Code § 7-33, subd. (c)(3) prohibited the relocation of any building which was over 15 years old. The ordinance was applied to plaintiff's application for relocation and resulted in a denial of the application. The court held that application of the ordinance constituted arbitrary or capricious action by a public entity, and allowed imposition of attorney fees pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 800. The denial of plaintiff's residential-structure-removal application fell within the concept of arbitrary and capricious conduct, as conduct not supported by a fair or substantial reason or as a stubborn insistence on following an unauthorized course of action. The statutory fees were proper.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in an appeal by defendant municipality from a decision in favor of plaintiff property owner in an action alleging that a municipal ordinance was unconstitutional. The court concluded that the imposition of statutory attorney fees upon defendant municipality was proper.

 

1740 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bailey v. Loggins


Civ. No. 44424.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


95 Cal. App. 3d 872; 156 Cal. Rptr. 654; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 2017



June 29, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 11, 1979 (S.F. 24076)
Mar. 25, 1981, case will be reheard when the court has its full complement of seven members.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 73331, Richard M. Silver, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prison officials sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) which issued a writ of mandate requiring the State Department of Corrections to enact censorship rules and to allow respondent inmate to publish articles in a prison newspaper under Cal. Penal Code §§ 2600, 2601.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prison officials refused to publish certain articles in the prison newspaper. After his three administrative appeals were denied, respondent inmate petitioned for writ of mandate. The trial court granted the petition and ordered appellants to permit the publication and to enact guidelines for censorship and prompt review. Appellants challenged the order, asserting respondent's failure to exhaust administrative remedies and the overbreadth of the order. The court affirmed, finding that respondent had used every available administrative remedy before seeking judicial relief. It ruled that appellants' guidelines for publication were overly broad because they conferred too much discretion on prison officials to suppress criticism and controversial views which presented no threat to the security interests of the prison. The trial court properly ordered appellants to enact new guidelines limiting censorship pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 2600, 2601 (c) and to set new rules for expeditious review of censored articles.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment issuing a writ of mandate directing the corrections department to publish the censored articles and to enact administrative guidelines for censorship and expeditious review of censored articles. First Amendment guarantees limited appellant prison officials' ability to control the content of prison publications.

 

1741 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Gunn v. Employment Development Dep't


Civ. No. 54622


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


94 Cal. App. 3d 658; 156 Cal. Rptr. 584; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1895



June 29, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 119148, Frank P. Kearney, J. *


* Retired judge of the municipal court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment under appeal is reversed for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion and for the purpose of the trial court hereafter determining, on appropriate motion therefor, the amount of fees to which petitioner is entitled under Code of Civil Procedure section 1021.5 for her attorney's services, both at the trial and the appellate level.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant appealed from a judgment by the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) which denied her petition for writ of mandate to direct respondents, California Employment Development Department and California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, to set aside the determination and decision of each of them that she was ineligible for unemployment insurance benefits.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimant was denied unemployment benefits by respondents, California Employment Development Department and California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, because she would not provide information regarding her pregnancy via a standard health questionnaire. Petitioner had provided respondents with a doctor's certificate indicating that she was in good health and could work. Petitioner's request for writ of mandate to set aside respondents' decision was denied. The court reversed. The court held that, under Cal. Const. art I, § 1, inquiries into petitioner's privacy had to be conducted in the least intrusive manner possible. The court held that the insistence by respondents on learning about petitioner's ability to work as a result of her pregnancy via the questionnaire prior to ruling on her eligible for benefits was constitutionally unjustified because petitioner's doctor statement provided the information they sought in a minimally intrusive manner and was prima facie proof of petitioner's eligibility for benefits. The court held that petitioner qualified for attorney's fee under the private attorney general theory codified in Cal Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment. The court held that respondents, California Employment Development Department and California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, did not inquire into petitioner claimant's ability to work while pregnant in a minimally intrusive manner because a doctor's statement provided the same information respondents sought via a standard health questionnaire. Respondents' behavior was constitutionally unjustified.

 

1742 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kate' School v. Department of Health


Civ. No. 3973


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


94 Cal. App. 3d 606; 156 Cal. Rptr. 529; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1889



June 28, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 22, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 213360-1, Hollis G. Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the superior court with directions to issue its writ remanding the cause to the Department with directions to the Department to reconsider its order in light of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant health department appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California) which directed the issuance of a peremptory writ ordering defendant to set aside its license revocations and to reconsider the denial of applications for additional licenses for plaintiff school.


OVERVIEW: Based on regulations prohibiting certain behavioral modification techniques involving the infliction of corporal punishment, defendant health department revoked plaintiff school's license as a community care facility for autistic and schizophrenic children and refused its applications for additional licenses. After the trial court directed an issuance of a peremptory writ which ordered defendant to set aside the license revocation and reconsider the applications, defendant appealed. In reversing, the court held that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 9 § 152 and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 22 § 80341, which defined and prohibited corporal punishment, were not vague and unenforceable and did not violate the constitutional rights of the children or their parents. The court further determined that the motive or intent of plaintiff in imposing corporal punishment on the children did not make its actions permissible under those sections. Additionally, the court concluded that in view of the trial court's specific findings of fact that certain acts amounting to corporal punishment took place, the judgment was based on the erroneous assumption that such acts were not proscribed by §§ 152 and 80341.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's judgment entered in favor of plaintiff school because the regulations which prohibited plaintiff's use of corporal punishment for behavior modification were not vague and unenforceable. Moreover, the trial court's findings of fact violated the regulations.

 

1743 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


R. E. Spriggs Co. v. Adolph Coors Co.


Civ. No. 52115


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


94 Cal. App. 3d 419; 156 Cal. Rptr. 738; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1871; 1979-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P62,764



June 26, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 23, 1979, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 6, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No C869998, Lester E. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to make findings on the issue of liability consistent with this opinion and to try the issue of damages.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted judgment, on appellant's cross-complaint, for appellee in appellant's action alleging a combination in restraint of trade and seeking damages under the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 16700 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Upon appellant's termination of a distribution agreement, appellee brewery sought injunctive relief against appellee's continued sales of its beer. Appellant cross-complained alleging a combination in restraint of trade and sought damages under the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 16700 et seq. The trial court held for appellee. On appeal, the court reversed since the trial court erred in failing to find as a matter of law that the factual circumstances surrounding appellee's imposition of territorial restraints were determined in prior actions. Appellee was collaterally estopped from relitigating the question whether it imposed territorial restraints upon appellant in order to facilitate a price fixing scheme. The court reversed with directions to try the issue of damages.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for appellee in appellant's action alleging a combination in restraint of trade because the court erred in failing to find as a matter of law that the factual circumstances surrounding appellee's imposition of territorial restraints were previously determined, and appellee was barred from relitigating the matter.

 

1744 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Ocean View Teachers Assn. v. Board of Trustees


Civ. No. 20051


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


94 Cal. App. 3d 294; 156 Cal. Rptr. 308; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1859



June 20, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 6, 1979, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 15, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 273523, Robert P. Kneeland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, teachers association and a teacher, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that denied a writ of mandate to compel respondent school district to pay certificated associate teachers the difference between the salary they received under a staffing exemption under 1970 Cal. Stat. ch. 966 and the salary received for a full-time certificated teacher as required by Cal. Educ. Code §45028.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to 1970 Cal. Stat. ch. 966, respondent school district had a salary schedule for associate certificated teachers not on the basis of years of training and experience, as provided in Cal. Educ. Code §45028, but by job specification. The associate teachers were subject to a lesser salary because they were assigned by respondent to different work conditions from full-time certificated teachers. Appellants, teachers association and a teacher, asserted that certificated teachers worked beyond the minimum school day provided in the Cal. Educ. Code and were full-time teachers pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code 45024, and ch. 966 did not allow a different salary structure as to full-time employees. The court held that ch. 966 was applicable because §45024 allowed a longer minimum day and associate teachers worked a minimum five hour day while full-timers worked a seven and one-half hour day. The court also rejected appellants' argument that the ch. 966 exemption was applicable only if one salary schedule for all teachers was implemented based upon job classification, as this defeated the legislative intent of ch. 966 that allowed flexible salary schedules to meet educational goals.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that denied appellants, teachers association and a teacher, a writ of mandate to compel respondent school district to pay certificated associate teachers the difference between the salary they received under a staffing exemption and that of the salary of full-time certificated teachers because the different salaries were proper under the legislative scheme.

 

1745 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vasquez


Crim. No. 8827


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


94 Cal. App. 3d 42; 156 Cal. Rptr. 235; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1834



June 13, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 29, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 40810, Roy G. Fitzgerald, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the municipal court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The cause is remanded for modification of abstract of judgment to give Vasquez credit for time served presentence. The judgment as modified is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of first degree robbery, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, and found that he used a firearm in the commission of the offense in violation of § 12022.5. Appellant contended there was prejudicial error in instructing the jury and in failing to give him good time/work time credit for his presentence detention.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree robbery, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, with a finding that he used a firearm in the commission of the offense in violation of § 12022.5. When appellant was sentenced he was not given good time/work time credit for his presentence detention. Appellant contended the trial court erred in not providing him good time/work time credit and in instructing the jury to consider his consciousness of guilt from an inconsistent pretrial statement and from evidence of flight after the offense. Appellant sought review and the court affirmed the convictions, finding that the jury instructions were appropriate because the evidence indicated appellant made a pretrial statement to a police officer concerning his alibi that differed from his in court testimony and the testimony of a witness clearly indicated appellant fled the scene after the offense. The court determined that such evidence could be considered by the jury in considering appellant's consciousness of guilt. The court modified appellant's sentence to allow good time/work time credit for his presentence detention. The court construed Cal. Penal Code §§ 2900 and 4019 to allow such credit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of appellant for first degree robbery with the use of a firearm, finding that the trial court was correct in instructing the jury on his consciousness of guilt from a prior false statement and in fleeing after the crime. The court modified appellant's sentence to grant him good time/work time credit for his presentence detention.

 

1746 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Fox v. Federated Department Stores, Inc.


Civ. Nos. 53040, 53041


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


94 Cal. App. 3d 867; 156 Cal. Rptr. 893; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1946



June 13, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 10, 1979, and the Petition of Appellants in Reuben v. Union Oil Company and Fox v. Mobil Oil Corporation for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 15, 1979. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 992893, C 992897, C 992899, C 992901, C 992903, C 992905, C 992907, C 992909, C 996035, C 996037, C 996039, William H. Levit, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, plaintiffs in class action suits challenging the validity of various charge account practices by defendants, appealed the decisions entered in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which vacated the interlocutory class certification order entered on one of the actions, dismissed the complaints in all of the actions, and entered judgment in favor of defendants.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs brought class action suits against defendant retail department stores and defendant gasoline companies challenging the validity of their various charge account practices. Defendants charged from 1 percent to 1 1/2 percent per month on plaintiffs' unpaid balances after certain periods of time. The per month charges exceeded the 10 percent rate of interest used in the state constitutional provision relating to usury. The trial court vacated the interlocutory class certification order entered in plaintiffs' action against one of the gasoline company defendants, dismissed the complaints in all the actions, and awarded judgment to defendants. Plaintiffs appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The revolving charge accounts were validly classified as subject to the time-price doctrine. They were not loans or forbearance of money and thus not subject to the usury laws. Defendants' credit card sales were within the meaning of the Unruh Act, Cal. Civ. Code, 1801 et seq., which the court upheld as constitutional. Because there was no cause of action against defendant gasoline company, the trial court did not err in vacating the class certification order in that action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision that defendants were entitled to judgment as a matter of law because defendants' credit card sales were not subject to state usury laws. In light of the fact that plaintiffs could state no cause of action against any of defendants, the trial court's order vacating plaintiffs' class certification in its action against one of defendants was not erroneous.

 

1747 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Sunnyside Nurseries, Inc. v. Agricultural Labor Relations Bd.


Civ. No. 41657


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


93 Cal. App. 3d 922; 156 Cal. Rptr. 152; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1824



June 11, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 10, 1979, and Petitioner's Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 22, 1979. Bird, C. J., did not Participate therein.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Agricultural Labor Relations Board is annulled insofar as it relates to the charged violations of Labor Code section 1153, subdivisions (b) and (c); it is affirmed insofar as it relates to Labor Code section 1153, subdivision (a). The sanctions of the order are set aside. The Board's motion to quash service of, and its motion to dismiss, Sunnyside's petition to this court for relief are denied. The cause is remanded to the Board for such further proceedings, and sanctions, if any, as are in accordance with law and not inconsistent with the views we have expressed. The parties will stand their respective costs of this review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer sought review of a decision of respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board (California), which determined that petitioner was guilty of unfair labor practices, pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 1153(a), (b), and (c).


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest was a labor union that engaged in a campaign to enlist in its membership agricultural workers of petitioner employer. After the election, some employees who voted in favor of union membership were discharged. Respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board ruled in favor of a complaint by real party in interest and adjudicated petitioner guilty of committing unfair labor practices. Petitioner sought review and reversal of respondent's order. The court determined that petitioner did not interfere with the voting rights of its employees by expressing its own views and arguments against union membership. The court also held that dismissal based on seniority was proper and fair and found that the layoffs were motivated by economic reasons. The court reversed the findings of respondent on those issues. However, the court found that petitioner made promise of benefit or threat of reprisal to some employees regarding the outcome of the election, thereby interfering with employees' rights contrary to Cal. Lab. Code § 1153(a). The court affirmed this part of respondent's decision. The court set aside the sanctions in the order and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the administrative order in part, holding that petitioner employer did not interfere with the administration of union membership and that the layoffs by petitioner, carried out according to seniority, were not unfair. However, the court affirmed the determination that petitioner interfered with the exercise of union membership rights by its employees.

 

1748 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Olson v. Cory


Civ. No. 53608.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


93 Cal. App. 3d 942; 156 Cal. Rptr. 127; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1825



June 11, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 15, 1979 (see 26 C.3d 672)
Superseded by Olson v. Cory, 26 Cal. 3d 672, 27 Cal. 3d 203, 27 Cal. 3d 532, 164 Cal. Rptr. 217, 178 Cal. Rptr. 568, 609 P.2d 991, 636 P.2d 532, 1980 Cal. LEXIS 154 (Mar. 27, 1980)
Related proceeding at Staniforth v. The Judges' Retirement System, 2014 Cal. App. Unpub. LEXIS 3513 (Cal. App. 4th Dist., May 19, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 000437, Ernest J. Zack, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant controller sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), holding that Cal. Gov't. Code § 68203, which placed a five percent cap on cost-of-living increases on judicial salaries and pensions of respondent judges and judicial pensioners was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant controller sought review of a decision that found Cal. Gov't. Code § 68203, which placed a five percent cap on cost-of-living increases on judicial salaries and pensions of respondent judges and judicial pensioners, was unconstitutional. The court reversed the judgment of the lower court. The court held that the cap on cost-of-living increases for judicial salaries and pensions was valid except to the extent that it purported to eliminate a salary increase for respondent sitting judges based on the cost of living during the current year. Respondent judicial pensioners were to continue to receive pensions tied to the current judicial salaries. As to future salary increases, the court held that because the salaries and pensions were set by public law rather than by contract, no officer or employee had an immutable vested right to any specific salary. Failure to receive an increase of salary did not constitute a reduction such as would trigger the Cal. Const. art. III, § 4 ban on the reduction of salaries of state officers during their term of office.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, holding that the cap on cost-of-living increases for judicial salaries and pensions was valid except to the extent that it purported to eliminate a salary increase for respondent sitting judges based on the cost of living during the current year. That interest was already vested because respondents had worked a substantial part of the year in reliance of the promise of a cost-of-living raise.

 

1749 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Younger v. Bd. of Supervisors


Civ. No. 16912


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


93 Cal. App. 3d 864; 155 Cal. Rptr. 921; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1818



June 7, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 1, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 386229, Benjamin W. Hamrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Supervisors sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which awarded summary judgment to respondent attorney general. The trial court held that San Diego County, Cal., Charter § 14.1 was invalid under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 4, because only the state could limit the number of terms that an elective county officer could serve.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Board of Supervisors sought to implement San Diego County, Cal., Charter § 14.1 after its approval by the voters. Respondent attorney general sought a declaratory judgment that § 14.1 was invalid under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 4, because only the state could limit the number of terms that an elective county officer could serve. The trial court awarded respondent summary judgment, and appellant challenged the ruling. The court affirmed, concluding that the charter provision was unconstitutional because it was an act in excess of appellant's authority. The court reasoned that the state had retained authority over elective county officers' qualifications and tenure because those aspects were not mentioned in Cal. Const. art. XI, § 4(f), whereas those aspects were specifically granted to charter counties for nonelected employees under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 1(b). The court held that a limitation on the terms that elective county officers could serve could not qualify as a "removal" provision within appellant's powers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of summary judgment granted to respondent attorney general, giving it declaratory relief against appellant Board of Supervisors. The court concluded that appellant's newly enacted charter provision imposing a restriction on the eligibility of elected county officials to seek reelection for the same office was unconstitutional as an act in excess of a charter county's authority.

 

1750 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Committee to Defend Reproductive Rights v. Myers


Civ. No. 45066.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


93 Cal. App. 3d 492; 156 Cal. Rptr. 73; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1786



May 29, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Sept. 20, 1979 (S.F. 24069)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 741710, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant organizations challenged an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), authorizing the implementation of state legislation that restricted funding for certain elective abortions. Appellants contended that such restrictions violated the Social Security Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, health care rights persons and organizations, brought a complaint to challenged implementation of the state's budget act of 1978, which restricted the circumstances under which public funds would be authorized for elective abortions for indigent women. Prior to the passage of the act, funds were available generally for all abortions. The court determined that the act did not infringe on the equal protection of the qualified recipients because the restrictions on the abortion funding were rationally related to the state's strong interest in protecting the potential life of the fetus, and in encouraging normal childbirth. However, it ruled that the act violated the Social Security Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396 et seq. The court held that the state act would not fund certain abortions, which would otherwise be funded under the federal scheme, where severe and long-lasting physical health damage to the mother would result if a pregnancy were carried to term. The court reversed and remanded with directions that implementation of the act be enjoined only to the extent necessary to comply with the federal statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court to the extent necessary to conform to federal law. The court held that the state legislation was invalid to the extent that it prohibited state reimbursement for certain abortions that would qualify for federal reimbursement.

 

1751 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


County of Ventura v. Castro


Civ. No. 54214


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


93 Cal. App. 3d 462; 156 Cal. Rptr. 66; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1784



May 25, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 19, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. D88353, Roland N. Purnell, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION:  The order denying defendant's motion to set aside the judgment is reversed and the cause remanded with directions to enter an order granting defendant's motion setting aside the agreement for the entry of judgment, and the judgment. Costs on appeal are awarded to appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which denied defendant's motion to set aside a child support order and paternity decree that was based on an agreement between defendant and respondent county. Defendant argued that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11476.1 deprived defendant of due process of law because it authorized entry of judgment without notice and hearing, or adequate waiver thereof.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered into an agreement with the district attorney's office for respondent county, which provided for entry of a child support order and paternity decree for a child born out of wedlock. Six months later, defendant moved to set aside the agreement and judgment pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 473, but the court denied it. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to vacate the agreement and judgment. The court noted that a judgment entered pursuant to a confession is constitutional only if the confession constitutes a valid waiver of the debtor's due process rights. Defendant was not advised that he could be subject to criminal prosecution for failure to support the child as ordered, nor that the existence of a judgment establishing paternity would be admissible as evidence on that issue in any subsequent support action. Because the statute did not contain adequate safeguards to assure that defendant's rights to due process were properly waived, the court held that the agreement and judgment entered pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11476.1 was constitutionally defective.


OUTCOME: The court held that the statute authorizing entry of paternity judgments and child support orders without notice and hearing, or adequate waiver thereof, was unconstitutional. Therefore, the order denying defendant's motion to set aside the judgment was reversed and the cause remanded with directions to enter an order granting defendant's motion setting aside the agreement for the entry of judgment, and the judgment.

 

1752 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Estrada


Crim. No. 14181


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


93 Cal. App. 3d 76; 155 Cal. Rptr. 731; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1749



May 17, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 12, 1979. Bird, C. J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 58411, J. Barton Phelps, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder, Cal. Penal § 187, while armed with a deadly weapon with a blade longer than five inches.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, while armed with a deadly weapon with a blade longer than five inches. In reviewing the grand jury selection, the court concluded that in the absence of a cognizable group at who unconstitutional practices are directed, no improprieties could exist. The court continued that the less educated, low income, blue-collar workers, and young adult groups were not cognizable. The court determined that the search warrant met the probable cause standard because the affidavit contained sufficient foundational circumstances to support its validity. The court also found that defendant was not denied the right to a reasonable examination of prospective jurors because the trial court conducted extensive and pertinent questioning. The court found no violation of any reasonable expectation of privacy because defendant failed to show that he had reason to believe his communications were confidential and would remain private and there was no deprivation of rights of an incarcerated person, which were not justified by a legitimate penological objective. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of first degree murder while armed with a deadly weapon with a blade longer than five inches. The court held that in absence of a cognizable group at who unconstitutional practices were directed, which the less educated, low income, blue-collar workers, and young adults were not, no systematic intentional discrimination in the selection of the grand jury could exist.

 

1753 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Gluck v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 54193


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


93 Cal. App. 3d 121; 155 Cal. Rptr. 435; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1751; 5 Media L. Rep. 1605



May 17, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 15, 1979. Newman, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 210462, Charles H. Phillips, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the superior court is reversed. The court is directed to enter a new order denying plaintiff's motion for preliminary injunction except to the extent that the motion seeks to enjoin application of section 1210, 1211, 1212, and 1213 of Los Angeles County Ordinance No. 11516. The court is further directed to issue its preliminary injunction restraining enforcement of those sections of the ordinance. Each party is to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant County of Los Angeles sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that restrained and enjoined display restrictions and impounding provisions in a previously invalid news rack ordinance that had been more narrowly drawn. The preliminary injunction was granted in response to the claims of plaintiff news rack operator that the ordinance was constitutionally defective and preempted by state law.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff news rack operator sought to enjoin enforcement of a narrowly drawn news rack law enacted by defendant, County of Los Angeles. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction that restrained enforcement of display restrictions and enjoined impounding provisions in the ordinance. On appeal, the court held that the ordinance did not violate the free speech and press guarantees in U.S. Const. amend. I, and was not preempted by state law. The display provisions that restricted sexually explicit materials were specific, and not indefinite, because dissemination was allowed, but displays that were used intentionally to exploit sexually explicit material to a "captive" public were limited. Because the "captive" viewer problem was a significant local interest that could differ between localities, the validity of the local ordinance was favored against an attack of state preemption. The enforcement provisions that permitted impounding were a constitutionally defective scheme to force payment of fees irrespective of the propriety of news rack seizure. Accordingly, the order was reversed, with directions for a preliminary injunction to issue as to the enforcement provisions.


OUTCOME: The preliminary injunction that restrained the display and enforcement provisions in the ordinance enacted by defendant County of Los Angeles was reversed except with respect to the impounding provisions. The display provisions represented constitutionally valid time, place, and manner regulation of protected speech and press and were not preempted by state law. The constitutionally invalid enforcement provisions were properly enjoined.

 

1754 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wong


Crim. No. 32540


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


93 Cal. App. 3d 151; 155 Cal. Rptr. 453; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1752



May 17, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 19, 1979. Bird, C. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. Z-858491, Joseph Kauffman, Judge, Joseph Ruffner, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The application for the appointment of counsel at public expense is denied. The case is retransferred to the appellate department for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the appellate department of the superior court (California) that denied his request for appointment of counsel at public expense. Defendant sought representation to appeal a decision that found defendant guilty of a misdemeanor traffic violation for failure to possess proper vehicle registration.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was an indigent who sustained a municipal court imposition of a $ 65 fine relative to a traffic violation and the failure to appear on that violation. Defendant appealed to the superior court and sought appointment of counsel at public expense on that appeal. The superior court denied the request, and the court ordered the matter transferred under Cal. R. Ct. 62 for consideration. The court determined that defendant was not constitutionally entitled to counsel at the public expense on appeal from a misdemeanor traffic offense, which carried with it a $ 65 fine and penalty. The court ruled that, while counsel at the public expense was a right on appeals in felony cases and in misdemeanor cases resulting in sentences of imprisonment, such a right to counsel on appeal did not exist on misdemeanor cases where the fine imposed was not substantial. The court reasoned that the state was not constitutionally required to protect against every consequence of indigency, and that such a right to counsel would result in a flood of appeals that would be beyond the capacity of the courts to handle.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that defendant, who was an indigent, did not have a constitutional right to counsel at the public expense on his appeal from the imposition of a fine and penalty totaling $ 65 on his conviction for a misdemeanor nonmoving traffic offense because the state was not required to provide for every consequence of indigency, and such a right would result in more appeals than the courts could administer.

 

1755 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Adoption of D. S. C.


Civ. No. 19870


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


93 Cal. App. 3d 14; 155 Cal. Rptr. 406; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1745



May 15, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 12, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. AD-15218, H. Walter Steiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which entered a judgment declaring defendant's minor child free from his custody and control, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232 (a)(4), on the basis of defendant's present and past criminal conduct.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed a petition for adoption of a minor and later filed a petition to declare the minor free from the parental control of defendant under Cal. Civ. Code § 232(a)(1), (a)(4). The trial court found against defendant under § 232(a)(4) as defendant's felony conviction proved his parental unfitness and because, under Cal. Civ. Code § 4600, awarding custody to plaintiffs would serve the child's best interests. Defendant argued that there was insufficient evidence to show that his felony rendered him unfit and that § 232(a)(4) was unconstitutional as applied because there was no nexus between his criminal conduct and his parental fitness. The court affirmed. The court held the inferences, which the trial court drew from the evidence were sufficient to establish a reasonable relationship between defendant's crime and his ability to parent and that the trial court properly considered § 232(a)(4) in conjunction with § 4600 and made findings as to future harm and best interests of the child, which were neither arbitrary nor capricious, thereby, making the application of § 232(a)(4) to defendant's behavior not unconstitutional as applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because the evidence was sufficient to support the inferences that the trial court drew regarding defendant's propensity for violent crime, his potential to turn to crime in the future, and his concomitant unfitness as a parent.

People v. Thygesen

Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


93 Cal. App. 3d 895; 156 Cal. Rptr. 212; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1821

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 13, 1979, and Appellants' Petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 1, 1979.

OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed their convictions of offering to sell or lease subdivided land without written notification from the Real Estate Commissioner, receiving prorated payments for taxes under a real property sales contract, and failure to place the amounts in a trust fund or to disburse money without consent of payors or other persons holding an encumbrance on the property. The court determined that defendants were not deprived of either their due process rights or their rights to a speedy trial, the trial court properly instructed the jury, and the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's convictions.

 

1757 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Anderson v. Dep't of Real Estate


Civ. No. 54049


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


93 Cal. App. 3d 696; 155 Cal. Rptr. 307; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1803



May 11, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 23 and May 30, 1979. Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 19, 1979. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. SP 48412, Steven J. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Department of Real Estate, challenged a ruling of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which overturned appellant's revocation of appellee real estate agent's license.


OVERVIEW: Appellee real estate agent held an inactive real estate license. Appellee conducted a business through which he ascertained available residential rental units, compiled a list of said units, and advertised and sold the list for a fee. This conduct put appellee in the class of advance fee rental agent and, as such, required an active real estate license under regulations implemented by the Real Estate Commissioner. Because appellee violated this requirement, appellant, Department of Real Estate, revoked appellee's inactive real estate license. Appellee challenged that ruling and, on appeal, the trial court reversed and held that the conduct at issue was not cause for discipline. Appellant challenged the trial court ruling. On appeal, the court affirmed on constitutional grounds. The court held that the regulation at issue, while valid on its face, failed to bear a rational relationship to a valid governmental purpose. Because only licensed agents could provide the information provided by appellee, their was a disincentive to provide consumers with such information. The court found that the licensure requirement violated constitutional commercial speech protections.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court ruling, which overturned the revocation of appellee real estate agent's license by appellant, Department of Real Estate. The court affirmed the trial court ruling on constitutional grounds and found that certain licensure requirements implemented by the Real Estate Commissioner violated appellee's rights to commercial speech.

Call v. Feher


Civ. Nos. 17524, 17699


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


93 Cal. App. 3d 434; 155 Cal. Rptr. 387; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1781



May 10, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Placer County, Nos. 46347, 49720 William A. Newsom, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed. Plaintiffs' motion for attorney's fees on appeal is granted and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to determine reasonable fees for services performed on behalf of plaintiffs on appeal and to add such fees to the appropriate judgment. (See Code Civ. Proc., § 1021; Sts. & Hy. Code, § 6615; American City Bank v. Zetlen (1969) 272 Cal.App.2d 65, 69 [76 Cal.Rptr. 898]; T. E. D. Bearing Co. v. Walter E. Heller & Co. (1974) 38 Cal.App.3d 59, 65 [112 Cal.Rptr. 910]; 6 Witkin, Cal. Procedure (2d ed. 1971) Appeal, § 587, subd. (2), pp. 4518-4519.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged rulings of the Superior Court of Placer County (California), which granted appellee bond holder summary judgment on its action to foreclose the lien on appellants' property and dismissed appellants' cross-claims against appellee, Regional Planning Agency, for declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Appellee bond holder filed an action against appellant landowners to foreclose the lien of a street improvement bond which appellee bond holder owned after appellants failed to pay the related assessments. The parcel owned by appellants was sold after the tentative subdivision map expired and after appellee, Regional Planning Agency, passed an ordinance which prohibited the subdivision of appellants' parcel. Appellants therefore filed a cross-complaint against appellee agency and sought a declaration that the affected property had a vested right to effect a subdivision. The trial court granted appellee bond holder summary judgment on her foreclosure action and sustained appellee agencies' demurrer to appellants' cross-complaint without leave to amend. Appellants challenged both ruling. On appeal, the court affirmed both rulings of the trial court and held that not only had appellants failed to exhaust administrative remedies prior to filing their vested rights action, no such action was supported prior to final map approval. On appellee bond holder's claim, summary judgment was proper where no consensual relationship existed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court ruling, which granted appellee bond holder summary judgment on its action to foreclose the lien on appellant landowners' property and dismissed appellants' cross-claims against appellee, Regional Planning Agency, for declaratory relief. The court held that appellants' cross-claim was unsupported where final map approval had not occurred, and summary judgment on appellee bond holder's action was proper.

Kellis v. Ring


Civ. No. 54086


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


92 Cal. App. 3d 854; 155 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1724



May 9, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 5, 1979. Mosk, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 199642, Jack T. Ryburn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of dismissal) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant assignee of a limited partnership interest brought an action in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) alleging that respondent general partner violated his fiduciary duty to appellant, Cal. Corp. Code § 15519 of the Uniform Limited Partnership Act. The superior court sustained respondent's demurrer and dismissed the action. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant assignee of a limited partnership interest brought an action against respondent general partner in the partnership. Appellant sought declaratory relief as to the parties' rights and duties and whether respondent violated his fiduciary duty to appellant. Defendant's demurrer was sustained and the action dismissed on the ground that appellant had no standing. The court affirmed. The court held that while under Cal. Corp. Code § 15519 of the Uniform Limited Partnership Act, appellant had a right to receive his share of profits or other property the assignor would have been entitled to, appellant lacked standing to interfere in the management of the partnership as an assignee and not a substituted limited partner. The court reasoned that a contrary result could allow a person rejected as a limited partner to defeat the desires of the limited or substitute partners by becoming an assignee. The court held that the issue of the constitutionality of § 15519 was not properly raised below because appellant did not raise the issue prior to the order of dismissal, and where appellant did not allege loss of property, there existed no unconstitutional taking, U.S. Const. amend XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's order dismissing appellant assignee's claim against respondent general partner for declaratory relief and breach of fiduciary duty. The court held that under the Uniform Limited Partnership Act, appellant, as assignee of a limited partnership interest, lacked standing to interfere with the partnership's management.

 

1760 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Tante v. Board of Administration


Civ. No. 55008


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


93 Cal. App. 3d 615; 156 Cal. Rptr. 53; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1796



May 9, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 5, 1979.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public employee filed a proceeding in mandate to require respondent retirement administration to approve petitioner's application to retire on October 1, 1979, with retirement benefits. At the direction of the Supreme Court of the State of California, the court granted an alternative writ.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner public employee became a miscellaneous member in a public employees' retirement system on June 16, 1975. Cal. Gov't Code § 20981 provided that such members who became 67 years old on or after October 1, 1973, had to retire on the first day of the following month after turning 67. Section § 20981 was later amended to change the retirement age to 70. Petitioner turned 67 years old on September 10, 1979. Cal. Gov't Code § 20393 provided that employees credited with five or more years of service were eligible for retirement benefits, while those with less than five years of service were, upon termination of state employment, entitled to the return of contributions to the retirement system plus interest. Petitioner filed a proceeding in mandate to require respondent retirement administration to approve petitioner's application to retire on October 1, 1979, with retirement benefits. The court denied the petition because petitioner had no vested interest in the pension.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner public employee's request for a writ of mandate to require respondent retirement administration to approve petitioner's application to retire on October 1, 1979, with retirement benefits. Petitioner was subject to the terms of the applicable statute. He could retire on October 1, 1979, and receive the return of his contributions plus interest, or he could complete five years of service and receive a pension.

 

1761 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Zamora


Crim. No. 32868.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


92 Cal. App. 3d 614; 155 Cal. Rptr. 109; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1705



May 2, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. on den. rehg. May 31, 1979; Hg. granted July 12, 1979 (See 28 C.3d 88)


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 31546058, Michael T. Sauer and Mary E. Waters, Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's convictions for battery against a police officer and resisting a police officer in the discharge of his duties were reversed by the appellate division of a California superior court. The court of appeals ordered that the case be transferred to it to determine whether sanctions against the prosecution were appropriate for destruction of evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's convictions for battery against a police officer and resisting a police officer in the discharge of his duties were reversed and the case was remanded to the appellate court to settle issues pertaining to the propriety of assessing sanctions against the prosecution. The people denied defendant discovery of citizen complaints against police officers because the police department had destroyed all complaints that were not sustained. The court held that the principal question was whether sanctions should have been imposed because of the destruction of the complaint files, and if so, whether the appropriate sanction should have been dismissal of the charges. The court concluded that sanctions were appropriate because destruction of the complaints deprived defendant of a fair trial. On remand defendant would be entitled to have the jury instructed that the officers used excessive or unnecessary force against citizens on whatever number of occasions was equal to the number of incidents for which complaint files were improperly destroyed. The court held that the destruction of the documents was not justified on the grounds that prior approval of the city council was obtained.


OUTCOME: After defendant's convictions for battery against a police officer and resisting a police officer were reversed, the appellate court concluded that sanctions against the prosecution were appropriate for destruction of citizen complaints against police officers. On remand defendant was entitled to have the jury instructed that the officers used excessive or unnecessary force against citizens on a certain number of occasions.

People v. Firstenberg


Crim. No. 33892


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


92 Cal. App. 3d 570; 155 Cal. Rptr. 80; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1702



May 1, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 17, 1979, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 25, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 31550806, Alan G. Campbell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The cause is remanded for a new sentencing hearing. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a decision of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him, following a jury trial, of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1290 by commingling patients' funds with his own which, in turn, is a violation of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 22 § 72557.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was the licensee of a skilled nursing facility under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1250. He was convicted of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1290 by commingling patients' funds with his own. Appellant was sentenced to jail for 90 days and a fine was imposed. Appellant's trial was due to an inspection of his facility and its books by a health official. Appellant sought review, contending that the warrantless health department inspection of the business records of his nursing home was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction because the administrative search was reasonable within the meaning of the U.S. Const. IV. The court held that such inspections were crucial to the effective oversight of the physical well-being of patients, to ensure that they were not neglected or even abused. The court, however, remanded the case for a new sentence because of appellant's age and health, and because he had been on bail for two years.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in part and reversed in part. The court affirmed appellant's conviction for commingling his patient's funds, holding that the administrative inspection was constitutional based on the nature of the industry. The court, however, remanded the case for resentencing because of appellant's age and health, and because he had been on bail for two years.

People v. Davis


Crim. Nos. 17453, 17463, 18007, 18036, 18257, 18419


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


92 Cal. App. 3d 250; 154 Cal. Rptr. 817; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1673



April 24, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 65013, 63938, 67009, 67554, 67353 and 66133, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied defendant's constitutional challenge to state statutes that regulate cocaine.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgments from the lower court because it found that defendant's due process and equal protection rights had not been violated when they were convicted under Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11350, 11351 for the unlawful possession, possession for sale, and sale of cocaine. The court held that the statutes were constitutional because the state legislature was authorized to classify cocaine as a narcotic and thus proscribe penalties for the possession and sale of cocaine. The court held that there was substantial evidence to show that the legislative classification had a rational basis and therefore did not violate the equal protection clause. The court held that there was sufficient evidence to show that cocaine presented a substantial threat to health and welfare and that the criminalization of the personal use and possession of cocaine in the home did not infringe on defendants constitutional right to privacy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions of defendants for statutory violations for the possession, sale, and use of cocaine because it found that the legislature had a rational basis for the criminalization of the drug and the statutes did not violate defendants right to privacy.

Rico v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


Civ. No. 55630.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


92 Cal. App. 3d 208; 154 Cal. Rptr. 692; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1669



April 23, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Hg. den. July 19, 1979, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14  [**1] Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for writ of mandate from an order from a trial court (California), which denied defendant's relatives' petition for writ of mandate to direct a magistrate to permit them to deposit their real property in lieu of cash or a bail bond to secure defendant's pretrial release, after defendant was charged with involuntary manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with involuntary manslaughter. Defendant's relatives applied for permission to post an interest in real property in lieu of bail. A trial court denied the relatives' petition for writ of mandate directing a magistrate to permit the deposit of their real property interest. Defendant filed a petition for writ of mandate from the trial court's order. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the trial court to accept the relatives' posting of an instrument of security in real property to secure defendant's appearance and obtain his release. The court construed Cal. Penal Code § 1298 as permitting the giving of security in the form of an interest in real property by others on behalf of defendant equally with the posting of the real property security by defendant personally. The court conceived of no rational basis which justified a distinction between pretrial release based upon defendant's posting an equity in his own real property, and the posting by others of their equity in the same form of property. Although protection of the licensed bail bond business protected a significant public interest, the classification involved was not a rational one.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate from a trial court's order denying defendant's relatives' request to deposit their real property interest to secure defendant's pretrial release because the California Penal Code permitted the giving of security in the form of an interest in real property by others on defendant's behalf. There was no rational basis justifying a distinction between defendant's posting equity and the posting by others.

Simac Design, Inc. v. Alciati


Civ. Nos. 43005, 43027


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


92 Cal. App. 3d 146; 154 Cal. Rptr. 676; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1663



April 20, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 14, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 389617 and 390295, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in Simac is reversed.

The judgment in Trumpp is modified to compel the city to act on respondents' application for approval of their subdivision maps in a manner consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, residential association, sought review of two judgments from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted a writ of mandate that ordered respondent city to issue building permits or to act upon the application for subdivision map approval for respondent developers.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the lower court judgment for respondent, developer because the lower court erred when it ordered respondent city to issue building permits that were in violation of a new city ordinance. The ordinance prohibited respondent city from issuing a building permit to developer because the ordinance had changed the requirements of the issuance of building permits and did not allow for a building permit unless a final map had been approved for development allotment prior to June 30, 1977. The court found that respondent did not receive final map approval until July 6, 1977 and that the lower court erred when it ordered the building permits in violation of the ordinance. The court modified the judgment regarding respondent, developer and ordered respondent city to act on the application for approval of respondent's subdivision maps. The court found that respondent city had refused to review respondent's maps on the ground that a temporary restraining order and preliminary injunction had been ordered in the preceding case. The court found that under the ordinance, respondent city was required to act on an application for map approval.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of respondent developer because the lower court erred when it ordered respondent city to issue building permits in violation of a new city ordinance. The court modified the judgment for the second respondent developer and ordered respondent city to act on the application for map approval in accordance with the new ordinance.

Tinsley v. Palo Alto Unified School Dist.


Civ. No. 43478


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


91 Cal. App. 3d 871; 154 Cal. Rptr. 591; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1634



April 13, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 8, 1979, and the Petitions of the Defendant and Appellant and the Defendants and Respondents for a Hearing by the Supreme Court were Denied June 7, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 206010, Lyle R. Edson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case is remanded with leave to petitioners to serve and file, within 30 days after this decision is final, an amended petition, substituting or adding the involved children as petitioners, and deleting the evidentiary matter the subject of the special demurrer upheld by the trial court. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, parents of elementary school children, challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which sustained demurrers to their petition seeking relief from respondent elementary districts, on the ground that the petition did not properly allege acts or omissions, intentionally segregative or otherwise, requiring interdistrict relief.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, parents of elementary school children, appealed from the trial court's judgment sustaining demurrers to their amended petition, and sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent elementary districts to submit a feasible plan to eliminate racial segregation in the districts' schools. The court reversed the trial court's judgment, holding that the children themselves were proper parties plaintiff, but that the defect was curable and petitioners should have been allowed to amend the petition to add the children as parties. The court held that government action produced the school district lines which determined the racial imbalance, and if those lines created or maintained an imbalance which deprived minority groups of their right to equal educational opportunities, then there was an equal protection violation under Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, regardless of whether state authorities intended the district lines to segregate, and petitioners should have been permitted to attempt to establish the feasibility of correcting the alleged interdistrict segregation. The court remanded for a determination of whether there was segregation entitling the pupils to interdistrict relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment sustaining respondent elementary districts' demurrers to petitioner parents' amended petition, and remanded with leave to petitioners to amend, substituting or adding the involved children as petitioners, because the state constitution required respondents to alleviate segregation created by the maintenance of arbitrary school district boundaries if reasonably feasible.

 

1767 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gonzales


Crim. No. 8832.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


91 Cal. App. 3d 853; 154 Cal. Rptr. 442; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1633



April 12, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Hg. den. June 14, 1979, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art VI, § 14  [**1] Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 41458, Benjamin W. Hamrick, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions entered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) for two charges of lewd or lascivious act on a child under 14, Cal. Penal Code §288, and two charges of participation in oral copulation by a person over 21 with another person under 16 years of age, Cal. Penal Code §288a(b)(2).


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of two charges of lewd or lascivious act on a child under 14, Cal. Penal Code §288, and two charges of participation in oral copulation by a person over 21 with another person under 16 years of age, Cal. Penal Code §288a(b)(2). Defendant was found to be a mentally disordered sex offender and was committed to a mental hospital pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 6316.1 for the maximum term of imprisonment which could have been imposed for the underlying offenses. Defendant argued the trial court erred in denying his motion to compel the prosecuting witnesses--two nine-year-old girls--to submit to psychiatric examinations. In affirming the convictions, the court held that where the trial court had an opportunity for full appraisement of the girls and where defendant conceded that what they testified to could have happened, it was not an abuse of discretion for the trial court to deny the motion. The court also held that defendant waived any objection he had to evidence of prior offenses by him against the girls by cross-examining them and that the maximum term provision of § 6316.1 met the strict scrutiny standard and was therefore not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for two charges of lewd or lascivious act on a child under 14 and two charges of participation in oral copulation by a person over 21 with another person under 16 years of age because it found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant's motion to compel the prosecuting witnesses to submit to psychiatric examinations and that it properly admitted evidence of prior offenses.

 

1768 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bruno v. Bell


Civ. No. 43677


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


91 Cal. App. 3d 776; 154 Cal. Rptr. 435; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1623



April 11, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified April 17, 1979. A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 8, 1979, and Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 7, 1979. Bird, C. J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 463863-4, Robert H. Kroninger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The portion of the judgment appealed from is reversed with directions to enter judgment for defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state financial officers, sought review of that portion of a judgment by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which awarded attorney fees to plaintiff attorney in conjunctions with his successful prosecution of an action that resulted in a declaration that Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 104.10 was invalid because it conflicted with Cal. Const. art. XXVI.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff attorney filed a petition for writ of mandate in the trial court seeking a declaration that Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 104.10 was unconstitutional. The trial court granted summary judgment to plaintiff, ruled that § 104.10 was unconstitutional, enjoined further distribution of the funds, and awarded attorney fees to plaintiff. The court, in reversing the trial court on the issue of attorney fees, held that plaintiff was not entitled to an award of attorney fees. There was no statutory or contractual provision providing for the award of attorney fees. The common fund doctrine did not apply because no identifiable sum of money was created, preserved, or recovered. The substantial benefit rule, as a basis for awarding attorney fees, was not applicable because a substantial benefit was not conferred on an ascertainable class. Plaintiff's representation did not meet the tests of the private attorney general theory because the strength or societal importance vindicated by the decision was minor, and plaintiff's stake in the matter far exceeded that of other taxpayers. It was improper for plaintiff to have been a party litigant and to have claimed entitlement to attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the portion of the judgment, which found that a revenue statute was unconstitutional, that awarded attorney fees to plaintiff attorney. Plaintiff was not entitled to attorney fees because there was no statutory provision or contractual agreement authorizing such fees, and the common fund doctrine, the substantial benefit rule, and the private attorney general theory did not apply.
