In re Davis


Crim. No. 33599.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


91 Cal. App. 3d 640; 154 Cal. Rptr. 330; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1606



April 5, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 30, 1979 (Crim. 20999)
June 18, 1980, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 2d Dist., Div. 1, for reconsideration in light of People v. Sage (1980) 26 Cal.3d 498.
Opn. filed July 3, 1980, not for publication.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus after the Superior Court for Los Angeles County (California) denied his motion for good time and work participation credits pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5, which allowed a credit against a term of imprisonment for days of custody, including days credited to the period of confinement pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4019.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant entered a plea of guilty to burglary, and was sentenced to the middle term of 2 years in state prison, which included credit for 152 days in custody. Defendant filed a motion for good time and work participation credits for the period of his presentence incarceration in county jail under Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5, which allowed a credit against a term of imprisonment for days of custody, including days credited to the period of confinement pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4019. The trial court denied the motion, and defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus with the court. The court dismissed the petition, holding that defendant was not within the class defined by § 4019(a) to receive behavior credits, as he was a state prison inmate. In addition, the court rejected defendant's equal protection claim on the ground that defendant was entitled to behavior credits under Cal. Penal Code § 2931 if he could establish entitlement, and that there was no constitutional requirement that state prison and local facilities inmates be treated with precise uniformity for the purposes of allocating potential behavior credits.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner inmate's petition for writ of habeas corpus because, as a state prison inmate, he was not entitled to behavior credits under the applicable statute for the period of his incarceration in county jail. The court also found that there was no merit to petitioner's equal protection claim.

 

1770 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Crest Brokerage Co. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 52116


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


91 Cal. App. 3d 635; 154 Cal. Rptr. 338; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1605



April 5, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C171823, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city and county, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiff importer after plaintiff filed a complaint for recovery of 1975 ad valorem property taxes paid under protest.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff importer filed a complaint for recovery of ad valorem property taxes for 1975 which it paid under protest to defendants, city and county. The trial court granted plaintiff's motion for summary judgment and awarded the taxes paid plus interest. Defendants appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that defendants' collection of the 1975 ad valorem taxes was in violation of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 226, which clearly provided for prospective-only application of a 1976 U.S. Supreme Court decision overruling previous cases preventing states from imposing nondiscriminatory ad valorem property taxes on imported goods until the goods lost their character as imports and became incorporated into the mass property of the state. In so holding, the court noted that the issue of constitutionality of the statute had already been decided in a previous case. Because the taxes paid by plaintiff under protest and sought to be refunded were for 1975, a year that fell within the time period when such taxes were declared unconstitutional, plaintiff was entitled to a refund as a matter of law. Thus, the court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to plaintiff importer because defendants, city and county, had attempted to retroactively apply the holding of a U.S. Supreme Court decision regarding ad valorem property taxes even though the California legislature had, via statute, clearly declared that the case would be applied prospectively only.

 

1771 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gray


Crim. No. 18154


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


91 Cal. App. 3d 545; 154 Cal. Rptr. 555; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1598



April 4, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified April 18, 1979. A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 4, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 95969, William E. Mullins, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Defendant argues that the identical reasoning applies here since the Legislature has defined and prescribed the punishment for a more specific offense, namely, Penal Code section 217 pertaining to assault with intent to commit murder. Defendant's argument is consistent with the well known rule by which a specific legislative enactment controls a more general one. We agree that here, Penal Code section 217 precluded his charge and conviction pursuant to the general attempt statute, Penal Code section 664. The People, relying on People v. Meriweather, 263 Cal.App.2d 559, 563 [69 Cal.Rptr. 880], contend that defendant was properly charged pursuant to Penal Code section 664. They concede that no other case has decided this question but rely on Meriweather which held that the offense of assault with intent to commit murder defined and punished by Penal Code section 217 is distinct from attempted murder (Pen. Code, §§ 187, 664). We hold that defendant's basic sentence should have been [***2] based on Penal Code section 217 rather than Penal Code sections 187 and 664; accordingly, the judgment is affirmed, except for the sentence; the trial court is directed to resentence defendant pursuant to Penal Code section 217.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction of attempted murder, with firearm and great bodily harm by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California). Defendant contended that Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7 did not apply to attempts, that the state's closing argument was prejudicial, that the admission of his statements violated U.S. Const. amend. VI, and that he was punishable under Cal. Penal Code § 217, not Cal. Penal Code § 664.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of attempted murder, with the use of a firearm and great bodily injury. For purposes of sentencing, his prior conviction, assault with a deadly weapon coupled with use of a firearm capable of being concealed, was considered. The court affirmed defendant's conviction of attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code § 664 and great bodily injury under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7 because attempted murder was a separate, distinct crime and not included in § 12022.7. The court ruled that the state made fair comment on the defense evidence and that the jury was properly instructed on defendant's right not to testify. The court found that defendant was properly advised and voluntarily waived his Miranda rights and that he had no per se right to counsel in the pre-indictment setting. The court reversed and remanded because defendant's sentence was excessive and incorrectly based on Cal. Penal Code § 664, the attempted murder statute. The court ruled that defendant was to be sentenced for assault with intent to commit murder under Cal. Penal Code § 217, an offense which was statutorily distinct from attempted murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and held that defendant's conviction of attempted murder was proper, that he was not denied his right to counsel during the pre-indictment process, and that the state made fair comment on the defense evidence. The court reversed and remanded for resentencing because defendant should have been sentenced under the assault with intent to commit murder statute, not the attempted murder statute.

 

1772 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Dawn v. State Personnel Board


Civ. No. 16328


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


91 Cal. App. 3d 588; 154 Cal. Rptr. 186; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1602; 19 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1030; 20 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P30,007



April 4, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 8, 1979. Bird, C. J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 260863, Frances N. Carr, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff male employee appealed from denial of a writ of mandate by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) in his action against respondent State Personnel Board for the selection of a woman over him for promotion when both were ranked within the top three competitors for the position.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff male employee applied for a promotion and took an examination. A female employee also applied for the promotion and took the same examination. Both had considerable experience and were ranked as qualified for the position. The regulations required that one of the top three competitors be selected for the promotion. Both plaintiff and the female employee were within the top three. The woman was selected for the position, and both she and plaintiff were held by respondent State Personnel Board to have been equally qualified. Plaintiff appealed, alleging that his gender was the reason why he was not selected, and the woman was selected, for the promotion. The court affirmed, holding that the regulations required choosing one of the top three competitors who took the same examination, and selection of the woman over plaintiff did not discriminate against him based on gender.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of respondent State Personnel Board because both plaintiff male employee and the female employee, selected for the competitive promotion, were equally qualified and plaintiff was not denied the position because of his gender.

 

1773 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Willamette Industries v. Franchise Tax Board


Civ. No. 17853


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


91 Cal. App. 3d 528; 154 Cal. Rptr. 183; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1595



April 3, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 256385, Fred W. Marler, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed summary judgment granted defendant Franchise Tax Board by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) holding the franchise tax for a partial year was properly increased and plaintiff was not entitled to a refund.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer brought an action for a tax refund against defendant Franchise Tax Board, alleging that it was unconstitutional for defendant to charge plaintiff a franchise tax based on a higher percentage of revenue for a short period of time when a short tax return was filed because plaintiff changed its fiscal year. Plaintiff objected to paying the higher rate because the annual rate of taxation was changed for the short period when the short return was filed because of the change in plaintiff's fiscal year. Defendant was granted summary judgment because the tax rate for the short period was in fact the higher tax rate, and the increase in the tax rate was constitutional. The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment, because plaintiff was in fact required to pay the higher rate for the short period because that was the rate in effect at the time.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, because plaintiff taxpayer was in fact required to pay the tax at the rate covering the short period during which plaintiff filed a short return based on a change in its fiscal year.

 

1774 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Lackner v. Perkins


Civ. No. 16757


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


91 Cal. App. 3d 433; 154 Cal. Rptr. 138; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1585



April 2, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 17, 1979, and Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 30, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 386673, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and remanded for further proceeding consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state department of health director sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted respondent nursing facility's motion for judgment on the pleadings and declared the penalty scheme set forth in the Long-Term Care, Health, Safety and Security Act of 1973, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1417 et seq., unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state department of health director, filed a complaint against respondent nursing facilities to collect a civil penalty for alleged violations of the Long-Term Care, Health, Safety and Security Act of 1973 (act), Cal. Health & Safety Code §1417 et seq. The trial court granted respondent's motion for judgment on the pleadings and declared the act unconstitutional in that the penalty scheme set forth in Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 1424, 1428(b) deprived respondent of due process. Appellant challenged the decision. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the act did not violate due process as the penalties were not binding and respondent had a free choice to accept a minimum fine or go to hearing or to pay the fine after the decision or pay the fine assessed by the hearing officer which could be a modified decision or have the whole matter decided by a judge. Moreover, the court found that the act was not unconstitutionally vague and it did not provide for an unconstitutional delegation of authority.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment because the penalty scheme under which respondent nursing facility was cited by appellant state department of health director did not violate due process as the penalties imposed were not binding and the respondent had options in responding to the penalties.

Tsingaris v. State of California


Civ. No. 44387


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


91 Cal. App. 3d 312; 154 Cal. Rptr. 135; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1574



March 30, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 18, 1979. Delucchi, J., * was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 30, 1979. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 218054, Melvin E. Cohn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimants sought relief from an order of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which denied appellant's petition for relief from lateness in the filing of a claim under the Government Tort Claims Act on the grounds that it was filed beyond the 100-day limitation period contemplated by Cal. Gov't Code § 911.2.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimants filed a claim against respondent government approximately six and one-half months after a probationer broke into their home, injured one of them, and killed their infant son. The court denied appellant's petition for relief from the 100-days filing requirement of Cal. Gov't Code § 911.2. On appeal, the court affirmed, rejecting appellant's contention that their ignorance of the claims statute and of the existence of possible causes of action against governmental entities should have been held to excuse compliance with the requirement that claims be timely filed. The court held that a compelling showing of excusable neglect was not made unless the claimant used reasonable diligence for the purpose of discovering the facts, which appellant did not do. The court also rejected appellant's claim that § 911.2 as applied to them was an unconstitutional deprivation of equal protection, as that contention was contrary to governing authority.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying appellant claimant's petition for relief from lateness, holding that appellant had not made a compelling showing of excusable neglect in the late filing of the claim against respondent government.

 

1776 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wiener


Crim. No. 8676


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


91 Cal. App. 3d 238; 154 Cal. Rptr. 110; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1568



March 29, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 16, 1979, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 24, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 35294, Roy G. Fitzgerald, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the municipal court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of seven counts of possessing obscene matter with intent to distribute it to others, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 311.2, 311.9, along with six prior convictions of violating Cal. Penal Code § 311.2, and sentenced him to prison consecutively on two counts.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possessing obscene matter with intent to distribute it to others, in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 311.2 and 311.9, and six prior convictions of violating § 311.2. He appealed based on his contention that § 311.2 was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. I, § 2, he was deprived of equal protection due to grand jury indictment instead of a preliminary hearing, and the prosecution failed to present expert evidence that the materials appealed to the prurient interest of a deviant sexual group. The court affirmed and held that the subject material fell into the general definition of obscene matter for which no expert testimony was required; in fact, no evidence beyond merely showing the materials was required. Moreover, defendant waived this argument by his opening statement that none was required. Since prior case law had squarely upheld the constitutionality of § 311.2, the court summarily dismissed that contention. Finally, the court held defendant's contention he was deprived of equal protection by the grand jury indictment process was not available because his guilty plea predated the judicial decision in support of his argument.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence on seven counts of possessing obscene matter with intent to distribute it to others because there was no reversible error in the absence of expert testimony establishing deviant group prurient interest, defendant was not deprived of equal protection by the grand jury indictment, and the consecutive sentences did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.

 

1777 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Santibanez


Crim. No. 3223


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


91 Cal. App. 3d 287; 154 Cal. Rptr. 74; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1572



March 29, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, No. 18481, J. Kelly Steele, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which convicted him of participating in an act of oral copulation while in the county jail in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a(e).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for participating in an act of oral copulation while confined in jail in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a(e). Defendant contended that § 288a(e) was unconstitutional in that it violated his fundamental right of sexual privacy, denied him equal protection of the law, and imposed cruel and unusual punishment. The court affirmed, holding that § 288a(e) did not violate a fundamental right, as defendant did not have a reasonable expectation of sexual privacy in jail. The court ruled that defendant was not denied equal protection of the law because punishing prisoners for consensual oral copulation while permitting adults outside the jail to engage in it, did not discriminate on the basis of a suspect classification. The court held that § 288a(e) was rationally related to the governmental purpose of maintaining prison discipline and order as homosexual contacts in jail could lead to violent altercations. Finally, the court held that § 288a(e) did not impose cruel and unusual punishment because oral copulation was not a status offense for which criminal punishment was prohibited and the punishment was not shockingly disproportionate to the crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for participating in an act of oral copulation while in jail. The court held that defendant did not have a reasonable expectation of privacy as to sexual behavior in jail, that he was not denied equal protection of the law because the law was rationally related to the purpose of maintaining prison discipline and order, and that the punishment was not cruel and unusual.

United Business Com. v. City of San Diego


Civ. No. 16731


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


91 Cal. App. 3d 156; 154 Cal. Rptr. 263; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1562



March 28, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 356994, Alfred Lord, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, on-premises sign owner and potential sign owner, sought review of a declaratory judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which held that defendant municipality's on-premises sign ordinance and its amendments were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant municipality enacted an on-premises sign ordinance that included subsequent amendments, which imposed various requirements on signs in the commercial and industrial zones within the city, required the payment of a sign inventory license fee by on-premises sign owners in those zones, and required the abatement of non-conforming signs throughout those zones within seven years. Plaintiff sign owner was one of those billed for the sign inventory fee under the original ordinance. Plaintiffs then filed a complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief. After the ordinance was amended by defendant, plaintiffs amended their complaint and contended that the sign inventory license fees imposed by the amended ordinance was an impermissible exercise of the defendant's police power used to impose a tax to generate revenue that was not authorized by defendant's charter. The trial court held the ordinance and amendments constitutional. The court affirmed and held that the defendant's amended ordinance, which included the imposition of reasonable sign inventory fees, was a valid exercise of defendant's police power as a charter city under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 7.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and held that the defendant city's ordinance was a valid exercise of defendant's police power. The court held that the sign inventory fee was a regulatory license fee that did not violate the constitutional right of freedom of speech, nor denied equal protection of the laws to owners or users, and afforded substantive due process to those persons affected, and that the amortization scheme for nonconforming signs was reasonable.

People v. Brown


Crim. No. 18320.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


90 Cal. App. 3d 1018; 153 Cal. Rptr. 762; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1547



Mar. 27, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 30, 1979 (Crim. 20988)
June 18, 1980, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 3, for reconsideration in light of People v. Sage (1980) 26 Cal. 3d 498 
Opn. filed June 27, 1980, not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 65044, Martin N. Pulich, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied defendant good time credit for the time served in the county jail prior to his felony conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted on his plea of guilty to voluntary manslaughter and was sentenced. The trial court found that defendant had been held in the county jail for 228 days prior to sentencing and pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5, was granted credit for the time served. However, the trial court refused to grant the request of defendant for an additional 32 days credit for good time served at the jail, to which he claimed he was entitled pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4019. The prosecution argued that Cal Penal Code § 4019 applied only to those ultimately convicted of a misdemeanor. Thus, the defendant was ineligible. The defendant argued that the rationale of the prosecution would result in an equal protection violation of the constitution. The court agreed with the defendant, finding that defendants ultimately convicted of felonies were entitled to good time for the time spent in the county jail prior to their felony convictions. The court affirmed the judgment, but remanded the case to the trial court to recalculate the credit time served by defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, but remanded the cause to the trial court to re-calculate the good time credit in order to give defendant credit for the time served in the county jail prior to his conviction.

People v. Woolsey


Crim. No. 11017


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


90 Cal. App. 3d 994; 153 Cal. Rptr. 746; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1545



March 26, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 24, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-41550, William T. Low, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which was entered upon his plea of guilty to receiving stolen property. The guilty plea was entered after defendant's motion to suppress evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to receiving stolen property after the trial court denied his motion to suppress evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5. Defendant appealed the judgment against him, claiming that the trial court erred in failing to suppress evidence obtained as a result of an illegal search. The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the search fell under the voluntary consent exception to the warrant requirement. The discussion between the investigating officer and defendant occurred during regular business hours, and the officer did not threaten defendant with a forcible entry of his premises to permit the inspection. In affirming the judgment, the court concluded that the environment in which defendant's conversation with the officer occurred was not inherently coercive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against defendant for receiving stolen property because the trial court did not err by refusing to suppress evidence seized as the result of a lawful search conducted under the voluntary consent exception to the warrant requirement.

 

1781 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Mears


Civ. No. 43653


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


90 Cal. App. 3d 885; 153 Cal. Rptr. 566; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1536



March 23, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 704479, Paul E. Springer, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter remanded to the trial court with directions to calculate the inheritance tax for the entirety of the two trust corpora, excluding from that calculation the trusts' bank deposits of $ 5,384.14.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Controller of the State of California challenged a decision by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which held unconstitutional Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 13851 in an action by respondent inheritor, who challenged the tax on his inheritance of intangible personal property.


OVERVIEW: Respondent inheritor inherited the corpora of two revocable trusts which consisted of shares of stock, government bonds and notes, and a bank account, that the decedent, a resident of Mexico and citizen of the United States, held in California. Because Mexico did not impose inheritance tax on the property, an inheritance tax referee determined that tax was due on the intangible personal property pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. § 13851. Respondent objected and the probate court ruled that no tax was due because the statute was unconstitutional. The court reversed and held that the statute did not deny respondent equal protection of the law because the statute had a reasonable objective of taxation by at least one jurisdiction but avoiding double taxation, and treated members of the same class in the same way. The court held that the statute was valid because the legislature could make any conditions it chose on exemptions because the right to make a testamentary disposition of property was not an inherent right or a right of citizenship. The court, however, held that the bank account was exempt from tax pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. § 13303.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision and held that the statute which governed inheritance tax exemptions for nonresidents of the United States and residents living outside of California did not violate equal protection laws. The court, however, held that the inheritance of a bank account was exempt from tax by statute. The court remanded the case with directions to calculate the inheritance tax in accordance to its ruling.

 

1782 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Ghent v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 44837


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


90 Cal. App. 3d 944; 153 Cal. Rptr. 720; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1540



March 23, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On April 19, 1979, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Petitioner's Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 17, 1979.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ of prohibition is discharged. The petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner criminal defendant challenged the order of respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied petitioner's Cal. Penal Code § 995 motion insofar as it sought to have the allegations of special circumstances stricken from several of the counts against him and to have certain counts dismissed, and denied his motion to sever the counts for trial.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner criminal defendant was charged with murder and rape. The people alleged special circumstances, seeking to impose the death penalty for the murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2. Petitioner brought a motion under Cal. Penal Code § 995, seeking to have the allegations of special circumstances stricken, and to have the counts of rape dismissed, upon the ground that they were unsupported by the evidence received at the preliminary examination. He requested an order severing the counts for separate trials. These motions were refused. The court held that petitioner's § 995 motion was not time-barred because it was brought within the no opportunity exception provided in Cal. Penal Code § 1510, but the evidence received by the magistrate showed reasonable cause for the allegations of special circumstances and a rational ground for the assumption that a rape was committed and petitioner was guilty of it. The court held petitioner was not entitled to relief from the orders denying his § 995 motions. The court found no abuse of discretion in the lower court's refusal to sever the three counts for trial, because the offenses were connected together in their commission.


OUTCOME: The court discharged petitioner criminal defendant's alternative writ of prohibition because there was reasonable cause for the allegations of special circumstances, and a rational ground for the assumption that a rape was committed and petitioner was guilty of it. The court also held that respondent court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to sever counts for trial.

 

1783 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Los Angeles v. Waller


Civ. Nos. 51526, 51527


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


90 Cal. App. 3d 766; 154 Cal. Rptr. 12; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1524



March 21, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 10, 1979, and the Petition of the Defendants, Cross-Complainants and Appellants for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 14, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 37925, C 107445, August J. Goebel and Lester E. Olson, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and orders are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landowners appealed from the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment for plaintiff city in its condemnation action. Defendants also appealed from postjudgment orders, which denied defendants' motions for a new trial, for costs and disbursements, for litigation costs, and expenses. Defendants also challenged an award of attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city brought an action for condemnation against defendant landowners, who brought an action for inverse condemnation against plaintiff for the same parcel of investment property. The trial court entered a judgment in condemnation for plaintiff and denied defendants' postjudgment motions for a new trial, for costs and disbursements, for litigation costs, and for expenses. At trial, appraisers for both parties testified to differing valuations. On appeal, the court found that there was nothing in the record to show that plaintiff's appraiser's testimony was not credible. The court also found that that the lower court properly took judicial notice of the appraisal as a genuine document. The court held that nothing about the appraisal entitled defendants to a new trial and that the appraisal properly took into account project enhancement. Because there was no evidence of physical invasion or direct legal restraint of defendants' property, the court found there was no de facto taking. The court found that delay damages were not proper because the property was held for investment only. Finally, the court found that the attorney fees awarded were not an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the condemnation judgment for plaintiff city and the denial of defendant landowners' post-trial motions because differing expert appraisals did not entitle defendants to a new trial and the trial court properly took judicial notice of the appraisal. The court also found that no de facto taking had occurred and that defendants could not recover delay damages against plaintiff because the property was for investment purposes.

 

1784 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sage


Crim. No. 10173.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


90 Cal. App. 3d 722; 153 Cal. Rptr. 533; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1520



March 20, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 30, 1979 (See 26 C.3d 498)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. CR 33961, William P. Hyde, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) which failed to give him good time and work time credits pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 2900.5, 4019 where he had been committed for treatment as a mentally disordered sex offender for approximately one year prior to sentencing.


OVERVIEW: A criminal defendant, who had been committed for treatment as a mentally disordered sex offender prior to sentencing, was denied credit for good time and work time pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 2900.5, 4019. On appeal, the court remanded the case to the trial court for a determination of defendant's credits for good time and work time during the approximately one year he spent committed for treatment as a mentally disordered sex offender prior to sentencing. The court held that Cal. Penal Code §§ 2900.5, 4019 provided that credit for good time and work time may be earned by a criminal defendant during periods of presentence confinement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court but remanded the case with directions to determine good time and work time credits which criminal defendant may have been entitled to as a result of his approximate one year confinement in a mental hospital for treatment prior to sentencing as a mentally disordered sex offender.

 

1785 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Post v. Prati


Civ. No. 53088


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


90 Cal. App. 3d 626; 153 Cal. Rptr. 511; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1510; 62 Oil & Gas Rep. 153



March 19, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 17, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 185127, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff prospective lessor challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ruled in favor of defendant surface owners, sustaining their demurrers without leave to amend, and upholding the constitutionality of Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 6922.


OVERVIEW: When defendant surface owners were allowed to make a preemptive bid with respect to a geothermal lease under Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 6922, plaintiff prospective lessor was denied the lease and challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 6922. The lower court ruled in favor of defendants and upheld the statute's constitutionality. Plaintiff appealed. Plaintiff contended that the preference extended to defendants under Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 6922 violated the gift prohibition contained in Cal. Const. art. XVI, § 6, and that it violated plaintiff's equal protection rights. The court affirmed the ruling of the lower court upholding the constitutionality of Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 6922, and held that the right of first refusal of a geothermal lease was not a gift in the constitutional sense, as it served a legitimate public purpose by likely promoting economic growth. The court also held that Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 6922 did not violate plaintiff's equal protection rights, as there was an important state interest in giving preference to defendants, and that the classification under Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 6922 significantly furthered the important state interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court and held that the preference extended to defendant surface owners under the Public Resources Code was constitutional because the preference was a legitimate state concern that served a justifiable public goal, and did not violate plaintiff prospective lessor's equal protection rights because the preference was rationally related to a legitimate state interest.

 

1786 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Rhodes v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 45041


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


90 Cal. App. 3d 484; 153 Cal. Rptr. 495; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1499



March 15, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of the Real Parties in Interest for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 10, 1979.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to vacate its order denying petitioner's request for a bond and to reconsider the motion in a manner consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mayor sought a writ of mandate to respondent Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which had denied petitioner's motion that plaintiff citizens be required to post bonds under Cal. Gov't Code § 91012 in a suit arising under the Political Reform Act of 1974.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner mayor was sued under the Political Reform Act of 1974 for failure to comply with the filing of statements of economic interest. Petitioner filed a motion under Cal. Gov't Code § 91012, to require that the underlying plaintiffs post a bond to assure payment of costs in the litigation. The trial court denied the motion and petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel the trial court to set an appropriate bond. The court issued the writ, directing the trial court to vacate its earlier order and to reconsider the motion. The court held that § 91012 was not unconstitutional. The court found that unlike other statutes that required the posting of bond, § 91012 was construed to require that, before ordering a bond, a trial court was to hold an evidentiary hearing to determine a reasonable amount for the bond and also to determine the likelihood of success on the merits of the case. He court held that those requirements provided due process protection.


OUTCOME: The court issued a preemptory writ of mandate and directed the trial court to vacate its order denying petitioner mayor's bond request and to reconsider the motion because the statute requiring a bond was not unconstitutional as it also required a hearing to determine the likelihood of success of the lawsuit and to set the bond at an appropriate amount.

 

1787 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Peles v. La Bounty


Civ. No. 53607


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


90 Cal. App. 3d 431; 153 Cal. Rptr. 571; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1494



March 14, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 10, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. EAC 25501, Roberta Ralph, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff graduate student appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate to reinstate plaintiff after expulsion from university filed against defendants, current president and admissions officer, after plaintiff was expelled for plagiarism.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff graduate student was terminated from the university for plagiarism. The charge of plagiarism was found true at a university hearing and the president of the university approved the recommendation of permanent expulsion. Plaintiff filed a petition for writ of mandate to reinstate student after expulsion from university against defendants, current president and admissions officer, more than four years later. The lower court denied plaintiff's writ because the statute of limitations had run pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 338. Plaintiff contended that a separate cause of action accrued on the refusal to reinstate that was made several years after plaintiff's expulsion. The court found that the refusal to reinstate did not give rise to a separate cause of action because no intervening act gave plaintiff a right that he did not have previously and the statute of limitations could not be extended by plaintiff postponing his demand for re-entry. The court also found that plaintiff had no right to higher education where he had abused it and that Rule 11 of the Student Disciplinary Procedures did not limit the period of plaintiff's suspension.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that denied plaintiff graduate student's petition for writ of mandate to reinstate student after expulsion from university against defendants, current president and admissions officer, because plaintiff did not file the petition within the three year statute of limitations.

 

1788 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Monroe v. Monroe


Civ. No. 16898


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


90 Cal. App. 3d 388; 153 Cal. Rptr. 384; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1489



March 13, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 390268, Alfred Lord, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted defendant husband's motion for summary judgment in an action for damages for personal injuries she sustained in a car accident. The trial court found no triable issue of fact and held that plaintiff was precluded, as a matter of law, from the recovery of personal injury damages from defendant when the charge was simple negligence.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff wife brought an action for damages for personal injuries she sustained in a car accident against her defendant husband. Plaintiff was a passenger, riding in a car that was driven by defendant, the husband and the co-owner of the car, when it collided with another car. She charged defendant with negligently causing her injuries. The lower court granted an order of summary judgment in favor of defendant. Plaintiff appealed. The court found that the section of the state code that prevented plaintiff from filing suit against defendant violated state and federal equal protection standards. The provision at issue was enacted to treat owner-passengers as guests under the guest statute. However, because car passengers were no longer denied recovery for injuries suffered because of a negligent driver, the classification defeated the purpose of according owner-passengers the same protections as guests because it singled out owner-passengers for differential treatment from all other car accident victims. It was permissible for a spouse to sue a spouse for an intentional or negligent personal tort. The decision of the lower court was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant. The state statute in question violated state and federal equal protection standards by singling out injured passengers who happened to own the car in which they were injured. Further, the marital state of the parties formed no rational basis for denial of equal protection of the law.

 

1789 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Rietz v. California Camino Bank


Civ. No. 43829.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


90 Cal. App. 3d 220; 154 Cal. Rptr. 3; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1471



Mar. 12, 1979.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Hg. den. May 10, 1979, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14  [**1] Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 387338, Marshall S. Hall, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, general partner and others, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), denying appellant's permanent injunction and directing that respondent banks comply with subpoenas duces tecum issued under Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 19254, requesting production of certain bank records. Respondent tax board was joined as a real party in interest.


OVERVIEW: An investigator for respondent tax board issued two administrative subpoenas duces tecum to respondent banks pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 19254, for the financial records of appellant, general partner and others, so as to determine whether the partnerships had complied with the Personal Income Tax Law. Appellants request for a permanent injunction was denied. On appeal, the court reversed and directed the trial court to dismiss the action as moot. Although the act appellants sought to enjoin had been completed, the court adjudicated the issues involved as being in the public interest and to prevent future litigation of the same questions. The court found that a 1974 amendment to Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, extended the right of privacy to a person's confidential financial affairs. The court held that when fundamental liberties were at stake, the challenged law must be shown to be necessary, and not merely rationally related to, the accomplishment of a permissible state policy. In applying strict scrutiny, the court found Cal. Gov't Code § 7474 overbroad and therefore, an unconstitutional invasion of a citizen's right to privacy in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, §1.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying the permanent injunction requested by appellants, general partner and others, with directions to the trial court to dismiss the action for being moot prior to its final determination on appeal. In adjudicating the issues as being in the public interest, the court found the statute authorizing issuance of subpoenas duces tecum was overbroad and unconstitutional.

Durham v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 54072


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


91 Cal. App. 3d 567; 154 Cal. Rptr. 243; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1600



March 9, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 29, 1979, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 30, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 58772, David N. Eagleson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment for Southern Pacific Transportation Company and the judgment of nonsuit for the city are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants minor and his mother challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sutter County (California), which entered a judgement of nonsuit for respondent city and, after a jury trial, a judgment for respondent railroad company in appellants' suit to recover for injuries suffered by appellant minor when he tried to hop a railroad train near his home.


OVERVIEW: Appellants minor and his mother brought suit against respondent city and railroad company after appellant minor was injured while trying to hop on a moving train near his home. The trial court entered a judgment of nonsuit for respondent city and, following a jury trial, a judgment for respondent railroad company. Appellants sought review of the judgments on the grounds that the grant of nonsuit was erroneous and that Cal Civ. Code § 1714.7, which established the liability of an owner or operator of a railroad to unauthorized riders, was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the trial court's decisions and held that the nonsuit was proper because the city's property was not defective and could not be considered dangerous except for its proximity to the railroad tracks. Further, the court held that § 1714.7 was constitutional because it was not a special statute and did not violate the equal protection or privileges and immunities clauses. Applying the same test for each of the constitutional challenges, the court held that the purpose of § 1714.7 and its distinction between those who have authority to use the railway and those who do not were reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of nonsuit to respondent city and judgment for respondent railroad company because the respondent city's property was in no way defective and the statute establishing the liability of an owner or operator of a railroad to unauthorized rider of a railway, such as appellant minor, was constitutional. The court held that both the classification in and purpose of the statute was reasonable.

 

1791 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Barr v. United Methodist Church


Civ. No. 18244


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


90 Cal. App. 3d 259; 153 Cal. Rptr. 322; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1475



March 8, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of the Defendant and Respondent for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 17, 1979. Richardson, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 404611, Ross G. Tharp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant retirement home residents sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted respondent religious organization's motion to quash service of summons in appellants' class action suit seeking damages or specific performance of continuing care agreements between appellants and the retirement homes which were alleged to be respondent's agents.


OVERVIEW: Appellant retirement home residents brought a class action suit against respondent religious organization, as a principal of the retirement homes, seeking damages or specific performance of continuing care agreements between appellants and the retirement homes. The trial court granted respondent's motion to quash the service of summons, finding that respondent was not a jural entity or unincorporated entity subject to suit under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 388. Appellants sought review. The court reversed and found, after reviewing respondent's hierarchical structure, day to day operations, and its relationship with the retirement homes, that respondent was a religious organization and unassociated entity under § 388, and that due process of law did not preclude it from being sued. Furthermore, the court found that neither U.S. Const. amend. I or Cal. Const. art. I, §4 prevented a court from concluding respondent could be sued, even though that conclusion was contrary to church doctrine, because finding that respondent was capable of being sued was not equivalent to a review of its polity thus interfering with its internal affairs in violation of the free exercise clause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision, granting respondent religious organization's motion to quash the service of summons, and found that respondent was capable of being sued as an unincorporated association as it did not violate due process of law or interfere with the internal affairs of respondent in violation of the free exercise clause.

 

1792 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


O'Connor v. Superior Court


Civ. Nos. 44865, 44907


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


90 Cal. App. 3d 107; 153 Cal. Rptr. 306; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1456



March 6, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a Rehearing were Denied March 23, 1979, and Petitioners' Applications for a Hearing by the Supreme Court were Denied May 3, 1979. Bird, C. J., did not Participate therein.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writs of mandate are discharged and the petitions are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxi permit holders sought writs of mandate to compel respondent, the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), to issue preliminary injunctions to prevent enforcement of a proposition passed by the voters that mandated the issuance of new, non-transferable taxi permits.


OVERVIEW: City and County of San Francisco, Cal. Prop. K required petitioner taxi permit holders to obtain new permits that would no longer be transferable as they had been under prior ordinances. Petitioners sought a preliminary injunction to prevent enforcement of Prop. K, claiming that the proposition was not properly submitted to the electorate and that the proposition was unconstitutional. The court discharged the writs of mandate and denied the claims. The court held that pursuant to City of San Francisco, Cal. Charter § 9.08, a proposition could be placed before the voters in a general election on a submission by one-third of the municipal supervisors. Prop. K, submitted by one-third of the supervisors, was on the ballot during a direct primary. The court held that a direct primary was a regular election under Cal. Elec. Code § 26 and that a regular election fell within the requirements of § 9.08. The court further held that petitioners had no vested property rights in the taxi permits. The permits were a privilege that could be regulated under local police powers. Without a vested property right, there was no constitutional violation.


OUTCOME: The alternative writs of mandate were discharged and the petitions were denied. The proposition was properly submitted to the voters and enacted by their vote. The proposition was not unconstitutional because taxicab use of the streets was a privilege and granting a permit did not create a constitutionally vested property right that the proposition interfered with.

 

1793 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Fair Political Practices Com. v. Suitt


Civ. Nos. 17707, 17708


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


90 Cal. App. 3d 125; 153 Cal. Rptr. 311; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1458



March 6, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 3, 1979, and the Judgment was Modified to Read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 270419, 270251, Joseph A. DeCristoforo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments in both cases are reversed, except only for the portion of the Suitt v. FPPC judgment which awards attorney fees (no appeal having been taken therefrom). Appellant shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Fair Political Practices Commission sought review of two judgments from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted relief to respondents, assemblyman and his political committee, in actions involving campaign disclosure statements pursuant to the Political Reform Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 81000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Fair Political Practices Commission brought an action against respondents, assemblyman and his campaign committee, seeking to compel respondents to file amended campaign statements under the Political Reform Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 81000 et seq. (Act), disclosing nonmonetary contribution for campaign work of an employee of the State of California. In anticipation of the suit, respondents filed an action against appellant. The court reversed the ruling for respondents, holding that the term "person" under Cal. Gov't Code § 82047 included public entities because general case law held that public entities were included among "persons" in statutory language where inclusion would not infringe upon sovereign governmental powers. Inclusion would not infringe upon governmental powers because the legislature only needed to exercise due care in separating campaigning and legislative activities, and the activities for the campaign, soliciting contributions, planning strategy, coordinating volunteer work, and preparing the budget, were unambiguously political. The legislature intended all contributions to be disclosed because the Act's purpose was to inhibit improper practices.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgments for respondents, assemblyman and his campaign committee, holding that the term "person" in the disclosure statute included public entities because the legislature intended that all contributions be disclosed, and inclusion would not infringe upon sovereign governmental powers because the activities were unambiguously political.

 

1794 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Biehler


Crim. No. 30781.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


90 Cal. App. 3d 52; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1452



March 5, 1979.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Mar. 30, 1979, opn. ordered by Ct. of App., 2d Dist. Div. 1, not to be published in the Official Reports.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A134587, Harry V. Peetris, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of four offenses of first degree murder and one of attempted murder of the first degree. Defendant challenged his enhanced sentence and a ruling regarding his right to cross-examine pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. VI and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of four offenses of first degree murder and one of attempted murder of the first degree, denied his motion for a new trial, and sentenced him to concurrent terms of life imprisonment. Defendant sought review of the judgment and noticed an appeal from the denial of his motion. The court dismissed the appeal from the order, modified the sentencing enhancements, and affirmed the judgment as modified. The court found that there was nothing tainted about the identifications by the witnesses of defendant at the courthouse. The court found that the witnesses' testimony was properly admitted. The court did not find any abuse of discretion by the trial court and was convinced of the integrity of the verdicts by the trial court. The court found that state produced compelling evidence of defendant's guilt. The court held that defendant received a fair trial and that his right to cross-examine witnesses, pursuant to the U.S. Const. amend. VI and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, was not violated.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal regarding defendant's motion for a new trial. The court modified the enhanced sentence and affirmed the judgment as modified, holding that defendant received a fair trial, that his right to cross-examine witnesses was not violated, and that the state produced compelling evidence against him.

 

1795 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Perrone C.


Civ. No. 18281.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


90 Cal. App. 3d 97; 153 Cal. Rptr. 275; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1455



March 5, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 2, 1979 (see 26 C.3d 49)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 95221, Artemis G. Henderson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his application for rehearing from a decision by a juvenile court referee, who found that appellant violated Cal. Penal Code § 236, false imprisonment. Appellant argued that he was denied due process, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7a, 15.


OVERVIEW: After a juvenile court referee made a finding that appellant juvenile came within Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 by violating Cal. Penal Code § 236, appellant juvenile requested a rehearing, which was denied by a juvenile court judge. Appellant sought review, contending that he was denied due process under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7a, 15 by being placed in jeopardy before a referee who could lawfully decide against him, but not in his favor, Cal. Const. art. VI, § 22. Appellant argued that a juvenile court could not hold a jurisdictional hearing under California's statutory procedure, absent a stipulation between the parties. Agreeing with appellant's contentions, the court reversed the lower court's order and remanded. The court found that a referee's acquittal could not be reheard because to do so would constitute double jeopardy. The court reasoned that the referee's findings would, therefore, be a final determination, which was constitutionally proscribed. Therefore, appellant was subject to a procedure where the trier of fact could only find against him, a violation of constitutional standards.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order declaring appellant juvenile a ward of the court and committing him to the California Youth Authority, and remanded to juvenile court for further proceedings. The court found that appellant was denied constitutional due process by being placed in jeopardy before a referee who could lawfully decide against him, but not in his favor.

 

1796 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Shirokow


Civ. No. 3960.


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


90 Cal. App. 3d 1; 153 Cal. Rptr. 141; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1447



Mar. 1, 1979 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 17, 1979 (See 26 C.3d 301)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Madera County, No. 20934, Charles W. Jennings, Judge. * 


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State of California sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Madera County (California), which denied appellant's complaint for injunctive relief against respondent landowner for respondent's use of a dam to make a reservoir on his property without a permit from the State Water Resource Control Board.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landowner constructed a dam across a river on his property, creating a reservoir and limiting the amount of water continuing on to down water property owners. Appellant State of California sought to enjoin respondent's use of the reservoir because respondent did not obtain a permit to build the dam and collect the water. The injunction was denied. The court affirmed the lower court's order. The court held that the permit requirements in the 1913 Water Commission Act, Cal. Water Code § 1200 et seq., applied only to waters taken by appropriation. Respondent took water based on prescription. The court found that respondent had perfected his title to the reservoir because he had used the reservoir continuously for more than five years, and the diversion of the water to the reservoir was open, notorious, under a claim of right, and hostile to downstream owners.


OUTCOME: The order denying injunctive relief was affirmed. Respondent landowner had perfected a prescriptive title to the use of water and pursuant to the 1913 Water Commission Act, prescriptive takings did not require permits from the State Water Resource Control Board.

 

1797 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Chrysler Corp. v. New Motor Vehicle Bd.


Civ. No. 15893


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


89 Cal. App. 3d 1034; 153 Cal. Rptr. 135; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1446



March 1, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 29, 1979, and the Petitions of Both the Parties for a Hearing by the Supreme Court were Denied April 26, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 258933, Frances N. Carr, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The case is remanded to the trial court with instructions to vacate the order granting the preliminary injunction. But inasmuch as the proposed dealership has been in actual operation since the spring of 1976, and will so continue if the Board ultimately finds no cause to reject it, equity requires that it be permitted to remain in operation until the Board renders a final decision. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, New Motor Vehicle Board (California), sought review of an order which granted a preliminary injunction to appellee company preventing appellant from ordering that appellee could not create a new motor vehicle franchise because it violated Cal. Veh. Code § 3062.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, New Motor Vehicle Board, sought review of an order which granted a preliminary injunction to appellee company preventing appellant from halting the creation of a motor vehicle franchise because it violated Cal. Veh. Code § 3062. The court held that the preliminary injunction issued by the trial court was vacated because the lower court erred in finding that appellant violated constitutional grounds in preventing the franchise application to proceed until a hearing was held due to local franchise owner's protests filed with appellant. The court held that neither U.S. Const. art. I, § 8 or Cal. Const. art. I, §7(a) were violated. The court remanded the case to the trial court ordering it to vacate the preliminary injunction but, because the franchise was already in operation, it could continue to operate until the appellant rendered a final decision.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the preliminary injunction granted to appellee company because the trial court erred in determining that appellant, New Motor Vehicle Board, violated the commerce clause of the United States constitution or the rights of an individual to pursue a business under the state constitution. However, the franchise could continue operation until a final decision of appellant because it already was in operation.

 

1798 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Municipal Court (Street)


Civ. No. 44563


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


89 Cal. App. 3d 739; 153 Cal. Rptr. 69; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1420



February 26, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 27, 1979, and the Petition of the Plaintiff and Respondent for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 26, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 150945, 144221, 153639 and 153638, Walter F. Calcagno, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The superior court's order granting a writ of mandate is vacated. We direct that the order of the municipal court granting discovery be reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prostitutes sought review of an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted a writ of mandate and denied appellants discovery of information relating to their claims for intentional and purposeful discriminatory enforcement of prostitution laws under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant prostitutes, after being charged with violating various penal code sections, filed a pretrial motion for dismissal and discovery, alleging that a trial or conviction on the charges would violate their rights to equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a), because the charges were the result of intentional and purposeful discriminatory enforcement of the law. The court vacated the lower court's order granting a writ of mandate, which denied appellants discovery, and directed that the order granting appellants' discovery request be reinstated because appellants made a clear allegation of a purposeful policy of discriminatory enforcement of the law, citing specific occurrences to support their claim, and their discovery requests were specifically aimed at obtaining information to support those allegations.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the lower court's order granting the writ of mandate and commanded that an order granting discovery be reinstated because appellant prostitutes specifically aimed their discovery requests at obtaining information to support clear allegations of discriminatory enforcement of the law.

 

1799 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Winter v. Gnaizda


Civ. No. 43558


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


90 Cal. App. 3d 750; 152 Cal. Rptr. 700; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1522



February 23, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 19, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 444051, Leonard J. Dieden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to dismiss the action as to the defendant Robert Gnaizda, in his capacity as Director of the California State Department of Health.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the director of the California state department of health, appealed from a judgment rendered in Superior Court of Alameda County (California) in an action for declaratory relief, where the court allowed plaintiff physician the difference between his full fee and the lesser amount paid under the California medical assistance program.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff physician initiated an action for declaratory relief against defendant trustee and defendant, the director of the California state department of health (DOH), because defendant trustee refused to pay plaintiff the difference between the amount plaintiff received for medical care rendered under the California medical assistance program (Medi-Cal) and the amount he would have received for medical care administered to a private patient, on advice from Medi-Cal's fiscal intermediary. The court held that defendant DOH's interest was not injuriously affected by the judgment, he was not a "party aggrieved" according to Cal. Civ. Proc. § 902, and thus had no legal right to pursue an appeal. Furthermore, defendant DOH was not a proper party to the declaratory relief action, as there was no justiciable controversy between defendant DOH and plaintiff. Without a controversy, the court would be required to render an advisory opinion, which was explicitly forbidden by law in an action brought for declaratory relief. The court remanded the matter to the trial court with directions to dismiss the action as to the defendant DOH.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the matter to the trial court with directions to dismiss the action as to defendant, the director of the California state department of health. Defendant was neither a proper party to the declaratory relief action, as no controversy existed between defendant and plaintiff physician, nor did he have a legal right to pursue an appeal, because he was not injuriously affected by the judgment.

 

1800 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Atkins v. Kessler


Civ. No. 53937.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


90 Cal. App. 3d 614; 153 Cal. Rptr. 281; 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1509



February 22, 1979 Hg. granted April 19, 1979, and cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 2d Dist., Div. 2, for reconsideration in light of Kaufman v. Gross & Co. (1979) 23 C.3d 750.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 146197, Joseph A. Wapner, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant purchaser sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent defaulting property owners, quieting title and declaring parts of the Improvement Act of 1911, Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 6400 et seq., unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The city foreclosed on and sold real estate, owned by respondent defaulting property owners, to appellant purchaser, because respondents failed to pay their assessed share of an improvement bond relating to street improvements. In an action to quiet title to the real estate in question, the trial court granted summary judgment to respondents, declaring parts of the Improvement Act of 1911 (act), Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 6400 et seq., unconstitutional, because it failed to give adequate notice to landowners of the risk of foreclosure. Appellant challenged the trial court's order. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of respondents, finding the provisions of the act, as it was at the time of the order, were not reasonably calculated to inform the property owner of the sale proceedings and of his right of redemption, which deprived him of his constitutional right to due process. However, the court did not hold that all the provisions of the act were unconstitutional, as the key notification provisions had been amended, since the judgment was originally rendered, to give adequate notification to property owners.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, quieting title in favor of respondent defaulting property owners over appellant purchaser, holding that sections of the Improvement Act of 1911 were insufficient to give fair notice, as required by constitutional due process. However, the court declined to hold all the applicable provisions the act unconstitutional, because later amendments provided property owners the requisite due process.
